# Good Room

# Chapter 1

Doug Bridge woke at 11:40 on a Sunday morning in February still wearing his suit pants and one dress shoe. The left one. His right foot was bare and cold where it hung off the edge of the mattress, and the sheets were bunched at the bottom like someone had fought them and lost.

The apartment was cold. He'd left the bedroom window cracked — he could hear the latch ticking against the frame in the draft — and the baseboard heater was doing what it always did, which was run at full volume and accomplish almost nothing. The ceiling above him was the same off-white it had been for twelve years. A water stain near the light fixture that he'd been meaning to assess had spread another inch since the fall, creeping toward the smoke detector with the patience of something that knew it had time. He noted this the way he noted all damage — automatically, with an estimate forming before the observation was even finished. Moderate. Cosmetic. No structural concern unless the source was active.

His head was not a headache. It was a condition. A full-body disagreement with being awake that started behind his eyes and traveled down through his neck and into his stomach, where something was sitting that he did not intend to investigate. His mouth tasted like pennies and carpet. The suit jacket was on the bathroom floor — he could see it through the open door, crumpled against the base of the toilet like it had been aiming for the hamper and lost interest halfway there.

He sat up. The room tilted, then caught itself.

His phone was not on the nightstand. It was not on the floor beside the bed. It was not in his pants pocket, which he checked by patting the fabric with the focused attention of someone looking for a misplaced document. He found it in the bathroom sink, face-down against the drain stopper, resting there as if someone had placed it with a care that the rest of the evening apparently hadn't earned. The screen was still on. He picked it up.

Forty-seven missed calls. Two hundred and fourteen text messages. Notifications from apps he hadn't opened in months — Instagram, which he'd downloaded for Kelsey's engagement photos and never deleted, and Twitter, which he didn't use but which was now informing him he'd been mentioned six hundred and eleven times. His email had thirty-one new messages. The most recent was from someone at a company called Meridian Talent Group. The subject line said: "RE: Representation Inquiry — URGENT."

Doug set the phone on the edge of the sink and looked at himself in the mirror. His tie was gone. His shirt was buttoned wrong — one off from the second buttonhole down, so the collar sat at an angle that made him look like he'd been dressed by someone working from incorrect instructions. His hair, which had been thinning at the crown since roughly 2019 in a way that didn't qualify as bald but had stopped qualifying as anything else either, was pressed flat on one side. His eyes were red and reduced, and the skin beneath them had a texture he associated with water-damaged drywall — puffy, discolored, unlikely to improve without intervention.

He turned on the faucet and held his hands under cold water until his fingers ached. Then he unbuttoned the shirt, pulled off the suit pants and the one shoe, and stood in his boxers in the bathroom while the water ran and the latch ticked at the window and the heater groaned in the wall.

Something had happened at the reception.

He had fragments. He remembered the ceremony — Kelsey in the dress, the officiant who kept saying "sacred bond" in a tone that suggested a woman reading a lease she hadn't reviewed, Tyler standing at the altar with the pleasant composure of someone who'd been told where to stand and was executing the assignment well. He remembered the cocktail hour. He remembered the first whiskey, and the second, and the one after that. He remembered Brendan's toast. Brendan, standing at the head table in a suit that fit him like suits fit people who buy them at places with mirrors and opinions, holding his glass, talking about Kelsey with the kind of composed emotional authority that made everyone in the room feel something specific and made Doug feel specifically that he was being outperformed by a man who had known his daughter for eight years and was speaking about her as though he'd invented tenderness. Brendan had cried. At exactly the right moment. One tear, left eye, wiped with his thumb, and the room had sighed like a single organism, and Doug had signaled the bartender for his fifth whiskey. Or his sixth.

After that, the evening became a file with water damage — legible in patches, ruined in the middle, the critical section exactly where the stain was worst. He remembered standing up. He remembered a microphone, the weight of it in his hand, heavier than expected, like a flashlight pointed inward. He remembered faces. He did not remember what he'd said to them.

He picked up his phone and scrolled through the texts.

Roy Utley, 11:47 p.m.: *You alright?*

Roy, 11:48 p.m.: *Call me when you're up.*

Megan, 12:03 a.m.: *Doug, we should talk about last night. I'm not angry. Just want to discuss when you're ready.*

Megan, 12:04 a.m.: *Brendan wanted me to tell you he thought it was extraordinary. His word, not mine.*

Janet Morse, his supervisor, 8:15 a.m.: *Hi Doug, hope you're doing well. Quick question re: Hoffmeyer claim when you get a chance. Also saw the video. We should chat.*

Doug stopped. He read Janet's text again. *Saw the video.*

He found Kelsey's thread. Three messages, sent at intervals that suggested each one had been composed, deleted, recomposed.

Kelsey, 11:52 p.m.: *Dad.*

Kelsey, 11:54 p.m.: *Call me tomorrow.*

Kelsey, 12:30 a.m.: *I'm not mad. I just need you to call me.*

Doug sat on the edge of the bathtub. The porcelain was cold through his boxers and he could feel the grout line pressing into the back of his thigh. He typed a message to Kelsey. Two words, because he did not yet know what the correct number was.

*How bad.*

No question mark. It wasn't a question. The three dots appeared immediately — she'd been waiting, or she was already holding her phone, or she'd been holding it since midnight.

*Do not go on the internet.*

Doug looked at his phone. He looked at the bathroom — the suit jacket on the tile, the one shoe beside the door, the water stain on the ceiling that was now directly above him like a thought he was underneath. He looked at the 214 unread texts and the 47 missed calls and the notification from an app he'd never used telling him that 611 people had opinions about him.

He went on the internet.

---

The video was the third result when he typed his own name into Google, which was not a sentence that had been true about Doug Bridge at any prior point in his fifty-two years. The first result was a Reddit thread titled "This man just eulogized his own marriage at his daughter's wedding and I can't stop watching," with 18,000 upvotes. The second was a tweet with the words "THIS MAN" in capitals and a nine-second clip he was not yet ready to watch. The third was the full video, seven minutes and fourteen seconds, uploaded to YouTube by an account called KelseyAndTylerWedding, which meant Tyler's cousin Marcus — who had been asked to record the toasts — had posted it without asking anyone, and Doug filed this piece of information away for later processing.

Two million views. The video had been online for fourteen hours and two million people had watched it. Doug tried to conceptualize two million. It was more than the population of Columbus. It was roughly the combined enrollment of every insurance adjusting certification program in North America, though he wasn't sure why that was the comparison his brain offered, except that it was the kind of fact he had.

He pressed play.

The video opened on the Marriott ballroom in Worthington — the warm lighting, the white tablecloths, the centerpieces Kelsey and Megan had spent four months negotiating. The camera was tilted slightly to the left. Marcus had been drinking too.

Doug watched himself stand up.

The man in the video was wearing the same suit that was now crumpled on the bathroom floor, except in the video the buttons were still aligned and the tie was still on. His face was the red of someone who has had eight whiskeys on a stomach that contained two crab cakes and a bread roll. He was not staggering — he had the careful steadiness of a man who knew he was drunk and was compensating for it by moving as though the floor were a surface he did not entirely trust. His eyes were bright and loose and he was smiling in a way that Doug had never seen on his own face, the kind of smile that happens when the mechanism connecting intention to expression has been disconnected.

He took the microphone from the stand. "So," he said.

His voice was louder than it should have been, and a woman off-camera laughed — not at anything yet, just at the single syllable landing in the room with more weight than one syllable was supposed to carry.

"Brendan just gave a hell of a toast," video-Doug said. "I think — yeah, that was something. The part about the sunset, Brendan, was that the — you cried. I saw that. Left eye first and then the right one caught up. Perfect timing. Like a movie." He paused. Took a breath that was audible on the recording. "I haven't cried since — I think 2012. Maybe 2011. Somewhere in there. I was on I-70 and the heater was going and I don't remember what started it but I couldn't stop for about six exits, and the guy in the next lane looked at me at a red light and I could see him trying to decide whether to do something, and he didn't, and I don't blame him." The room laughed, but the laugh was uncertain, a group of people who hadn't decided yet what kind of event they were attending. "That's not a joke," video-Doug said. "That's a traffic safety issue."

Doug, sitting on the edge of his bathtub in February, watched himself and did not recognize the man holding the microphone. That Doug was something the current Doug had never been — unguarded in a way that looked, on camera, almost like ease.

"So I'm Kelsey's father," the video continued. "For the Tyler side of the room — I'm the other one. Not the one who just made everybody cry. The other one." A harder laugh this time, from more of the room. "I adjust insurance claims. For a living. I've been doing it for twenty years. If your water heater explodes, I come look at the damage. If a tree falls on your garage, I assess the loss. That's my whole thing. That's the pitch." He spread his free hand in a gesture that seemed to take in himself, the room, the entirety of what he'd done with the years since Kelsey was born. "I want you to know I'm aware that Brendan is an orthopedic surgeon. I'm aware of that. I did the math a while back and he makes more in a month than I make in — but we're not here for that. We're here for Kelsey."

He didn't talk about Kelsey. Not yet. He reached across the table and picked up someone's wine glass and drank from it, and the person whose wine it was — an older woman, one of Megan's aunts maybe — reached for it and then pulled her hand back.

"Megan and I were married for eighteen years," video-Doug said. The room shifted. Doug could hear it on the recording — the sound of 180 people adjusting in their chairs. "Which — in insurance terms, the depreciation on eighteen years is significant. After that long, the actual cash value of a marriage is basically what you can get for the furniture, minus what you spent fighting about the furniture. And I'll tell you right now, Megan came out ahead on that calculation." He paused. "She comes out ahead on most calculations. Megan's a smart woman. She made a good decision." The pause this time stretched longer, and the room went quiet in a way that felt different from before — not amused-quiet but held-breath-quiet. "I mean that. I'm not — that's not bitterness, that's an accurate assessment. She looked at the situation, she evaluated her options, and she made the right call. That's not a joke either."

He took another drink of the wine that wasn't his. "And Brendan's a good man. He is. I know people think I should hate him and I gave it a real shot for about eighteen months and I couldn't pull it off because the son of a bitch is genuinely decent, and let me tell you, that's worse." His voice rose on the last word, and several people laughed, including someone who sounded like Brendan himself. "That's so much worse than if he were an asshole. If he were an asshole I'd have something. I'd have a position. But he's nice, and he's good to Kelsey, and he cooks, and he gave that toast, and what am I supposed to do with that? What's the coverage for that? There's no line item."

Doug, on the bathtub, heard himself say "son of a bitch" and "asshole" in a room that contained Megan's parents, Tyler's mother, Kelsey's coworkers from the dental practice, and a woman named Diane from Megan's book club who had once told Doug he seemed "very capable" in a tone that clearly meant something else entirely. The sound that came out of Doug's mouth in the bathroom was quiet and involuntary and was the word "shit," spoken to no one.

The video kept going.

"But here's the thing," video-Doug said. He was pointing now — at nothing, at the room, at something only he could see. "Here's — Kelsey, can you hear me? Are you — okay." His eyes found a point off-camera and held there. His voice dropped, not to a whisper but to something closer to the register people use when they've forgotten they're being listened to.

"The kitchen table. In the house on Briarwood. You used to sit at one end doing your math homework, and I'd sit at the other end filling out proof-of-loss forms. You had that TI-84 calculator that you'd punch so hard I thought you were going to break it, and I had my files, and we'd just — we'd sit there. For two hours. Three hours. We didn't talk. You'd look up every now and then and ask me how to spell 'necessary' or 'February' and I got 'February' wrong every time, and you'd laugh at me." He stopped. The room had gone silent in a way that was different from every other silence in the video — not uncomfortable, not amused, but the silence of people who have been suddenly and without warning shown something private. "That was — those were the best evenings of my life. The furnace going and your pencil on the paper and the calculator and me not saying anything because I didn't need to say anything. That was the whole thing. That was everything." He was still holding the microphone but he'd lowered it slightly, and his voice was quieter on the recording now, and Marcus's camera was shaking slightly because Marcus was either drunk or moved or both.

"When your mother and I split up, I should have — the table was maybe eighty dollars at Sears. I didn't fight for it. I fought about the 401k and the Civic and the — I had a whole spreadsheet of things that mattered and the table wasn't on it, because how do you put a dollar value on sitting across from your kid while she does her math homework?" His voice stopped. Not a sob — a mechanical failure, the voice quitting because the thing behind it was larger than the aperture. He stood there for four seconds that, on the recording, felt like a weather event. Then he cleared his throat. "That's who I am. That's exactly who I am. I fight for the things with paperwork and I let the real stuff go."

Doug paused the video. He sat in the bathroom with the phone in his lap and the faucet dripping — he'd left it running — each drop hitting the basin with a sound he could feel in the bones of his face. His hands were resting on his thighs and they were shaking, not from anything he could name but from the specific physiological event of hearing yourself say the truest thing you've ever said and not remembering the decision to say it.

He pressed play.

The last ninety seconds were messier. Video-Doug had broken through something and couldn't find his way back to the form of a toast. He said something about Tyler — "He seems like a good guy, he laughed at the 401k thing, and I can't tell if that's polite or if he actually thought it was funny and I genuinely don't know which concerns me more." He said something about getting old — "I'm fifty-two. I live alone. I eat cereal for dinner most nights, standing at the counter, because sitting at a table by myself felt like it was making a statement I wasn't ready to commit to. And the cereal's gotten more expensive, which I take personally." He said something about Brendan's toast again — "I want to circle back to the sunset thing because that was really well done, Brendan, did you practice that? I'm not — that's a real question. Did you practice? Because if you just came up with that, then I've been outperformed worse than I thought."

And then he looked off-camera again, at the spot where Kelsey apparently was, and the room went still one more time.

"Kels." His voice was almost normal now, which was worse than when it had broken. "You turned out so good. I don't think that's because of me, or at least not the amount I'd want it to be. But you turned out really, really good, and I'm —" The failure again, longer this time. He stood there with the microphone in one hand and someone's stolen wine in the other and the room full of people waiting for him to finish a sentence he couldn't finish. "I'm proud of you in a way I don't have the right words for, which is why I'm saying it wrong, drunk, at your wedding, in front of your mother-in-law." A beat. "Tyler's mom, I apologize for the language earlier. And the bit about the furniture. And probably some other parts I haven't gotten to yet."

A hand — it looked like Tyler's, steady and unhesitating — reached in and took the microphone gently, the way you take something from a person who doesn't realize they're still holding it. Doug let it go. The applause that followed was scattered and uncertain, the sound of people who'd just watched something they would need to discuss in the car on the way home, and then the video cut to a wider angle that showed the room — the tables, the flowers, the wedding party — and in the far right of the frame, barely visible, Kelsey was looking at her father with an expression that Doug, watching from his bathroom, could not read, because the camera wasn't close enough and because even if it had been, he might not have had the vocabulary.

The video ended. 2.1 million views. Fourteen thousand comments. The runtime said 7:14.

Doug stared at the frozen final frame — a blurry wide shot of the ballroom with his own figure visible at the edge, still standing, one hand at his side. He sat there on the bathtub for what might have been a minute or five minutes. The faucet dripped. The heater ran. A car passed on the street outside, its tires on the wet pavement making a sound like paper tearing slowly.

He pressed play again.

The second time was different. The second time, the horror backed off enough for him to notice things. He noticed the moment where video-Doug said "that's a traffic safety issue" — the comedy wasn't in the words, it was in the delivery, the flat insurance-adjuster tone applied to the experience of crying so hard on the highway he couldn't change lanes. He noticed the furniture-depreciation line and heard it not as mortifying but as precise. It was exactly how he would describe a marriage if someone put a claims form in front of him, and the precision was what made the room react — not because it was cruel but because it was the kind of true that people organize their lives around not saying.

He noticed the table section, and it still made his stomach drop, but he also heard the silence in the room differently this time. It wasn't pity. It was the silence of people recognizing something — not the specific table, not Doug's specific failure, but the shape of it, the way everyone has a version of the thing they didn't fight for because they were too busy fighting for the things that had dollar values attached.

And he noticed the part about Kelsey. The "you turned out good" part. He'd meant every word of it, and the problem wasn't that he'd said it in front of 180 people after eight whiskeys. The problem was that he'd needed the eight whiskeys and the 180 people. He'd had four thousand evenings — standing in his kitchen, driving her to the dentist, sitting across from her at restaurants where she ordered pad thai and he ordered whatever was too spicy — and he'd talked about the weather and the Reds and the condition of the roads and never once said the thing he'd said drunk to a room full of strangers.

He put the phone down and walked into the kitchen. The apartment arranged itself around him the way it always did — the Ikea bookshelf with its slight forward lean, the one art print from a Columbus gallery that Megan had left when she moved out because it didn't match Brendan's house, the counter where he ate cereal standing up every evening. The second bedroom door was closed. It was always closed. Behind it were six boxes he'd packed twelve years ago and labeled in his own handwriting — BOOKS, KITCHEN MISC, TAX DOCS 2010-2013, and three that just said STUFF — and they sat in there in the dark the way they'd sat for the entirety of his life in this apartment, which was all of his life since the life before.

He drank a glass of water. He drank another one. He put the glass in the sink and looked out the window at the street, where a neighbor was chipping ice off a windshield with a credit card, and the ordinary fact of this — a Sunday in February in Clintonville, someone dealing with ice — was so far from what was happening inside his phone that the distance felt structural, a gap between two conditions that no adjuster could reconcile.

---

The phone rang at 12:15. Doug was in the kitchen in sweatpants and a T-shirt from the laundry pile, eating a piece of bread because bread was the only thing that didn't require a decision. The caller ID showed a 614 number he didn't recognize. He let it ring through. It rang again — same number. He let that go too.

A text from Roy: *Morning. Call me if you want. Don't if you don't.*

Doug looked at the text for a while. It was the right thing, constructed at the right scale — an offer that made no demand, carried no curiosity disguised as concern. Roy had sent four sentences and meant each of them at face value, which was a skill Doug had never seen anyone else manage.

He typed back: *I'm up. I'll call later.*

Roy: *Okay.*

The phone rang again. 216 — Cleveland. Kelsey. He let it go. He wasn't ready for Kelsey, for the conversation that would begin with him saying something insufficient about being sorry and end with her saying things she was too angry to organize, except Kelsey angry wasn't Kelsey loud — it was Kelsey quiet and specific, and Doug knew exactly what that was like because she'd gotten it from him.

He ate the bread. It was the heel, the end piece that most people threw away, and Doug ate it because discarding a usable portion of a purchased product had always seemed to him like a small, correctable failure. The bread was dry. He didn't toast it because the toaster had been making a burning smell since October and investigating it would mean pulling it away from the wall, and behind the toaster was an accumulation of crumbs from a period he preferred not to calculate, and the whole project exceeded what this particular Sunday could support.

His phone buzzed. Then again. Texts were arriving from numbers he didn't have saved — people who'd gotten his number from somewhere, a cousin in Akron he hadn't spoken to since his father's funeral, someone who identified themselves only as "Mike from college" though Doug could not place a Mike from college and had attended a commuter campus where he'd known perhaps eleven people. He put the phone face-down on the counter and watched the back of it vibrate against the laminate, a small persistent tremor like something trying to get his attention from the other side of a wall.

He thought about going back to bed. He thought about getting in his Civic and driving to the Mutual Fidelity office and sitting at his desk and opening the Hoffmeyer claim file, which concerned a detached garage in Reynoldsburg and a disputed cause of structural failure, and doing the work of assessing damage that belonged to someone else. He thought about the video. It was still accumulating views at a rate he could calculate — the insurance adjuster in him wanted the number, wanted to quantify the scope of the event the way he would quantify square footage of water damage or the replacement cost of a ruined furnace — but he stopped himself, because the number would be larger now, and larger was a direction he could not yet face.

He washed the glass. He wiped the counter. He stood at the window and watched the neighbor finish scraping the windshield and get in the car and drive away, and the street was empty again, and the quiet of it was the same quiet his apartment always had, except that now the quiet felt like the pause before something, like the space in a claims report between the description of the incident and the estimate of the loss.

The phone rang. He looked at the screen. 310. Los Angeles. Doug had never been to Los Angeles. He did not know anyone in Los Angeles. He had no professional or personal connection to the city, the state, or the time zone, and the number was not one that had any reason to appear on the phone of an insurance adjuster in Columbus, Ohio, on a Sunday afternoon in February.

He didn't answer.

The phone went still. The kitchen was quiet. The heater clicked off and the sudden absence of its noise was louder than the noise had been, and Doug stood there in his sweatpants with crumbs on his shirt and a hangover that had settled into something permanent and low-grade, more a new climate than a temporary condition, and the apartment was exactly what it had been when he'd gone to sleep — the bookshelf, the print, the closed door, the boxes behind it — except that somewhere between last night and now, the walls had become thinner, and two million people were on the other side of them.

The phone rang again. 310.


# Chapter 2

The video was at fourteen million views when Doug checked it at 6:47 a.m., standing in his bathroom in boxers and a t-shirt that said MUTUAL FIDELITY CLAIMS DIVISION SOFTBALL 2019. They had never had a softball division. Someone in marketing had ordered the shirts anyway, and Doug had kept his because it was soft and because he had no one to be embarrassed in front of.

Fourteen million. He said the number to the mirror, to his own face, which looked approximately the way a face looks after four hours of sleep, three of which had been spent reading what strangers thought of it. The number had no meaning. It was the population of a moderately sized country. Doug had looked this up at 1 a.m., when the count was still at eleven million and he'd needed some frame of reference — Belgium, or close to it. More people had watched him say things he couldn't remember deciding to say than lived in a nation with its own parliament and a functioning rail system.

He drove to work in the Civic with the heat on high because February in Columbus was the color of wet concrete and roughly as warm. His phone sat in the cup holder, buzzing at intervals that had stopped corresponding to individual events and had become ambient, like sitting on a washing machine. He'd stopped looking at each notification around midnight. The 310 number — Los Angeles, he'd figured out — had called six more times since Saturday. He hadn't answered. He didn't know anyone in Los Angeles. He didn't want to know anyone in Los Angeles.

The internet had been busy overnight. Doug knew this because he'd watched it happen before giving up on sleep, lying on his couch in the dark with the phone held above his face, reading the terms of his own situation.

There were memes now. Someone had captured the moment — three seconds into the toast, roughly — where Doug's left hand was raised in a gesture that, in context, was him reaching for words and, out of context, looked like a man delivering a papal blessing over his daughter's open bar. This image existed now with white block text: YOUR MARRIAGE HAS BEEN ADJUSTED. There was another one, his face mid-sentence, mouth open, eyes half-closed in what was clearly the alcohol but read on screen as beatific certainty: WHEN THE WHISKEY KICKS IN AND THE TRUTH COMES OUT. There were dozens more. Doug's face with the caption PROOF OF LOSS, which he recognized as insurance terminology someone had pulled from his job title, and which was more accurate than whoever made it probably intended.

Someone had auto-tuned the toast into a song. Doug had listened to thirty seconds. His voice — his actual voice, the one that came out of his body — had been pitch-corrected and layered over a beat that sounded like it belonged in a car driven by someone thirty years younger than him. The words were still his words. The cadence was still roughly his cadence. But the musicality made every sentence sound like it was building toward a chorus that never arrived, which was, Doug thought, not a bad description of his marriage.

He'd closed the app.

A Twitter thread by someone whose profile identified them as a "body language analyst and certified life coach" had broken down the toast frame by frame. Doug's posture (defeated). His hand gestures (subconsciously reaching). His eye contact with Kelsey (avoidant but searching). His single glance toward Megan, which the analyst called "the micro-expression of a man who has rehearsed saying nothing for twelve years and just forgot the script." The thread had four thousand retweets. Doug had watched the original video twice and could not identify any of the things this person was identifying, which either meant the analyst was making it up or that Doug's body had been doing things he hadn't authorized, which was, given the toast itself, not surprising.

A Reddit post titled "This man just eulogized his own marriage and I can't stop watching" had, when he'd last checked, forty thousand upvotes and a comment section that scrolled for what appeared to be geological ages. Doug had started reading the comments. This had been a mistake he intended to repeat.

The Mutual Fidelity parking lot looked the same as it always did. Doug pulled into his spot — not assigned, but his through the accumulation of years that turns habit into tenure — and sat in the Civic for a minute with the engine running. The building was three stories of beige brick. It processed insurance claims. It was the most uninteresting structure in a city that had several strong contenders, and Doug had never been so grateful to see it.

He walked in. The lobby had the same carpet, the same security desk where Greg sat — a large man who read Louis L'Amour paperbacks during the slow hours, which were most hours, the spines cracked and face-down on the counter like small collapsed tents. Greg waved Doug through every morning with a two-finger salute he'd been giving for nine years. This morning he held the salute a beat too long, and his expression had the quality of a man who wanted to say something and was choosing, with visible effort, not to.

"Morning, Greg."

"Morning, Doug." A pause. "Cold one today."

"Yeah."

Doug took the elevator to the second floor. The doors opened onto the open plan: thirty desks in rows, fluorescent lighting that made everyone look like they were recovering from something, the hum of phones and keyboards and the break-room coffee maker that produced a liquid Doug had accepted as coffee the way he accepted most things — without enthusiasm, without complaint, as a fact of the environment.

He could feel them looking. Not everyone — some people were working, or performing work, or genuinely hadn't seen the video, which was still possible on a Monday morning, though the window was closing fast. But enough. Karen from auto claims, who sat three rows over and who Doug knew primarily as someone who ate a hard-boiled egg at her desk every morning at 9:15, the smell arriving with the regularity and gentleness of a small chemical event — Karen looked up when he walked past and then looked down so fast she nearly pulled something. Dave, who handled commercial property and who kept a signed photo of Woody Hayes on his desk next to a jar of mixed nuts he ate from continuously with the unconscious rhythm of a man feeding a parking meter — Dave opened his mouth, closed it, opened it again, and said "Hey, Doug" in the careful voice people use when they're pretending this is a normal interaction and the pretending is the loudest thing in the room.

"Hey, Dave."

Doug sat at his desk. His desk was the same desk. His computer was the same computer. His work inbox — the Mutual Fidelity one — had seventeen new messages, which was about right for a Monday, though two turned out to be from people he barely knew in other departments who'd found pretexts to email him. One was from a woman in accounting named Sherry who needed "clarification on a claim number" and who had never contacted Doug directly in six years of working in the same building. The claim number she referenced was real, but the question about it was so simple that anyone with system access could have answered it themselves, and Doug understood this in the way he understood most things about Sherry — distantly, without opinion, and with the recognition that the email was about the video the way Janet was about to be about the Hoffmeyer claim.

Janet appeared at 9:40. She stood at the edge of his cubicle in her usual posture — one hand on the fabric partition, the other holding her coffee mug, which was ceramic and white and said WORLD'S OKAY-EST MOM, a gift from her teenage son that she'd been drinking from for two years with an expression that suggested she was still deciding whether it was affectionate or an insult. Janet kept a dish of butterscotch hard candies on her desk, the kind in gold cellophane wrappers, and she'd been offering them before difficult conversations for so long that the appearance of a butterscotch had become, around the office, a kind of early warning system. She wasn't holding a candy now, which was either a good sign or evidence that she was trying to approach this casually and couldn't quite get there.

"Doug. Do you have a minute?"

"Sure."

She sat in the chair next to his desk — technically Roy's overflow chair, but it served as the conference seat for conversations that didn't merit the actual conference room, which had been double-booked since January and retained the faint memory of a tuna someone had microwaved in there during the holidays.

"The Hoffmeyer claim," Janet said. She opened the folder. "The contractor's estimate came in, and it's within range, but I want to make sure we're documenting the square footage correctly. We had that issue last month with the Patterson file where the adjuster — not you — used the wrong calculation for the roof pitch, and we ended up overpaying by eleven hundred dollars, which isn't catastrophic but it's not nothing."

"I can double-check the measurements."

"Good. Thank you."

They looked at each other. Janet closed the Hoffmeyer folder. She didn't leave. She held the folder in her lap and directed her gaze at a spot slightly left of Doug's face — the spot on the partition where he'd pinned a Columbus Blue Jackets schedule that was two seasons out of date and that he kept meaning to take down and hadn't, because taking it down would require deciding what, if anything, to replace it with, and that question, over time, had become harder rather than easier.

"So," Janet said.

"Yeah," Doug said.

Janet opened her mouth, closed it, and stood up. "Let me know about the Hoffmeyer measurements."

"I will."

She walked back to her office. Doug watched her go. She paused at her desk, picked up a butterscotch, unwrapped it, and put it in her mouth. She stood there with the cellophane in her hand and the expression of a woman who had failed to say something and was using sugar to fill the space where the words should have been.

---

Roy arrived at 10:15, which was late. Roy was never late. Roy was early or precisely on time because his relationship with time was the same as his relationship with everything else — he'd arrived at an accommodation and didn't see the point in revisiting it. But this morning Roy had been to the dentist, and the right side of his jaw was swollen in a way that made him look like he was keeping a small, uncomfortable piece of information in his cheek. He sat at his desk, which was adjacent to Doug's in the way it had been adjacent for nine years, and opened his computer with the patient deliberation of a man logging into a system he didn't particularly want to use but had accepted as part of the arrangement.

"Morning," Roy said. The word came out slightly sideways because of the jaw.

"Morning. You okay?"

"Root canal." Roy said this the way he said everything — as fact, without commentary or expectation.

"Sorry."

"It's fine. It's a tooth."

Doug's phone buzzed on the desk. He turned it face-down. His work email dinged — Janet, forwarding something from HR titled "Social Media Guidelines: A Reminder for All Employees," which had been sent to the full office but which Doug understood, with the practiced clarity of a man trained to read the subtext of policy language, was for him.

"Roy."

"Yeah."

"Did you—" Doug stopped. He wasn't sure how to phrase it. "Have you been on the internet this weekend?"

Roy looked at him with the expression of a man who'd been asked whether he'd visited the surface of Mars. "I don't watch videos," he said. "I barely watch television. I'm not opposed to it." He paused, as if confirming to himself that this was accurate, and determined that it was. "I just don't find it adds anything."

"Okay."

"Why?"

Doug looked at his phone, which was buzzing against the desk surface with a sound like something small and trapped. "No reason."

Roy considered this. He opened a file — paper, because Roy still preferred paper files even though the system had been digital for three years and Janet had stopped fighting this preference approximately eighteen months ago.

"I heard something," Roy said, without looking up.

"Yeah?"

"Something happened at the wedding. A couple people mentioned it."

"Yeah."

"I don't need to know what it was."

"Okay."

"I'm just saying that. In case you were going to tell me."

"I wasn't."

"Good." Roy turned a page. He read it. He turned another page. Doug watched him read the way you watch a person do something simple and ordinary when the rest of the world has become complicated and strange — with a gratitude so large he could feel it in his hands, which had been gripping the armrests of his chair without his noticing, and which he now made himself release.

The desk phone rang. The landline, which almost never rang because most business came through email and the only people who called the desk line were older policyholders who didn't trust computers and Kelsey, who sometimes tried the office when Doug's cell went to voicemail. He picked it up.

"Doug Bridge, Mutual Fidelity."

"Mr. Bridge, this is Tammy Hess from the *Today* show. I'm calling to see if—"

"I'm sorry, this is my work line."

"I understand, and I apologize. We got your office number from—"

"I'm sorry," Doug said, and hung up.

He looked at the phone. The *Today* show had his work number. The *Today* show had found the general number for Mutual Fidelity Insurance, navigated the automated directory, and selected Doug Bridge, Claims Division. He tried to imagine the person — Tammy Hess — sitting at a desk somewhere, probably in New York, probably in a building that was not beige brick, calling an insurance adjuster in Columbus because of something he'd said drunk at a wedding.

"Who was that?" Roy asked.

"Nobody. Wrong number."

Roy accepted this the way he accepted everything — completely, without investigation. He turned another page.

Doug's personal phone had accumulated, during the Roy conversation, eleven more emails to the Gmail account he used for nothing except password resets and the occasional communication from Kelsey's high school, which she'd graduated from ten years ago and which still sent him fundraising appeals he'd never unsubscribed from because the process of unsubscribing felt, somehow, like a more decisive severing than the divorce had been.

The emails were from people and organizations he'd heard of and many he hadn't. A production company. A podcast called *Dads Being Real*. Two literary agents. A woman who identified herself as a "brand partnership consultant." Someone from a morning radio show in Cincinnati. The subject lines varied — "Quick question," "Loved your speech," "Opportunity," "Collaboration inquiry," "YOU'RE AMAZING" — and each one was a small door Doug had not opened and did not want to open and could not stop looking at.

He put the phone in his desk drawer. He closed the drawer. The phone continued buzzing inside the drawer, muffled now, the sound of a wasp under a glass.

He opened the Hoffmeyer file. He measured the roof pitch calculation. He documented the square footage. The numbers were straightforward and the numbers asked nothing of him and he read them with a care that anyone watching might have mistaken for dedication but was actually something closer to need.

By noon the video was at twenty-two million. Doug knew this because he checked in the bathroom, standing in a stall with his pants on and the seat down, reading the screen with the focused attention of a man studying the results of a test he hadn't known he was taking. He refreshed the page. Twenty-two million and climbing. He put the phone away and went back to his desk and ate the turkey sandwich he'd brought from home and didn't taste any of it.

By two o'clock it was at twenty-six million. By the time he left the office at 5:30, walking across the parking lot in the dark — because February in Columbus meant darkness at 5:30, and the parking lot lights made the slush look orange, and the cold was the kind that found the gap between his collar and his neck and settled there — the count had reached thirty-one million.

---

He drove home. The apartment was the same as he'd left it — the couch cushion still dented from where he'd sat reading comments until two the night before, the suit from the wedding draped over the kitchen chair where he'd finally taken it off Sunday afternoon, the one dress shoe by the front door and the other one still unaccounted for. He'd been wearing one shoe when he woke up Sunday morning. The other was somewhere between the reception hall and his apartment, and the fact that he'd been home for two days and hadn't looked for it or thought about it until now was the kind of thing that, in a different person's life, might have been funny.

He stood at the kitchen counter and ate a bowl of cereal. Raisin Bran, the store brand, not because he preferred it but because it was $2.19 and the name brand was $4.49 and the taste difference wasn't enough to justify the markup. This was the kind of calculation Megan had watched him make for eighteen years — the per-unit comparison, the coupon logic, the small satisfactions of rational purchasing — and the look on her face during the last three years of their marriage, when he'd hold up two boxes of something in the cereal aisle and explain the math, had been the look of a woman who had realized that the man she'd married would comparison-shop his way through every experience, including the ones where the point was not the savings but the standing next to someone in a grocery store because you wanted to be there.

The cereal was fine. The apartment was quiet. Outside, a car drove past with its bass turned up so loud the glasses in the cabinet above the sink rattled against each other, a sound Doug heard every night at roughly this time and had stopped noticing and now noticed again because noticing was all he'd been doing for two days and he couldn't turn it off.

He checked the phone. Forty-four million.

The number had more than tripled since the morning. Doug tried to visualize forty-four million people. He could not. He tried to think of it in insurance terms — forty-four million policyholders, each one a file, each file a name and an address and a specific thing that had gone wrong with their house. He couldn't process that either. The number existed outside any frame he had for understanding the world. He assessed claims for houses in central Ohio. He understood square footage, depreciation schedules, the rate at which a water-damaged hardwood floor loses value. He did not understand forty-four million strangers forming opinions about seven minutes of his life that he himself could barely remember living.

He sat on the couch. He opened the comments.

He knew this was a mistake. He'd known it was a mistake the first time, Sunday morning, and had done it anyway, the way he sometimes couldn't stop re-reading a claim file that didn't add up — the compulsion of looking for the one detail that would make the whole thing cohere.

The comments didn't cohere. They weren't supposed to. They were the sound of a fraction of forty-four million people processing Doug Bridge into something that fit their needs.

**This guy is my hero.** That was the most common type. Doug as folk hero, Doug as truth-teller, Doug as the man who said what everyone thinks but nobody says. These came with exclamation marks and crying-laughing emoji and the phrase "I felt that" repeated so many times it had stopped being a feeling and become a category.

**This is the saddest thing I've ever seen.** The second type. Doug as cautionary tale, Doug as the embodiment of male loneliness, Doug as what happens when you don't talk to someone for twelve years and then talk to everyone at once. A woman with a profile picture of a sunset had written a three-paragraph comment about her own father that was longer and more emotionally detailed than anything Doug's own daughter had said to him about the toast, and he read it twice and then felt guilty for reading it, which was a strange thing to feel about a stranger's confession about her own life that had nothing and everything to do with his.

**This is clearly staged.** A persistent minority. Doug was an actor. The whole thing was a marketing stunt. The "wedding" was fake. One commenter had produced a frame-by-frame analysis arguing that the lighting was "too professional for a phone camera" and that Doug's stumble at the four-minute mark was "choreographed." Doug had been drunk enough that he couldn't remember the stumble at all, but he could guarantee it hadn't been choreographed, because he had never choreographed anything in his life, including the one dance at his own wedding, which Megan had led.

**What an asshole.** The angry ones. Doug had ruined his daughter's wedding. Doug had humiliated his ex-wife in front of everyone she knew. Doug was a narcissist, a drunk, a man who had made his child's day about his own failures. These were the ones that came from people who seemed to be yelling — sentences short and emphatic and absolutely certain — and Doug read them with the numb attention of a man reviewing a claims denial he'd already anticipated. They weren't wrong. Some of the things he'd said — he still hadn't watched the full video a third time, but the pieces he remembered were enough — some of those things should not have been said. Not because they weren't true but because a wedding reception at the Columbus Athenaeum with eighty guests and an open bar and his daughter in a white dress was not the venue for truth, and knowing this didn't help, because knowing things after the fact was Doug's particular skill, and it had never once in his life made any difference.

**I can't stop watching this.** The addicts. People who'd watched it three times, five times, twelve times. People who'd memorized lines. People who quoted his sentences back with timestamps and wrote "THIS PART" as though the words were a shared text they'd all studied. Doug did not understand how something he'd said once, drunk, to a room of eighty people at the Athenaeum, had become a thing strangers annotated.

There were others. A man who'd written, simply, "I'm going to call my daughter." A woman who said, "The part about the table made me leave my husband," and Doug stared at this for a long time because a person he had never met was claiming that something he'd said, drunk, about a table — a table; he could barely remember the table part — had ended her marriage, and either she was exaggerating or she wasn't, and he didn't know which was worse. A teenager, or someone who typed like one, had written: "ok boomer but also i'm crying." A commenter named InsuranceBro614, whose username alone made Doug's stomach tighten because it could have been someone from the office, had written: "I work in claims and this is the most accurate description of a marriage I've ever heard from an adjuster. 10/10 would recommend the whiskey." Doug did not want to figure out who InsuranceBro614 was. He also could not stop trying to figure out who InsuranceBro614 was.

He read comments until 1:45 a.m. The apartment was dark except for the phone's glow. His cereal bowl was in the sink, unwashed. The suit was still on the chair. The missing shoe was still missing.

At 1:47 he put the phone down and picked up the Gahanna file.

The Gahanna claim was a water heater. A standard forty-gallon residential unit in a house on Agler Road — the homeowner had submitted it Friday, before the wedding, and it had been sitting in Doug's take-home stack since then, untouched, while Doug was getting drunk and saying true things and becoming a person forty-four million strangers had opinions about. The water heater had burst at the base seam. The basement had taken four inches of standing water before the homeowner found it. He'd documented everything: photographs of the water line on the basement walls, the receipt for the original unit purchased in 2016, a contractor's estimate for remediation and replacement. The documentation was clean. The coverage was clear. The depreciation on a 2016 unit was calculable — ten years of expected life, six years in service, the math was straightforward and the math didn't care what Doug had done at a wedding.

He read the file. He read it again. He read the contractor's estimate line by line — demolition of damaged drywall, removal of saturated carpet padding, installation of dehumidification equipment for a minimum of seventy-two hours, replacement of the forty-gallon unit with a comparable model, labor at $85 per hour for an estimated fourteen hours. The numbers were real. They referred to actual things — water that had actually damaged a floor, carpet that was actually ruined, a heater that had actually failed at a seam the way heaters do when they're six years old and the anode rod hasn't been replaced and nobody checks the anode rod because nobody thinks about the thing that keeps the tank from corroding until the tank corrodes. The numbers didn't have opinions about Doug. They didn't think he was brave or pathetic or a genius. They were a claim, and he was an adjuster, and the distance between the problem and the resolution was a thing he could measure and close.

He made notes on the file. He flagged a question about whether the homeowner's policy covered mold remediation or whether that would require a separate rider — the standing water had been there for several hours before discovery, and four inches for several hours in a basement with limited ventilation was enough to start a mold issue that the homeowner might not see for weeks. He paper-clipped the estimate to the photograph of the water line. He put the file on the kitchen table, aligned with the edge, and set his pen on top of it.

It was 2:20 a.m. The phone sat face-down on the couch where he'd left it. The screen was dark. Somewhere — on the internet, in Belgium, in all the places people look at their phones in the middle of the night — millions of people were watching Doug Bridge say the truest things he'd ever said, and none of them knew that the man in the video was sitting in a two-bedroom apartment in Clintonville, in his underwear, at a kitchen table, at 2:20 in the morning, processing a water heater claim from Gahanna because it was the only thing left that made sense.


# Chapter 3

Doug got to the office at ten to eight. He was always early, not from ambition but from the same instinct that made him check his mirrors twice before changing lanes — a sense that arriving on time meant arriving late, and arriving late meant something had gone wrong that he should have prevented. The parking lot was a third full. Roy's car, a 2011 Camry with a dent in the rear quarter panel that Roy had decided years ago was not worth fixing, was already in its usual spot near the loading dock, where nobody else parked because it was farthest from the entrance and because nobody else wanted to walk, which was exactly why Roy parked there.

Mutual Fidelity occupied the second floor of a building on Sawyer Road that also housed a tax preparation service and an orthodontist whose patients could sometimes be heard through the floor, a low hum of suction and distress that Doug had long ago stopped noticing. The elevator was slow enough that Doug took the stairs, which he did not consider exercise. The office was open-plan, thirty desks arranged in rows of five, separated by low partitions that provided the illusion of privacy without any of its function. The carpet was the color of weak tea. The ceiling tiles were acoustic and stained in two places from the building's intermittent plumbing issues, which Doug understood better than the building management did — he'd assessed the office's own water damage claim two years ago, and the repair had not addressed the root cause, which was a compression fitting in the third-floor bathroom that would fail again within eighteen months. He'd noted this in his report. No one had followed up.

His desk was fourth in the second row, next to the window. Roy's desk was across the partition. Both desks had the same equipment — a monitor, a keyboard, a phone, a wire inbox that held actual paper because claims documentation still arrived by mail more often than people assumed. Doug's desk had a coffee mug from the 2019 office Secret Santa that said WORLD'S MOST ADEQUATE on it, a gift from Roy that was either a joke or a description, and Doug had never asked which because he suspected the answer was both.

He hung his coat on the back of his chair and turned on his monitor. The inbox had eleven new emails, eight of them internal. Two were from claimants. One was from his supervisor Janet, forwarded without comment, subject line: "FW: Social Media Use and Company Representation — Revised Guidelines." Doug opened it. The email was from HR and used the phrase "professional conduct in digital spaces" three times in four paragraphs. It did not mention Doug by name. It didn't need to. Mutual Fidelity had existed for forty-one years and none of its employees had ever gone viral for anything, a record that Doug had broken by accident on a Saturday night with eight whiskeys in him and a microphone someone should have taken away.

He closed the email and opened the first claim file.

The morning had its own architecture. Doug liked mornings at the office — the specific quiet of thirty people doing the same kind of work, the heating system pushing warm air through the ceiling vents with a sound like slow breathing, the occasional phone conversation conducted in the moderate tone everyone used, as if the entire floor had agreed on a volume and stuck to it. He'd been here fifteen years. The work suited him in a way he'd never had to justify because no one had ever asked. You processed claims. You read the documentation, assessed the damage, compared it to the policy, determined what was covered and what wasn't, and issued a finding. The work was precise, specific, and consequential to the people whose houses had flooded or burned or collapsed, and it required a kind of attention that Doug gave without effort — the attention of someone who noticed what was actually there rather than what should be there.

His specialty since 2014 was residential water damage. He'd ended up in it the way people end up in most specialties — a staffing need, a reassignment, a gradual accumulation of expertise that became identity. He knew what happened when a pipe burst in a wall at 2 a.m. in January — the sound first, which homeowners always described as a pop or a crack but was really more of a give, something structural surrendering. He knew the smell of a house three days after a sump pump failure — the sweet, vegetal rot that got into the drywall and stayed there, behind the paint, in the fiberglass insulation, months after the standing water was gone. He knew the look on people's faces when they stood in their own ruined living rooms holding the documentation they'd been told to gather, which was always either too much or not enough and never exactly what the form asked for.

The first file on his desk was a garage roof in Reynoldsburg. A family named Pardee had woken up on a Thursday to find their garage ceiling bowed inward, the weight of ice accumulation having finally exceeded the structural capacity of a roof that was, according to the building permit, twenty-three years old. The claim was straightforward — ice damage, covered under the homeowner's policy, minus the deductible. Doug reviewed the adjuster's field photos on his monitor: the sagging drywall, the water stain spreading across it like a bruise, the ice dam visible on the exterior shot where the gutter had separated from the fascia board. He compared the roof age to the depreciation schedule and prepared his assessment. The Pardees were going to get less than they expected, because the roof's actual cash value was not what it would cost to replace it — it was what a twenty-three-year-old roof was worth with twenty-three years of weather on it. This was the part of the job that required a specific kind of honesty: telling people that the thing they'd lost was worth less than they thought, by the numbers, even though the numbers had nothing to do with what it felt like to wake up to a ceiling caving in over your car.

He finished the Pardee file at nine-fifteen and opened the next one. A grease fire in a duplex on East Main. The tenant had left a pan of oil on the stove and gone to check on a child in the other room, which was a sentence Doug had written in various forms hundreds of times — the particular American catastrophe of unattended cooking oil, which caused more residential fires than any other single source, and which almost always started with someone leaving the kitchen to do something that seemed like it would take ten seconds. The fire itself was contained to the kitchen, but the smoke had traveled through the shared wall into the other unit, and the question of how far smoke damage extended was always a negotiation between what the nose could smell and what the policy defined as "damaged." The neighbor in unit B claimed she could still smell it in her bedroom closet. The insurer's position was that odor without visible residue did not constitute damage. Doug was going to have to go out there with a thermal imaging camera and settle it. He made a note.

He was halfway through the grease fire documentation when Kevin Beech from IT stopped at his desk. Kevin was twenty-six, tall, with the kind of beard that was either intentional or a decision he'd forgotten to revisit. He carried a laptop under one arm and held his phone in the other hand in a way that suggested he was about to use it. He'd been wearing the same pair of New Balance sneakers for as long as Doug had known him, gray with a orange stripe, and they were holding up in a way that seemed to bother Kevin, like he was waiting for permission to replace them.

"Hey, Doug." Kevin shifted the laptop to his other arm. "So I know this is kind of random, but — could I get a picture? My girlfriend's been sharing your video all week and she's watched it like fifteen times."

Doug looked at Kevin. Kevin had fixed his email three months ago when the Outlook sync failed, and they had discussed nothing personal on that occasion or any other.

"A picture," Doug said.

"Yeah, like a selfie? If that's cool. She'd just — yeah."

It was not cool, in a way Doug couldn't have explained. It was a small thing, a phone held up, a moment. But something about it felt like being reclassified — moved from one category to another without filling out the paperwork. He'd been a coworker Kevin nodded at in the hallway. Now he was something Kevin's girlfriend wanted proof of.

"Sure," Doug said, and Kevin leaned in and held up his phone and Doug made a face that he could feel, from the inside, was the face of a man being identified in a lineup.

"Awesome. Thanks, man." Kevin looked at the photo. "She's gonna lose it. You're, like, her favorite person on the internet right now."

Doug wanted to say that being someone's favorite person on the internet was a category he had not known existed until a week ago and would have preferred to go on not knowing about. He said, "Tell her I said thanks."

Kevin left. Roy, on the other side of the partition, had not looked up from his monitor.

"That'll happen," Roy said.

"What will."

"The picture thing. Once people know you're a person who gets pictures taken, they want to take pictures." Roy typed something, finished it, and leaned back. "My nephew is on YouTube. He reviews flashlights. People send him flashlights. He has over three hundred flashlights now."

"He reviews flashlights."

"He reviews flashlights. He's very thorough about it. Tests the lumens, the battery life, the water resistance. I don't understand it but he seems to enjoy himself. He's got a whole room that's just flashlights."

Doug couldn't tell if this was offered as comfort, comparison, or simply information. With Roy, it was usually the third.

He turned back to his monitor and opened the next file.

---

The pool claim had been sitting in his queue for two days, and Doug had been saving it the way some people save the good part of a meal — not consciously, just pushing it down the stack. The file was labeled BURRIS/COMMONWEALTH — two parties, two policies, a jurisdictional tangle that the intake notes described as "complex coverage determination required."

Carl Burris, of Westerville, owned a twenty-four-foot above-ground pool. Round, steel-walled, installed in 2019 by a company that had since gone out of business, which was not relevant to the claim but was noted in the file and struck Doug as the kind of detail that attached itself to bad luck. On the night of February 8th, the wall of the pool had split along a manufacturing seam. The manufacturer's warranty had expired nineteen months prior. Approximately six thousand gallons of water — Doug did the math: a pool that size held roughly eight thousand gallons when full, and February in central Ohio meant it was at three-quarter capacity from snowmelt and winter runoff — had poured through the gap in the wall, crossed eight feet of frozen yard, gone under the chain-link fence that separated Burris's property from his neighbor's, and flooded the rear garden of one Margaret Commonwealth.

Margaret Commonwealth grew roses. Specifically, she grew hybrid tea roses, which Doug learned from the documentation were a particular category of rose bred for large blooms on long stems, the kind you saw in florist shops and at funerals. She had seventeen of them. She had receipts for all seventeen, dating back over six years of purchases from a nursery in Delaware, Ohio, that specialized in rose stock. The total documented value of the seventeen hybrid teas was $4,218.47, which included the plants themselves, the soil amendments, a drip irrigation system she'd installed herself, and a cedar trellis structure that the floodwater had knocked sideways and cracked at the base.

The roses were dead. Six thousand gallons of chlorinated pool water in February had killed them comprehensively — the chlorine burned the root systems and the cold prevented any chance of recovery. Margaret Commonwealth had photographs. She had taken them, Doug noted, in the specific way of someone who understood that documentation mattered: each plant labeled, each receipt matched to a purchase date, the trellis photographed from three angles. She had done this better than most professional claimants he worked with. Her file was immaculate. The roses were still dead.

She had filed the claim. And here was where it got interesting.

Carl Burris's homeowner's policy covered the structural failure of the pool — the replacement cost of the wall, the labor to repair it. But his liability coverage, Section II of the standard homeowner's form, covered bodily injury and property damage to third parties only under specific conditions, and the definition of "occurrence" in Burris's policy required that the damage result from either an "accident" or "negligence." The pool wall splitting was arguably an accident. But the insurer could also argue negligence — that Burris should have inspected the wall before winter, should have maintained the seam, should have known that a steel wall installed by a company that no longer existed might not last forever. The adjuster's field notes indicated that Burris had not inspected the wall. Burris had not, as far as Doug could determine from the documentation, thought about the wall at all between the day the pool was installed and the night it failed. Doug understood this completely. Most people did not think about the structural integrity of the things that held their lives in place until those things gave way.

Margaret Commonwealth's policy was a different problem entirely. Her homeowner's coverage included a rider for her garden — she'd had the foresight to schedule the rose collection individually, which meant the plants were listed and valued on the policy. This was unusual and, Doug thought, genuinely impressive. But the coverage was for named perils: wind, hail, fire, theft, vandalism, and a handful of other events that the actuarial tables considered worth insuring against. Not flood. Not "structural failure of adjacent property." Not "your neighbor's above-ground pool catastrophically discharging six thousand gallons of chlorinated water onto your prize hybrid teas in the middle of February," which was not a named peril in any homeowner's policy Doug had ever read, because the actuarial tables had not, apparently, accounted for this possibility.

Doug read the file twice. He pulled up the policy language for both Burris and Commonwealth on side-by-side windows. He cross-referenced the liability endorsement on Burris's policy with the Ohio Revised Code standards for negligence in property maintenance. He looked up the replacement cost of hybrid tea roses from the Delaware nursery's website, which listed prices by cultivar — the cultivar names alone were worth the search: Mister Lincoln, Double Delight, Peace, Chrysler Imperial. Margaret Commonwealth had a Pink Peace that the nursery listed at ninety-two dollars.

He was, he realized, enjoying himself. Not the loss itself — the loss was real, the roses were dead, Margaret Commonwealth's six years of cultivation were gone and no policy was going to bring back a mature Pink Peace in bloom. But the coverage question was genuine. Two policies, neither designed for this scenario, both containing language that could be read in multiple directions, and the resolution would determine who paid for seventeen dead roses and a cracked cedar trellis. It was the kind of problem that had a right answer buried somewhere inside it, and finding that answer required exactly the kind of attention Doug was built to give.

---

The sandwich place was called Sal's, or had been — Sal had sold it four years ago to a couple named Park who had kept the name because changing the sign would have cost more than the goodwill it might generate. It was a block and a half from the office, a narrow storefront with a counter, a deli case, and six tables with chairs that didn't quite match. Doug and Roy had been eating here every workday for nine years. Roy ordered the same thing every time: turkey on wheat, mustard, no tomato. Doug varied his order within a narrow range — ham and swiss, or roast beef, or occasionally the Italian, which was good but more bread than he wanted by the end.

Today Doug got the roast beef. Roy got the turkey. They sat at the table by the window, which was their table in the way that it was simply the table they always sat at, not because they'd claimed it or would have been bothered if someone else were sitting there. No one ever was. The table wobbled slightly and had wobbled for the entire nine years, and neither Doug nor Roy had ever mentioned it or attempted to fix it, which Doug supposed was a form of acceptance.

"So the pool wall splits," Doug said. He was holding his sandwich but hadn't bitten into it yet. "Six thousand gallons. February. It goes under the fence and into the neighbor's garden."

Roy ate. He ate with the efficiency of a man who considered lunch a task to be completed rather than an experience to be prolonged. He chewed, swallowed, and listened with the same expression he wore for everything, which was no particular expression at all.

"The neighbor has roses," Doug said. "Seventeen hybrid teas. She's got receipts for all of them going back six years. She scheduled them on her policy individually. And they're all dead."

"Chlorine," Roy said.

"Chlorine and cold. Either one probably would've done it. Both together, there's no question."

"So she files."

"She files. But here's the thing — whose policy covers it? Burris, the pool owner, he's got liability coverage for third-party damage, but only if it qualifies as an occurrence under the definition, which means either accident or negligence. Did the wall splitting count as negligence? He never inspected it. Nobody inspects their pool wall. But the insurer could argue he should've."

Roy took another bite. He was already halfway through the sandwich. "What about her policy."

"Named perils only. She's got the garden rider, which is smart — most people don't schedule their plants — but the perils are wind, hail, fire, theft. Not flood. And not 'my neighbor's pool blew out.'"

"Nobody's policy covers that."

"Nobody's policy covers that. That's the gap. Both policies get close but neither one closes the distance. And in the middle you've got seventeen dead roses and a woman who did everything right — she documented, she insured, she scheduled. And it still might not be enough."

Roy considered this. He folded the paper his sandwich had come in into a neat square and set it on the tray. He'd done this every day for nine years. The square was always the same size.

"How much," he said.

"The roses? Four thousand two hundred eighteen dollars and forty-seven cents. That's plants, soil, irrigation, and a cedar trellis."

"That's a lot of roses."

"Hybrid teas. Some of those cultivars run sixty, seventy dollars a plant. She had a Pink Peace listed at ninety-two."

Roy looked out the window. The street was gray and wet, the kind of late-winter Columbus day where the sky and the pavement were the same color and the temperature sat right at thirty-four degrees, which was too warm for snow and too cold for anything to feel like it was going anywhere. A woman walked past with a stroller that had a plastic rain cover over it, the kind that made the stroller look like a small greenhouse.

"You'll figure it out," Roy said.

"I don't know that I will. There might not be a clean answer."

"There's always an answer. Sometimes the answer's just that nobody pays."

Doug bit into his sandwich. The roast beef was fine. It had been fine for nine years. He had not ever needed it to be more than fine, which was either a statement about the sandwich or about Doug, and he chose not to determine which.

They ate. The lunch crowd at Sal's was small and consistent — two women from the physical therapy clinic downstairs who came in most days, a man in a reflective vest who ordered three subs to go and left without sitting down. The Parks' daughter, who was maybe ten, sat behind the counter doing homework with headphones on, her pencil moving in the unhurried rhythm of a kid who'd been doing homework in this exact spot for years. She had a juice box balanced on a stack of napkins.

Roy finished and set his hands flat on the table, which was how Roy indicated he was done eating but not ready to leave. He sat like that, looking out the window.

Doug watched him. Roy's life fit him the way certain clothes fit certain people — not tailored, not stylish, but the right size and worn in. He owned a house in Gahanna that was, by Roy's own description, "a house." Three bedrooms, one of which he used, one of which was empty, one of which held a treadmill he'd bought in 2017 and used regularly until he decided that walking outside achieved the same result without the monthly cost of electricity, so the treadmill sat there now, unplugged and draped with a bath towel he'd never retrieved. He drove the dented Camry. He watched no streaming services and did not own a subscription to anything except the newspaper, which arrived in physical form and which he read at the kitchen table each morning. He read one book a month from the Columbus Metropolitan Library, choosing the next one by walking to the new arrivals shelf and picking up whatever was at eye level — a system he'd described once, without irony, and which Doug had found both unreasonable and difficult to argue with. He went to bed at nine forty-five every night.

Roy was not performing anything. He was not making a statement about simplicity or consumption or the way other people lived. He was a man who had arrived at a set of preferences through experience and saw no reason to revise them. He was content — not happy, which implied a condition that could be lost, but content, which implied one that had been decided on. Doug found this comforting and also, in a way he couldn't quite name, vaguely threatening, like sitting next to someone who'd already finished an exam you were still working on.

"You hear from the phone people?" Roy said. He meant the calls Doug had been getting — the interview requests, the media inquiries, the 310 area code that kept ringing. Doug had mentioned them at the end of yesterday, briefly, in the tone of someone reporting a weather event that had happened nearby.

"A few more," Doug said. "I haven't been answering."

"Probably wise."

"I don't know what I'd say."

"You'd say whatever you'd say. Then they'd put it somewhere and other people would have opinions about it. That's how it works."

"That's how it works," Doug repeated. Not as agreement. Because hearing Roy say it made it sound both obvious and slightly terrifying.

Roy looked at Doug's plate. "You going to eat that pickle?"

Doug looked at his plate. There was a pickle spear he hadn't touched. He didn't like pickles, which Roy knew, and Roy liked pickles, which Doug knew, and the transfer of the pickle had been happening for so long that asking about it was itself the point — not necessary, but repeated, and the repetition had made it into something.

"All yours," Doug said.

Roy ate the pickle. They walked back to the office. The air was cold and smelled like car exhaust and the particular mineral flatness that Columbus air carried in February — no flowers, no cut grass, nothing decomposing or growing, just the neutral smell of a city in winter waiting for a reason to change. Doug's phone was in his coat pocket. It had not buzzed during lunch, which was a relief and also, in some small way he didn't want to look at directly, a disappointment.

---

He spent the afternoon on the Commonwealth file. He called the Burris insurer's claims department and spent fourteen minutes on hold listening to a piano arrangement of a song he almost recognized before a representative named Theresa confirmed that the liability endorsement on Burris's policy did include a negligence provision but that the company's position was that pool wall failure due to manufacturing defect did not constitute owner negligence. Doug disagreed but said he'd review the language again. Theresa sounded tired. She probably had forty open files on her desk. Doug thanked her and hung up.

He called Margaret Commonwealth's insurer next. The representative there, whose name Doug didn't catch because the man spoke as though the phone were a formality he'd rather not be participating in, confirmed that the garden rider covered named perils only and that water damage from a neighbor's structural failure was not among them. Doug asked about the policy's "additional coverage" section, which sometimes included a catch-all provision for unusual events. The representative said he'd check. He did not sound like a man who intended to check.

At four-thirty Janet stopped by his desk. Janet was fifty-three, had managed the residential team for eleven years, and wore reading glasses on a beaded chain that she adjusted so often the motion had become unconscious — she'd reach up and touch the chain even when the glasses were already in place, the way some people touch a ring they've been wearing for years. She had a pen behind one ear that Doug was fairly sure she'd forgotten was there.

"Did you get a chance to look at the email I forwarded?" she said.

"I did."

"HR wants to make sure everyone's aware of the updated guidelines." She said this with the expression of a person delivering a message she had not written and did not entirely endorse. "It's not about you specifically."

"I know."

"It's a general policy refresh." She paused. The pen shifted behind her ear as she tilted her head. "How's the Commonwealth file looking?"

"Complicated. Two policies, coverage gap between them. Neither one was written for what actually happened."

Janet nodded. This was the language she understood. "Keep me posted. If it goes to subrogation, I'll need the full timeline."

She walked away. Doug appreciated Janet for the same reason he appreciated the office itself: she operated within a system that had consistent rules, and those rules held even when the outcomes were unsatisfying. She had not asked about the video. She had not asked how he was doing or whether he was okay or what he planned to do about any of it. She had forwarded the HR email without comment and then asked about a file, and both actions were exactly what they should have been.

At five-fifteen Doug shut down his monitor. Roy was already putting on his coat — Roy left at five-fifteen every day, not from rigidity but because he saw no benefit in staying past the time he was paid to stay. They walked to the parking lot together without talking, which was not silence so much as the absence of anything that needed to be said, and the difference between those two things was one of the reasons the friendship worked.

Doug got into his Civic. The seat was cold through his pants.
