He started the engine and sat for a moment while the heat worked its way through the vents, watching the lot empty in the ordinary rhythm of a workday ending — cars pulling out one by one, headlights in the gray dusk, everyone going somewhere that was not here.

His phone buzzed in his coat pocket. A text from a number he didn't recognize: *Hi Doug, this is Layla Banks — we spoke briefly last week. I'd really love to set up a time to chat about some opportunities. I think there's something real here.*

Doug read it. He didn't respond. He put the phone on the passenger seat, put the car in reverse, and drove home. The route was twelve minutes. He'd timed it once, four or five years ago, and it had been twelve minutes every day since, give or take whatever the light at Indianola and North Broadway decided to do, which was either a coincidence or the kind of consistency that a city offered when you weren't asking it for anything unusual.

The apartment was dark when he walked in. He turned on the kitchen light. The cereal box was on the counter where he'd left it that morning, the top still open, and he poured a bowl and ate standing up. The Cheerios tasted the way they always tasted. The milk was getting close to its sell-by date but was still fine. He ate and thought about the roses — all seventeen of them, and the woman who had documentation for every one, and the gap between what the policies said and what had actually happened, and how sometimes the most interesting problem was the one that didn't have a clean answer, just two incomplete answers sitting next to each other.

He rinsed the bowl and set it in the sink next to the bowl from yesterday. The apartment was quiet in the way it was always quiet — not peaceful, just empty, the specific silence of a space that had room for more than one person and contained only one. The second bedroom door was closed, the way it was always closed. Behind it were the boxes he hadn't unpacked when he'd moved in twelve years ago, and behind the boxes was a room that was listed on his lease as an office but contained no desk and no computer and served no function except to store the evidence that he had once lived somewhere else.

He didn't turn on the television. He sat on the couch and opened the Burris file he'd brought home and read the policy language again from the beginning, Section I through the endorsements, and the sentences were precise and the logic was clear and for an hour, sitting in his apartment with the cereal bowl in the sink and the phone on the counter and the day behind him like a door he'd walked through and closed, the work was the steadiest thing he had. It was not everything. But it was enough.


# Chapter 4

She had called four times before Doug answered. Three times he'd watched the number glow on his phone — a 917 area code, New York — and let it ring through to the voicemail he had never set up. The fourth time she left a message with the front desk at Mutual Fidelity, and Janet had walked the pink message slip to Doug's desk with a look that said she had questions she was choosing not to ask.

"A Layla Banks called," Janet said. "She says she's a talent manager. She says it's urgent." Janet placed the slip on Doug's keyboard with the care of someone delivering evidence. "She called on the main line, Doug."

"Thank you," Doug said.

"The main line," Janet said again, and went back to her office.

Doug called back that evening from his apartment, standing in the kitchen with a bowl of Grape-Nuts going soft on the counter. He didn't know why he chose that moment. Maybe because the cereal was already ruined and the evening had nowhere else to go.

She picked up before the first ring finished.

"Doug. Doug Bridge. Oh my god, finally. Okay, hi. Hi. I'm Layla Banks, I run Banks Management, and I — okay, first of all, I've watched your video probably thirty times, which I know sounds insane, but I'm going to be really honest with you because I think that's what you respond to based on, you know, the whole toast thing. I think you're sitting on something massive. I'm not talking about the video. I mean, yes, the video, but the video is the door, not the room. The room is — okay, can I just — are you free Thursday? Or Friday. I can fly out tomorrow."

"Fly out where?" Doug said.

"To you. Columbus. I want to sit down with you and talk about what's possible here because what's possible here is — I don't want to do this on the phone. Can I come to you?"

"You want to come to Columbus?"

"I'm looking at flights right now. I can get there Thursday afternoon. Is there somewhere we can meet? Your office, or — actually, I'd rather do it somewhere relaxed. Your place? Is that weird? That's probably weird. A coffee shop. Or your place, whatever's easier."

Doug looked at his apartment. The Ikea bookshelf with the shelf that sagged on the left side. The kitchen table with three chairs because the fourth had broken in the move and he'd never replaced it. The art print from the Columbus gallery that Megan had left behind, which he'd never taken down because the wall behind it was a slightly different shade of white.

"My place is fine," he said, because a coffee shop seemed worse.

"Amazing. Okay. Thursday. Text me the address. This is going to be so good, Doug. I'm — you have no idea. Okay. Thursday."

She hung up. Doug picked up his cereal. The Grape-Nuts had achieved the consistency of damp gravel. He ate them anyway, standing at the counter, because it was seven-thirty on a Tuesday in February and a woman in New York had just booked a flight to come see him, and the Grape-Nuts were the only thing in the apartment that hadn't changed.

---

Layla Banks arrived on Thursday at 4:20 p.m. She was smaller than her voice. Five-three, maybe five-four, in boots that added two inches but didn't change the fundamental impression, which was of someone who moved through space as if she were late for something better. She wore a black leather jacket that looked expensive over a white shirt that looked deliberate, and she carried a bag — not a purse, a bag, the kind with structure and intention — from which she produced, in the first four minutes of being in Doug's apartment, her phone, a laptop, a legal pad, two pens, and a folder with a printed logo that said BANKS MGMT in a font Doug associated with dentist offices.

She shook his hand. Her grip was firm and practiced. She smelled like something — not perfume, something sharper, something that had a name Doug would never know.

"Okay," she said, and she was already past him, already in the living room, scanning. Doug watched her eyes move across his apartment the way he watched a claims inspector move through a damaged house — cataloguing, assessing, determining what was worth what. The bookshelf. The table with three chairs. The print on the wall. The coat hooks by the door with one coat and one jacket, both brown. The kitchen visible through the pass-through, the counter with the cereal box he'd forgotten to put away.

"I love this," Layla said, meaning the apartment, or meaning she was ready to start.

She sat on his couch — the one he'd bought at a Cort furniture clearance eight years ago — and set her phone face-down on the coffee table. She picked it up within ten seconds, checked something, set it down again. This happened three more times before Doug sat in the armchair across from her.

"So," Layla said. "Let me tell you what I'm seeing."

What Layla was seeing, as it turned out, was everything. She talked for twenty-two minutes. Doug knew this because he'd glanced at the microwave clock when she started and glanced again when she paused for the first time, and the difference was twenty-two minutes. In that time, she had outlined what she called "the landscape of opportunity," and the landscape was vast and detailed and apparently ready for immediate construction.

There was the Netflix special. "I've already had preliminary conversations with a development exec there — Charlie Falk, he's great, he totally gets it. They want to meet you. They're thinking a one-hour special, shot somewhere real, not a studio, somewhere that feels like you. Your apartment, maybe. Or not your apartment. We'll figure out the venue."

There was the book deal. "Three publishers have already reached out to me — well, to you, technically, but they can't find you because you don't have representation, which is why you need representation, which is why I'm here. We're talking a humor book, or a memoir, or a humor-memoir hybrid. The category's flexible. The advance would be — I don't want to throw numbers around prematurely because nothing's real until it's real, but we're talking probably mid-six figures. Maybe more. The market for this right now is insane."

There was the TED talk. "They love regular people with a moment. You're the definition of a moment. We'd position it around, like — I don't know — vulnerability? Honesty? The comedy of being real? We'd figure out the framing. But TED is a door that opens other doors."

There was the corporate speaking circuit. "This is where the money actually is. Pharmaceutical companies, insurance companies — oh my god, you literally work in insurance, that's perfect — tech companies, sales conferences, leadership retreats. They pay between fifteen and forty thousand dollars for a keynote, and you'd be doing, I don't know, two or three a month at first. You don't have to be slick. They don't want slick. They want the toast guy."

There was the CBD endorsement.

"Just hear me out," Layla said, raising both hands.

"I don't know what that is," Doug said. It was the first complete sentence he'd managed in twenty-two minutes.

"It's — okay, it's a wellness brand. They do gummies and tinctures and — the point is, they're looking for a spokesperson who's real, who's relatable, and they specifically asked about you. The money is — it's not huge, maybe twenty or thirty thousand, but it's easy money. You hold a bottle, you say something about relaxing, they put it on Instagram."

"I don't have Instagram."

"We'll get you Instagram. That's — honestly, that's a whole other conversation. Your social media is — I mean, you don't have social media, which is actually kind of amazing as a branding angle but it's a nightmare operationally, so we'll deal with that."

There was also, possibly, a podcast.

"Everyone has a podcast," Doug said.

"Exactly! And none of them are interesting! Yours would be interesting because you're not trying to be interesting, you're just —" She gestured at his apartment, at him, at the space between them, encompassing everything. "This."

Doug sat in the armchair. His hands were on the armrests. The upholstery had a texture like corduroy but wasn't corduroy — Megan had told him the name of the fabric once, years before the clearance sale, when they were still people who bought furniture together, and he'd forgotten the word but remembered the conversation, which was the kind of thing his memory did to him.

"Can I ask a question?" Doug said.

"Absolutely. Yes. Ask me anything."

"Who are your other clients?"

Layla's phone buzzed on the coffee table. She flipped it face-down without looking.

"So, Banks Management is a boutique firm. We're intentionally small because I believe in focused attention and — I have a roster of about twelve clients right now. A few podcast hosts, some branded content creators, and Derek Voss — do you know Derek? He was on The Bachelorette, season — I want to say fourteen? Fifteen? He's doing really well. He has a fitness brand and a supplement line."

"I don't watch The Bachelorette."

"That's fine. That's not the point. The point is, I know this space. I know how to take a moment — what you have right now is a moment — and turn it into a career. Not a flash-in-the-pan thing, not a meme lifecycle. An actual career."

She leaned forward. Her hands were on her knees. Her nails were short and painted a color that Doug's mother would have called "unserious." A silver ring on her right hand, not her left — thin, worn to a smooth shine, the kind of ring you didn't take off.

"Doug. People are going to come for you. They're already coming. Producers, publishers, brands, every talent manager in New York and LA who watched that video and did the math the same way I did. The question isn't whether someone's going to help you navigate this. The question is whether you have someone in your corner who understands how this works, or whether you're sitting here answering calls from people who don't have your best interest at heart."

She paused. Her voice had dropped half a register, and Doug couldn't tell if this was strategy or something closer to honest.

"I have your best interest at heart. I also have my own best interest at heart, and I'm going to be straight about that because I think you can tell when someone's bullshitting you."

"Usually," Doug said.

"I think you'd be great for my company and I think I'd be great for you. I think in six months we could be looking at a — I don't want to say a number, but it would be a number that would change what your year looks like. What your next few years look like." She stopped herself, recalculated. "What do you make now? If you don't mind."

"Sixty-one thousand, four hundred," Doug said. "Before taxes."

Layla absorbed this number. Something happened in her face that she recovered from quickly, a microexpression that Doug recognized from claims work — it was the look adjusters got when a homeowner revealed the full extent of the damage and it was less than the adjuster expected. Not disappointment, exactly. Recalibration.

"Right," she said. "Okay. So."

"So," Doug said.

She picked up her phone, set it down, picked it up, set it down. The fourth time, she left it in her hand.

"Can I be really honest with you?"

"You said you were going to be."

"Yeah, I — okay. Here's what I think. I think you could make more money in the next six months than you've made in the last five years. I think the Netflix deal alone could be — I really don't want to say numbers until something's signed. But significant. And the corporates, the book, the — look, I know this is a lot. I know I've been sitting in your apartment for twenty minutes telling you everything's about to change, and you're probably thinking, who is this person and why should I trust her."

Doug wasn't thinking that, exactly. He was thinking about the number five years, which at his salary was roughly three hundred and seven thousand dollars, and then he was thinking about the microwave clock and whether she'd notice if he looked at it again, and then he was not thinking about either of those things because he was thinking about the way Layla Banks had said his name on the phone — "Doug. Doug Bridge" — with a certainty that implied she'd been saying it to herself for days, practicing, the way you'd practice saying a name that mattered to you.

"Okay," Doug said.

"Okay what?"

"I'm interested."

Layla's whole body changed. She sat up straighter. She smiled with her whole face, not just the professional part, and Doug could see both the strategy and the genuine thing underneath it, and it didn't matter, because the genuine thing was real even if it was also strategic.

"The CBD thing," Doug said. "I'm not sure about the CBD thing."

"Forget the CBD thing. The CBD thing is — we'll park it. That's totally separate. The real stuff is Netflix, the book, and corporates. That's the core. That's the foundation we build on."

"And the TED talk."

"And the TED talk, eventually. TED moves slow. TED is a longer play."

"And the podcast."

"Okay, the podcast is — honestly, the podcast is just an idea I had on the flight. It might not — we'd figure out the podcast later. The podcast is phase two."

"How many phases are there?"

"There aren't official phases. I'm using 'phases' loosely. The point is, we'd start with the big-ticket items and build from there. You don't have to say yes to everything tonight. You just have to say yes to me."

Doug looked at her. She was leaning forward on his couch, phone in hand, the folder with BANKS MGMT on her lap, and she had the expression of someone waiting for something she'd been waiting for longer than this conversation. It occurred to Doug, in the way things occurred to him — gradually, factually, without drama — that he might not be the only person in the room being offered something they wanted. Layla Banks ran a twelve-person management company out of New York. Her biggest client had been on a reality dating show three or four seasons ago. She was sitting in a two-bedroom apartment in Clintonville on a Thursday afternoon, on a couch that cost four hundred dollars and smelled faintly of its previous home.

She needed this too. Maybe more than he did.

"Okay," Doug said. "Yes."

"Yes?"

"Yes."

"Okay! Oh my god, okay." She was already opening the folder, unclipping something, sliding pages across the coffee table. "I have the paperwork. It's standard representation. I brought it because I was hopeful, but I also didn't want to fly home and have to FedEx it, so —"

She placed the contract in front of him. It was eleven pages, held together with a black binder clip.

---

Doug picked up the contract and read it.

This was not what Layla expected. He could tell because she shifted on the couch three times in the first minute, crossed and uncrossed her legs, and picked up her phone twice without looking at it — a new behavior. Previously she'd at least checked the screen. By page three, she was watching him with the expression he'd seen on homeowners when he sat in their living rooms and read their policies for forty-five minutes: respect cut with irritation, the irritation winning.

"It's pretty standard," she said, when he was on page four.

"Mmhm," Doug said.

He read the way he read everything — every word, every clause, every defined term tracked back to its first appearance. This was the fundamental skill of his profession, the competence on which his career rested: the ability to read a document the way most people refused to. Doug had processed over four thousand claims in his time at Mutual Fidelity. He had read policies, endorsements, riders, amendments, proofs of loss, contractor estimates, and enough subrogation correspondence to fill the second bedroom twice over, which was saying something because the second bedroom was already full. He did not skim. He did not flip to the signature page. He read.

The contract was with Banks Management LLC, a New York limited liability company. It established a management relationship granting Layla Banks exclusive representation rights for all entertainment, media, publishing, and endorsement opportunities. The commission structure was twenty percent of gross revenue on entertainment and media, fifteen percent on publishing, and twenty-five percent on endorsements. The term was two years with automatic renewal unless terminated by either party with sixty days' written notice. The exclusivity clause was broad. The termination provisions were narrow.

"Page six," Doug said. "The indemnification clause."

"What about it?"

"It says I indemnify you against any claims arising from my public appearances. That's one-directional. If you book me somewhere and the venue has a liability issue, or if there's a contractual dispute with a third party you introduced, I'm the one carrying the exposure."

Layla blinked. "That's — I mean, that's standard language. Every management contract —"

"It's standard because most people don't read it. Can we make it mutual?"

"Mutual?"

"If I'm indemnifying you, you indemnify me. Same language, reversed. My exposure shouldn't be asymmetric."

Layla looked at him for a long moment. Then she pulled out one of her pens and wrote something on her legal pad. "Okay. Sure. We can do mutual indemnification. What else?"

"Page eight. The revenue definition. It says 'gross revenue from all professional activities entered into during the term of this agreement.' All professional activities."

"Right."

"Does that include my job?"

"Your — at the insurance company?"

"At Mutual Fidelity. If I keep working there — and I'm going to keep working there — this language could be read to include my salary as a professional activity entered into during the term. You'd be entitled to twenty percent of sixty-one thousand, four hundred dollars."

Layla opened her mouth, closed it, and tilted her head at him the way people did when they were reconsidering something they'd already decided.

"I would never — that's not the intent, Doug."

"The intent isn't in the contract. The language is in the contract. Can we add an exclusion for pre-existing employment income?"

She wrote on the legal pad again. Faster this time. "Absolutely. We'll carve that out. What else?"

Doug turned to page nine. The apartment was quiet except for the refrigerator, which had a hum that Doug had stopped hearing three years ago but which reasserted itself in silences like this one. Layla sat on the couch. She was not checking her phone. This was, Doug would later realize, the longest he ever saw Layla Banks sit still.

On page nine he found the termination clause. Either party could terminate with sixty days' written notice, but termination did not end the commission obligation — Layla would continue to receive her percentage on all deals initiated during the term, regardless of when revenue was received. Doug recognized this structure. He'd seen it in commercial insurance brokerage agreements: a trailing commission designed to protect the broker after the relationship ended. It was reasonable in principle. In application, it could go on forever.

"The trailing commission," Doug said. "After termination, you receive your percentage on deals negotiated during the term. For how long?"

"For the life of the deal."

"So if you negotiate a book contract in month one and I terminate in month three, you receive fifteen percent of that book's earnings for as long as the book earns?"

"That's how these work."

"I believe you. I'd like to cap it at twenty-four months post-termination."

"Doug."

"It's a trailing commission on a management contract, not a life estate."

Layla stared at him. Something shifted in her expression — not the microexpression from earlier, when he'd told her his salary, but something slower and more deliberate. She bit the inside of her cheek, and Doug could see the muscle working along her jaw. She was doing math, or she was deciding how much she wanted this.

"Thirty-six months," she said.

"Twenty-four."

"Thirty. Final."

Doug considered this. Thirty months was reasonable. It gave Layla protection on deals she'd invested time in while giving him a defined exit. He'd seen worse terms in commercial brokerage. He'd also seen better, but those were negotiated by attorneys billing four hundred dollars an hour, not by insurance adjusters sitting in armchairs on a Thursday evening with cold cereal on the counter.

"Thirty months," Doug said. "With the mutual indemnification and the employment income carve-out."

"Done."

She wrote it down. Her handwriting was fast and angular, the letters connected by strokes that were more intention than ink. She looked up.

"Anything on ten and eleven?"

Doug read pages ten and eleven. Page ten was a standard confidentiality provision — mutual, limited to proprietary business information, with a two-year survival period. Reasonable. Page eleven was signature blocks and a choice-of-law clause specifying New York.

"The governing law is New York," Doug said.

"That's where the company's organized."

"I'm in Ohio."

"Right, but it's — the contract is with a New York LLC. Every one of my agreements uses New York law."

Doug looked at the clause. In insurance, choice-of-law provisions could determine the outcome of a coverage dispute. Which state's regulations applied, which courts had jurisdiction, what standards governed interpretation — these were real decisions with real consequences. But this was a management contract, not a commercial liability policy, and the practical difference between Ohio and New York contract law for a document this size was marginal. He was not going to fight for this one.

"Fine," he said. "The rest is fine."

Layla let out a breath she'd been holding since page six. She put her pen down and looked at him the way Roy looked at a claim file when the documentation was surprisingly complete — something that lived between admiration and the discomfort of having underestimated someone.

"You know," she said, "most of my clients sign these in about thirty seconds."

"Most of your clients are podcast hosts."

She laughed. It came out sudden and unguarded, and for a second she was not a pitch or a strategy or a leather jacket from New York — she was a thirty-eight-year-old woman sitting on a bad couch in an apartment in Ohio, laughing because a man had said something true without meaning it as a joke. The laugh had a rough edge to it, like she'd surprised herself. She caught it, tucked it away, and was Layla Banks again by the time she reached for her bag.

"I'll have my attorney redline these three changes and send you a clean copy by Monday," she said, packing up the laptop, the legal pad, the pens. Her phone was in her hand before the bag was closed, her thumb already moving across the screen. The stillness was over. She was back in motion. "Once you sign, I'll set up the Netflix meeting. Charlie's been waiting. The book publishers can wait another week but not much longer because the window on this kind of opportunity is — it's not forever. The interest is real but interest decays. We need to move."

"How fast?"

"Fast. Not reckless fast. Strategic fast."

She pulled on her jacket. Doug stood up. They were in his living room, which had never felt particularly small until someone with Layla's velocity was in it. She took up more space than her body accounted for, the way a sound fills a room regardless of its source.

"I'm staying at the Hilton downtown," she said. "I fly back tomorrow afternoon. If you think of anything between now and Monday, call me. I answer my phone."

"I know," Doug said. "You picked up before the first ring."

"Yeah, I — I was waiting for you to call back. I'm going to be honest about that. I've been waiting for you to call for two weeks."

She said it without the pitch rhythm, without the forward lean. Just a sentence, flat and true, delivered at the door of his apartment while she adjusted the strap of her bag. Doug understood that this was one of the moments Layla Banks produced by accident — a sentence that slipped through because she was tired or because she'd been performing for ninety minutes and the performance had briefly exhausted itself. She had been waiting. Her small company, her twelve clients, her former Bachelorette contestant with a fitness brand — she had been checking her phone for two weeks, looking for a 614 area code, waiting for an insurance adjuster in Columbus to decide she was worth calling back.

"Thank you for coming out here," Doug said.

"Thank you for picking up the phone, babe. I'll talk to you Monday."

And she was through the door, and the leather jacket and the energy and the bag with the BANKS MGMT logo were gone, and the apartment returned to its normal dimensions. The refrigerator hummed. The bookshelf sagged on the left. The Grape-Nuts box sat on the counter where he'd left it that morning, and the bowl from Tuesday was in the sink, rinsed but not washed.

Doug sat back down in the armchair. The couch cushion where Layla had been still held the impression of someone sitting there. He looked at the coffee table where the contract had been — she'd taken it with her to send the redlined version. All that remained was a pen she'd left behind, black with a silver clip, the kind that came in packs of twelve from an office supply store. He picked it up and set it next to his water glass.

He was going to be on Netflix. He was going to write a book. He was going to stand on stages in front of people and say things, and those people were going to pay money for it — real money, money that existed in a different order of magnitude than his salary, which he had never thought of as small until ninety minutes ago when he'd said the number out loud and watched a woman's face recalibrate.

He tried to feel excited. He thought he should feel excited. What he felt instead was something he didn't have a good word for — a low hum in his chest, warm and persistent, like a furnace that had kicked on after being cold for a long time and hadn't yet reached temperature. It wasn't excitement and it wasn't fear. It was closer to the feeling he got when a complicated claim suddenly resolved — when the documentation aligned and the coverage was clear and the path from loss to payment became visible. Relief, maybe. Or recognition. The sense that something that should have been simple had been simple all along, and you'd just been looking at it wrong.

Except that wasn't right either, because what had happened tonight wasn't simple. A woman had come to his apartment and told him his life was about to change, and he had said yes, and the reason he'd said yes was not the money or the Netflix or the book deal or the TED talk or the corporate speaking circuit or the podcast that might not happen or the CBD endorsement he'd turned down. The reason was simpler than any of that, and more embarrassing, and he was going to think about it now because he was alone and the apartment was quiet and there was no one to perform an answer for.

He had not been wanted in twelve years.

Not by Megan, who had wanted him gone with a fairness that was its own kind of cruelty — she'd left him nothing to be angry at, and the absence of anger was worse than the absence of her. Not by Kelsey, who wanted something from him he'd never figured out how to provide — not money or time but a version of himself that could say things without being drunk, that could be present without retreating into the specific details of whatever room he was in. Not by Mutual Fidelity, which wanted his output and his compliance and would replace him with a two-week posting on Indeed and a three-day handoff of his active files. Roy wanted him the way you wanted your lunch order — you'd miss it if it weren't there, but you wouldn't rearrange your day.

Layla Banks, on his couch, phone in hand, pitch in her mouth, had wanted him. For her own reasons, for her commission, for her roster, for whatever a man with eighty million views was worth to a twelve-person management company that needed a break — she had wanted him. And the transparency of it hadn't diminished the feeling. She'd told him he was a product and she intended to sell him, and she'd told him this while looking him in the eye and calling him babe, and the ugly, undeniable truth was that being wanted as a product was better than not being wanted at all. It was better by a margin so large that Doug couldn't look at it directly, the way you couldn't look at the full depreciation schedule on a house you'd lived in for twenty years — the math was correct and the conclusion was devastating and you'd rather just keep paying the premium.

He'd won two points in the contract. The mutual indemnification and the employment income carve-out. Good points. Specific, defensible, correctly identified. He had lost the commission percentages, the exclusivity, the trailing commission term — thirty months instead of twenty-four — the automatic renewal, the choice of law, and every other provision that determined the actual trajectory of his professional life for the next two years and beyond.

He'd won the small ones. He'd lost every large one. This was, he thought, a pattern he should examine more carefully, and he was not going to.

Doug got up. He went to the kitchen. He poured a bowl of Grape-Nuts and stood at the counter and ate them, and the cereal was dry and crunchy and familiar, and his life was different now in a way he could not yet feel in his body but could see if he looked — the way you could see a waterline on a basement wall after the water receded, a faint brown mark that showed exactly where the damage reached, even after everything dried out and the claim was closed and the check was cashed and you went on living in the house as if nothing had happened.


# Chapter 5

The diner was on High Street in Worthington, a place Doug had driven past a hundred times without forming an opinion about it. Kelsey had picked it — neutral ground, she'd said, which implied that Doug's apartment and her apartment and every restaurant they'd ever been to together were charged territory now, places where the toast might follow them in. He'd agreed because he agreed with most things Kelsey suggested, and because the last time he'd disagreed with her about a restaurant she'd been fourteen and the argument had been about Applebee's and he'd lost.

He arrived twenty minutes early. He always arrived twenty minutes early — a claims habit, left over from years of showing up first to assess the site, note the conditions before anyone else's presence altered them. The conditions here were a half-full diner with vinyl booths, a specials board in faded chalk that listed a turkey melt and a soup that was either cream of mushroom or cream of chicken depending on how you read the handwriting, and a draft coming under the front door that carried the smell of road salt and wet asphalt. February in Columbus. Not brutal, just relentless — a cold that didn't argue with you, just waited.

He chose a booth near the back, away from the window, which turned out poorly because the kitchen door swung open every thirty seconds and he could hear the fryer pop and plates being stacked and a cook saying something muffled and laughing at it alone. He ordered coffee. The waitress was a woman around his age, heavyset, with a brace on her left wrist and a pen tucked behind each ear — both blue, like she'd been burned once by running out of ink and had decided it wasn't going to happen again. She brought the coffee in a mug that said OHIO IS FOR LOVERS, which was wrong about Ohio in at least two ways Doug could think of.

He wrapped his hands around the mug. The heat was good. He sat there for eleven minutes watching the front door and thinking about what he was going to say and not arriving at anything that worked, which was how most of his important conversations began.

Kelsey came in at seven past noon. She was wearing a coat he didn't recognize — dark green, longer than what she usually wore, the collar up high on her neck — and her hair was pulled back tight, the no-nonsense configuration she wore at work, a woman who spent her days leaning over open mouths. She spotted him and didn't wave. She walked to the booth, sat down across from him, put her phone face-down on the table, and said, "I left at nine. The drive was shit."

"Road work?"

"No, the road was fine. The drive was shit because I spent two hours thinking about what I'm supposed to say to you and I still don't have it."

Doug nodded. He'd spent the morning doing roughly the same thing, except his commute from Clintonville was twelve minutes, and he'd filled the remaining time rearranging the salt and pepper shakers on his kitchen counter as if their alignment might produce a script for this conversation.

The waitress came back. Kelsey ordered a club sandwich and a Diet Coke without looking at the menu. Doug ordered a BLT because he'd been staring at the specials for eleven minutes and the BLT was the only item that didn't require a decision about sides or bread type. The waitress wrote it down left-handed, the brace adjusting her grip slightly off-angle, and Doug noticed that the brace looked new and clean, which meant the injury was recent, which meant she was still figuring out the small recalibrations — the grip and reach and angle — that came with working around a part of your body that had stopped doing what it used to do.

"I'm sorry," Doug said, when they were alone.

"Okay."

"I mean it."

"I know you mean it, Dad. You've been sorry since the day after. Sorry isn't really the question."

She picked up a sugar packet from the caddy and turned it over between her fingers — front, back, front — not opening it, just giving her hands something to do. He recognized this. She'd done it with erasers as a kid, her phone case as a teenager. Kelsey's anger always went to her hands first.

"What's the question?" he said.

"I don't know yet. That's what I drove two hours to figure out." She dropped the sugar packet. "Can I just tell you what it's been like? The last two weeks. Can I just do that?"

"Yeah."

"Twenty-three hundred," she said. "Tags. Not mentions — tags. People tagging me in posts about your video. People I went to high school with. People I've never met. My coworker Jen — you've met Jen, short, has the bangs — she screenshot a TikTok reaction video, some guy doing commentary over the toast, and she put it in the work group chat. With a heart emoji. She thought she was being supportive."

"That's —"

"I'm not done." Her voice didn't rise. It dropped, and the drop was worse. "A patient. I had a patient in the chair, bib clipped on, I'm in there with the scaler doing a subgingival, and she goes, 'Oh my God, are you Wedding Toast Girl?' My hand is in this woman's mouth. She's trying to talk around a scaler and she's telling me she cried, she thought it was so moving, she's actually tearing up while I've got calculus on my instrument and I'm just standing there with my hand in a stranger's mouth listening to her cry about my dad."

Doug looked at the table. There was a ring of dried coffee near his placemat, a faint circle, residue you'd document in a claims photograph if the surface were insured.

"Tyler's mother," Kelsey said. She'd gone quiet, and with Kelsey the quiet was always where the real pressure lived. Not the volume — the precision. "She sent me a text. Three sentences. 'We saw your father's speech. We understand.' She italicized 'understand.'"

"You can italicize in a —"

"Yes, Dad, you can italicize in a text message. That's not the point." She looked at him across the table with the particular steadiness she deployed when he did this — when he retreated into the small technical question because the larger one was sitting right there and he didn't know how to pick it up. "Tyler's mother. Who I sat next to at the rehearsal dinner for forty minutes while she told me about her bridge club and her hip replacement and her opinions on the asparagus. Who I've met four times. And she understands now. She understands our family. Because you got drunk and talked into a microphone about things you've never said to any of us sober."

The food arrived. The waitress set down the plates with her braced left hand, compensating slightly, the BLT landing a half-inch off the placemat. Doug thanked her. Kelsey didn't look up.

They ate. Or they went through the motions. Doug picked up a triangle of the BLT. The bacon was cold and rigid, one strip hanging past the bread like it was trying to leave the sandwich. He ate it anyway. The lettuce was brown at the edges. The tomato was the pale pink of February tomatoes, shipped from somewhere warmer and emptied of whatever they'd been by the trip.

Kelsey dismantled the club sandwich. She ate the turkey layer first. She'd been doing this since she was twelve, when she'd decided that sandwiches made more sense as components — disassembled and consumed in order of preference. Turkey first, then bacon, then bread and lettuce last, which she liked least. It was a detail Doug knew about his daughter, part of the private archive he'd accumulated over twenty-eight years: the turkey first, the way she gripped pens too tight, the scar on her left knee from a bike pedal in fourth grade, the fact that she always parked facing out because she'd read somewhere it was safer. He catalogued these things the way he catalogued the details of a claim file — thoroughly, precisely, without ever quite understanding what they added up to.

"How's work otherwise?" he said, because the silence between bites was getting heavy and he didn't know how to hold it yet.

"It's fine. It's teeth."

"Right."

She chewed. He chewed. The kitchen door swung. Somewhere behind it a plate broke and someone said "shit" and someone else laughed and the normal operations of a diner on a Wednesday in February continued without regard for what was happening in the back booth.

"Can I ask you something?" Kelsey said.

"Yeah."

"The thing you said about the table — was that real?"

Doug's hands went still on the sandwich. The table. She was asking about the table — the table in the kitchen of the house on Briarwood, the maple table they'd bought when Kelsey was four from a furniture store that no longer existed, a table with a gouge near one leg where the delivery men had caught the doorframe and a long pale scratch across the top from Kelsey's protractor in seventh grade. She'd been horrified about the scratch. He'd told her it was fine, it gave the table character. And in the toast, drunk and specific in the way he was always specific, he'd said something about that scratch — he couldn't remember the exact words, the eight whiskeys had turned the evening into a landscape seen through rain — but he remembered the word "protractor," because even eight drinks in he was the kind of man who named the instrument, and he remembered something about the scratch being the only lasting mark he'd made alongside anybody. Or close to that. Close enough that it was on the internet and his daughter was sitting across from him asking if it was real.

"I was pretty far gone," he said.

"That's not what I asked."

"I know."

"Was it real? What you said about the scratch. The thing about — you know what you said."

Doug looked at the salt shaker. It was glass with a chrome lid, and the salt inside had clumped at the bottom from humidity, a solid mass where the granules had fused into something denser than they were meant to be. He focused on the salt and not on the table and not on the scratch and not on the house on Briarwood that Megan had sold fourteen months after she left, to a couple from Westerville who didn't know about the protractor, who would sand the mark smooth or cover it with a placemat or not notice it at all.

"I don't remember exactly what I said," Doug told her. This was a lie. It was the specific lie of a man who remembered precisely what he'd said and could not say it again sober because saying it sober would mean he'd chosen to, and choosing was different from the words walking out of you at a wedding reception while the room was still recovering from your ex-wife's husband's perfect toast. Choosing meant it was yours. Drunk, it was an accident. He could live with an accident.

Kelsey watched him. Her eyes were her mother's — dark, level, capable of holding a gaze longer than was comfortable as a form of pressure all its own. She knew he was deflecting. She had spent a lifetime watching him deflect. She'd learned to read his retreats into literalness the way you learn to read the weather — not by what it shows you but by what it's deciding not to show.

She set down the piece of bread she was holding.

She folded her hands on the table.

And then neither of them spoke.

The diner went on around them. The kitchen door swung and the fryer crackled. Two booths over, a couple in their sixties argued about a nephew — the word "irresponsible" recurring at intervals like a metronome. The waitress passed with a pot of coffee and refilled Doug's mug without asking, which he registered as both a kindness and an intrusion but couldn't manage to say anything about either way. At the counter, a man was eating soup and reading his phone, and the sound of his spoon against the bowl was steady and small and regular, measuring time in cream of mushroom. The front door opened and cold air rushed in and someone said "Jesus, it's cold" and someone else answered "February" as if that were an explanation, which in Columbus it was.

A song played on the diner's radio — something country, a truck and a dirt road, a woman who left. Then Fleetwood Mac, "Landslide," which was so on-the-nose it had to be coincidence. Then a commercial for a dealership in Delaware, Ohio, zero percent financing, which Doug happened to know meant the interest was folded into the sticker price — useful information for no one in this booth at this moment.

Five minutes. Maybe six. He didn't look at his phone and neither did she.

It was the most honest they'd been with each other in years, and neither of them said a word during it. The toast had said things — too many things, wrong-venue things, true things that belonged in a kitchen or a car or a phone call at midnight, not in a banquet room with two hundred people and somebody's phone pointed at the podium. And the toast was on the internet now, where strangers scored it and shared it and wept about it in dental chairs. But this silence was just theirs. It didn't perform. It didn't ask for a reaction. It was Doug sitting across from his daughter and his daughter sitting across from him and both of them knowing what the scratch on the table meant without either of them needing to confirm it, because his deflection was the confirmation — if he hadn't meant it, he'd have just said no, and they both knew that, and the knowing was the conversation they were actually having.

Kelsey picked up her sandwich again. She ate the bread — the layer she liked least — steadily, without pleasure. She finished her Diet Coke. The ice clinked against the empty glass.

"Tyler says hi," she said.

"Tell him hi."

"He liked the toast. He thought it was funny."

"Tyler did?"

"He laughed. I don't know at which part. I didn't take a survey." She shrugged. She was coming back to the surface, reassembling the normal shapes of conversation around herself. "Maybe it's funny if you're not in it."

"Maybe."

"Anyway." She wadded her napkin and dropped it on her plate. "I should go. I told Ty I'd be home by five."

"Drive safe."

"I always drive safe."

"I know you do."

She stood. She pulled on the green coat, zipped it to her chin. She was shorter than he expected — she was always shorter than he expected, because he carried in his mind a Kelsey who had kept growing past five-four, which she hadn't, same height as Megan, same height as Megan's mother, a genetic line that had nothing to do with anything and that he thought about now because thinking about his daughter's height was easier than thinking about the silence that was still sitting in the booth between them.

"The BLT was pretty good," he said.

Kelsey looked at him. And then she laughed — short, barely a sound, escaping before she'd decided whether to allow it. "God, Dad."

"What?"

"Nothing. The BLT was pretty good." She shook her head. She put a twenty on the table and when he reached for his wallet she said "Don't" in a voice that closed the discussion. Then she walked out of the diner without looking back.

He watched her through the window, crossing the parking lot in the dark green coat. She got into a silver Civic — she drove a Civic too, a newer model than his, because she'd replaced the one he'd given her at graduation with the same make and he didn't know whether that was loyalty or habit or the simple fact that Civics held their resale value, and he preferred to think about resale values rather than about the silence and the table and the five minutes during which his daughter had sat across from him knowing everything he couldn't say and letting the not-saying be enough.

The waitress brought the check. $23.40. He left the twenty Kelsey had put down and added twelve of his own, because the waitress had the brace and the two pens and had refilled his coffee without asking and he didn't know what else he could do for anyone today, so he could at least do this.

---

He called Megan at 4:30. He was sitting on the couch in the dark. He hadn't turned on the lights when he got home, and the February afternoon had already given out — the transition from gray to black so gradual it didn't register as a change, more like the gray just thickening until it was opaque. His phone was at twenty-two percent. He should have charged it first, but the charger was in the kitchen and the cord was too short to reach anywhere comfortable, and he'd spent the two hours since Worthington sitting on this couch building up to this call, and if he moved he might lose whatever fragile momentum he'd managed.

She picked up on the second ring. "Doug."

"Hey."

"Hi." Behind her, a sound — a cabinet closing, something being set down carefully. She was in her kitchen. Brendan's kitchen. The one with the island and the six-burner range and the herb pots on the windowsill in labeled ceramic containers, because in Brendan's house even the basil had a system. "I had a feeling you'd call."

"Yeah. I wanted to apologize. For the toast. What I said about — about us."

"I appreciate you saying that." She said it in her important-conversation voice — clear, measured, each word given its full weight and proper spacing. "I want you to know I'm not angry. I've thought about this a lot over the past two weeks, and I think it's important we contextualize what happened."

Doug pressed his thumbnail into the arm of the couch. The upholstery had pilled in the places where his hands rested — eight years of the same position on the same cushion producing small hard knots of polyester fiber. He focused on one, about the size of a lentil.

"Contextualize," he repeated, not because the word was unfamiliar but because it was so perfectly Megan — the impulse to frame, to organize, to give the mess a structure that made it manageable. She contextualized the way he processed claims. They'd been married eighteen years and divorced for twelve and they still couldn't just say the thing.

"The toast happened under specific conditions," Megan said. "You'd been drinking. You were emotional. And Brendan's toast —" She stopped. This was notable. Megan did not stop. Megan assembled sentences in advance and delivered them whole, like prepared statements, and a stop meant she'd reached something the preparation hadn't accounted for. "Brendan's toast was very moving. I think that may have created an emotional charge that contributed to yours."

"You're saying his toast set me off."

"I'm saying it may have been a factor. I'm not assigning blame."

"It sounded like you were assigning a factor, which is — yeah."

"Doug."

"Sorry. Go ahead."

"I've thought about what you said. About our marriage. And I think what's important is that we don't let the video become a new narrative. We've both done a lot of work to get to a healthy place, and I don't want one night to —"

"You've done a lot of work."

"We've both done work. In our own ways." The patience in her voice was genuine, and that was the problem. He could hear it — not performed, not therapeutic-by-rote, but real patience, the patience of a woman who had once loved him and now cared about him from a careful distance and was trying, in good faith, to handle this well. He could have pushed against anger. Against coldness. Megan's patience was warm and thorough and left him nothing to push against at all.

"How's Brendan?" he heard himself ask, which was not what he'd intended to say and not a question he wanted answered.

"He's good. He's at the hospital tonight." A pause — this one deliberate, shaped. Megan's silences were never accidents; they were load-bearing structures in her conversations, placed where she needed them. "He watched the video, Doug. I want to tell you that because I think you should hear it."

"Everyone watched the video. That's sort of the whole situation."

"He watched it several times." Her voice shifted slightly, the uptick that meant she was delivering something she'd decided he needed to receive. "He said it was brave."

The word arrived in Doug's dark apartment and just sat there.

Brave.

Brendan Coyle — orthopedic surgeon, weekend triathlete, a man whose own toast at the same wedding had been polished and warm and had brought the room to tears at precisely the right moment because Brendan understood pacing the way Doug understood depreciation schedules — thought that Doug's drunken seven-minute catastrophe was brave.

Doug pressed the fabric pill flat with his thumbnail. It sprang back.

"He said he thought it took real courage to be that honest in front of the people you love," Megan continued. "I'm telling you because I think it's a generous reading and I want you to hear it from me."

Doug tried to locate what he felt. It wasn't one thing — it was a stack, multiple items on the same claim, each requiring separate assessment. Gratitude, because Brendan was being kind and the kindness was real. Humiliation, because the kindness came from the man who had his wife and his house and his composure and his herb pots. And something else underneath those, harder to file — the specific weight of being admired by the person whose life was the clearest evidence of everything you'd lost. Brave implied he'd chosen to do it. Brave implied he'd stood up at that podium and decided, sober and deliberate, to tell the truth. He hadn't chosen anything. He'd been eight whiskeys past the last checkpoint and the truth had walked out on its own, and calling that brave was like complimenting a burst pipe for the water pressure.

"That's a nice thing for him to say," Doug managed.

"It is."

"Tell him I said thanks."

"I will."

A silence settled between them — shorter than the one at the diner, thinner, managed. A Megan silence: placed, purposeful, the conversational equivalent of a clean counter.

"How are you doing with all of this?" she asked. "Honestly?"

"I'm fine."

"Doug."

"I signed with a manager. A woman in New York. She's — handling the offers."

"What offers?"

"Netflix thing. Speaking. Possibly a book." He heard himself listing them the way he'd list water damage on a claim form. One item, then the next. Factual. Sequential. No commentary.

"That's — wow." Megan paused. "Is that what you want?"

"I don't know."

"I just want to make sure you're making decisions from a clear —"

"Yeah."

"I'm not trying to tell you what to do."

"I know."

"I want to be clear about that."

"You're clear. You're always clear, Megan."

She was quiet. He could hear her deciding whether to say more. Deciding not to. Megan's restraint was never the absence of something to say — it was the active choice to hold it, the way you'd contain a slow leak before it reached the subfloor.

"Take care of yourself," she said.

"Yeah. You too."

He hung up. The apartment was fully dark. His phone said 4:53, the screen glowing in the room like something left on by mistake. He got up and plugged it into the kitchen charger and stood there at the counter while the little lightning bolt appeared on the screen, and the apartment was quiet in the way it was always quiet — not peaceful, not hostile, just the sound of a space with one person in it and room for more.

He opened the refrigerator. Milk. Condiments. Half a sleeve of saltines from a stomach bug in January. One egg that had rolled to the far back corner of the shelf and sat there under the light, small and solitary, like something waiting to be assigned a purpose.

He closed the refrigerator. He wasn't hungry. He'd eaten a BLT in Worthington and his daughter had driven two hours to sit across from him and tell him that his drunken honesty had turned her into a punchline and a pity case and a dental-chair anecdote, and then they'd sat in silence for five minutes and it was the closest they'd been since she was a kid doing homework at a table that didn't exist anymore.

His ex-wife's husband thought he was brave. His daughter knew the truth without him confirming it. Tyler's mother understood. Eighty million people had opinions.

Doug walked to the bedroom. He took off his shoes and placed them by the door — left, right, aligned with the baseboard, because he was a man who put things in order even when nothing else was — and lay down on the bed in his clothes. The ceiling had a water stain directly above him, a ring of discoloration from the tenant upstairs, something he'd noticed when he moved in twelve years ago and had never reported. He knew what caused it — a slow leak, probably a supply line fitting, a failure that cost maybe eighty dollars to fix and three hundred in drywall if you waited. He'd waited twelve years. The stain was roughly the shape of Ohio, which was either fitting or coincidence, and he went with coincidence, because the alternative — that his ceiling had developed opinions about his life — was a thing that happened in movies, not in two-bedroom apartments in Clintonville.

He lay there. He didn't sleep. Outside, the February cold sat on the city and didn't move. Somewhere on I-71 North, or already home in Cleveland, Kelsey was carrying the silence from the diner with her, the unanswered question that both of them knew the answer to — the scratch on a table that had been sold to strangers, the only permanent mark he'd made alongside anybody, and his daughter had asked if it was real and he'd told her he didn't remember, and she'd known that was a lie, and the lie was his answer, and somehow that was enough.

Somewhere in a better part of this same city, Brendan Coyle was at the hospital doing whatever orthopedic surgeons did on Wednesday evenings — fixing things, probably, putting bones back the way they were supposed to go — and he'd watched Doug's worst night multiple times and decided it was courage, and Doug would have traded quite a lot, not everything but quite a lot, to be the kind of person who could hear that word and feel anything other than what he felt, which was smaller than gratitude and heavier than shame and didn't appear in any policy language he'd ever read.

He stared at the water stain shaped like Ohio. He thought about calling his landlord. He thought about the egg in the refrigerator. He thought about the salt in the shaker at the diner, fused into a solid mass at the bottom from sitting too long in conditions it wasn't meant for.

He didn't call his landlord. He didn't do anything about the egg. He lay on his bed in his clothes in the dark and let the apartment be quiet around him, and after a while the quiet stopped feeling like absence and started feeling like the only honest thing left in a day that had used up all his other options.


# Chapter 6

Doug had never been to Los Angeles. He had never had a reason to go. The farthest west he'd traveled was Denver, for a claims seminar in 2017, and Denver had been fine in the way that most places were fine — the hotel had a lobby, the conference room had a projector and a bowl of individually wrapped mints, and the airport was a building he left through. Los Angeles was not Denver.

The first thing he noticed was the light. Not that it was sunny — Columbus had sun, sometimes, and Doug was not opposed to sun — but that the light had a different quality, as if someone had adjusted the settings on the day itself. Stepping out of the terminal at LAX, his shadow on the concrete had edges. Clean lines where light ended and shade began. In Columbus, in March, shadows were suggestions. Gray smears that could have been a cloud, a building, a guess. Here his shadow was precise enough to trace.

Layla had arranged a car, which turned out to be a man named Dmitri in a black Escalade who held a sign with Doug's name spelled "Doug Brigge" and drove with one hand on the wheel and the other on his phone, texting in what looked like Russian at a speed that suggested either the conversation was urgent or Dmitri simply drove and texted at the same rate, which was fast. He had a small laminated photo of a girl, maybe five or six, clipped to his sun visor, and he drove like a man who wanted to get home to her while also not entirely believing this was achievable. He asked Doug where he was visiting from. Doug said Columbus. Dmitri said, "Ohio?" Doug said yes. Dmitri nodded once and did not ask a follow-up question, which Doug appreciated.

The freeway had seven lanes. Doug counted them. Seven lanes of traffic moving at what appeared to be both very fast and completely stationary, depending on which lane you looked at, and Dmitri wove between them with the indifference of a man who had calculated the odds of his own death and found them acceptable. Outside the windows, the city slid by in a blur of low buildings and strip malls that looked, briefly, like any city, until Doug started noticing the plants. Palm trees lined the boulevard like tall bare poles with mops at the top — they looked less like trees than like something a landscaper had installed as a concept. Along the concrete walls bordering the freeway, bougainvillea exploded in bright pink and purple, pouring over fences and climbing walls and blooming in March with a kind of aggression that felt like a violation of seasonal protocol. In Columbus, in March, nothing bloomed. Things were still thinking about it. Here the flowers had decided months ago and were already bored with being in bloom.

The hotel was in Silver Lake, which Layla had described as "totally your vibe" in a text, a phrase Doug had looked at for a long time without understanding. The building was two stories, painted a gray that someone had probably called "concrete" or "ash" on a paint chip, and had a sign that was just the hotel's name in lowercase letters without punctuation: the wilding. Not The Wilding. Just the wilding, as if capitalization was something that happened to other hotels.

The woman at the front desk had short hair and earrings made from what appeared to be tiny pieces of driftwood. She was maybe thirty, and she wore a linen shirt that was either wrinkled by design or had been ironed by someone who didn't believe in it. She called Doug "love" when she handed him his key card, which was not a plastic card but a matte black rectangle with no visible markings. "You're in room seven, love. Up the stairs and to the left. There's cold brew in the lobby fridge, and we've got a sound bath in the garden at six if you want to decompress from your travel."

Doug did not know what a sound bath was. He did not ask. He took the matte black rectangle and went up the stairs.

The room had exposed brick on one wall, which Doug understood as a design choice rather than a construction deficiency, though his instinct was to assess the mortar joints, which looked original and were, in his professional opinion, due for repointing within five years. The bed was low to the ground and made with white sheets that had the crisp authority of sheets that had never been slept in, or had been washed at a temperature that removed all evidence of prior use. There were no closet doors. There was a closet — a rectangular alcove built into the wall with a rod and four wooden hangers and a folded white robe — but where doors would normally be there was nothing. Just the opening. Doug's bag had his suit in it, the same suit he'd worn to the wedding, the one Layla had told him to bring because "you might need it, babe, you never know," and he hung it on one of the wooden hangers and watched it hang there, visible from the bed, from the desk, from the bathroom doorway. You could not not see the closet. The suit looked like a person standing in the alcove, waiting to be worn by someone who didn't live here. This was apparently the point.

The bathroom had a shower made entirely of glass — floor, walls, door, all of it, the kind of arrangement that meant the person showering had no barrier between their body and the rest of the room. In Doug's apartment in Clintonville, the shower had a vinyl curtain from Target that he replaced every eight months when it started to mildew, and the replacement cost for that curtain was $12.99, and nobody had ever been in a position to observe him showering, which was exactly how he preferred it.

On the nightstand, next to a small succulent in a clay pot: a book called *The Surrender Experiment*. Doug picked it up. The cover had a picture of a man who looked calm in a way that suggested either genuine inner peace or a significant financial investment in appearing calm. The subtitle was "My Journey into Life's Perfection." Doug read the first sentence, which was about the author sitting in meditation and realizing that his personal preferences were the source of all his problems. He put the book down. He had not considered that his personal preferences were a problem. He had considered them preferences.

He sat on the edge of the low bed and looked at the room. The exposed brick. The doorless closet with his suit hanging in it. The glass shower he could see from where he sat. The book about surrendering. The succulent, which was alive and seemed to be doing fine without anyone attending to it, which was maybe the point of succulents.

His phone buzzed. Layla: "How's the hotel?? Isn't it incredible??"

Doug typed: "There are no doors on the closet."

Layla: "OMG I know right?? It's so Silver Lake. Get some rest babe, big day tomorrow!! Meeting's at 2. I'll pick you up at 1."

Doug looked at the time. It was 4:30 in the afternoon, Pacific, which was 7:30 in Columbus, which was when he normally ate dinner, which was normally cereal. He was hungry. He went downstairs. The woman at the desk — her name was either Marin or Maren, the tag was in a cursive that blurred the difference — directed him to a restaurant three blocks away. "They do amazing small plates," she said.

He walked three blocks through a neighborhood where every storefront had a single word for its name — Provision, Botanica, Yield — and nothing in the windows that indicated what was sold inside, and where a vine with orange flowers he'd never seen before was growing out of a crack in a retaining wall as if the crack were the point. The restaurant was Provision, and the menu was written on a chalkboard in handwriting so deliberate it might have been a font.

He ordered a chicken dish that came with something called "charred broccolini" and a grain he couldn't identify. The waitress asked if he wanted "still or sparkling" and he said "tap" and she paused as if processing this. The bill was $34 for one plate and a water. In Columbus, $34 bought him and Roy lunch for three days including pickles. He paid it and walked back to the hotel in air that was sixty-eight degrees at seven o'clock in March, which in Columbus would have required July, and the wrongness of the temperature made everything feel slightly tilted, as if he'd walked onto a set where the season was incorrect and nobody had fixed it.

He went to bed at nine o'clock Pacific, which was midnight his time. He brushed his teeth in a sink made of concrete — actual concrete, the kind used for foundations, formed into a basin — and looked at his face in a mirror that was larger than the mirror in his apartment and somehow showed more of him than he wanted to see. He got into bed. The sheets were smooth and cool. Through the doorless closet opening, his suit hung in the dark, a familiar shape in an unfamiliar room, shoulders slightly off because the wooden hangers were wider than his actual shoulders. He closed his eyes. Somewhere outside, a dog barked twice and then stopped, and the silence that followed was warmer and softer than any silence in Columbus, where March silence had a metallic quality, the sound of cold air holding still.

---

The Netflix office was not what Doug expected, though he wasn't sure what he'd expected, since his exposure to the entertainment industry was limited to a documentary about the Fyre Festival that Roy had described to him in such detail that Doug felt he'd seen it, even though Roy didn't watch documentaries, which meant Roy had heard about it from someone who had, and the secondhand summary had arrived at Doug's desk at Mutual Fidelity fully intact.

The building was glass and pale wood and had a lobby that felt more like a hotel lobby than the hotel's lobby had. A woman at the front desk gave Doug a visitor badge on a lanyard and told him to wait, and while he waited he sat on a couch that was either very expensive or an extremely convincing reproduction of something very expensive, and next to the couch there was a small table with a bowl of tangerines on it, and Doug ate one because he hadn't had lunch. He hadn't had lunch because the meeting was at two — which was apparently when people in Los Angeles ate lunch, not met about television — and Layla had picked him up at twelve-thirty instead of one, talking on her phone in the back seat of another black car about someone named Marcus who was "literally on fire right now, not in a bad way, in a brand way." Doug had watched Silver Lake turn into a freeway turn into another neighborhood turn into the Netflix parking lot with his stomach empty and a tangerine peel in his hand and nowhere to put it. He folded it into his jacket pocket and the jacket smelled like citrus for the rest of the day.

The conference room was on the third floor. Glass walls, which Doug was beginning to think was a condition in Los Angeles — an allergy to opacity, a commitment to making everything visible, including himself, which was the part that tightened his grip on the tangerine peel in his pocket. There were succulents on the windowsill. The table was long and pale and had the grain of real wood, which Doug noticed because the table in the Mutual Fidelity break room was laminate with a wood-grain print, and the difference was the difference between a claim with documentation and a claim without.

Charlie Falk came in like weather. He was mid-forties but moved like he hadn't been informed. White sneakers that were geometric and angular and looked like they'd been designed by someone who also designed buildings. A gray T-shirt that fit in the way of T-shirts that cost more than Doug's dress shirts. He had a notebook — not a phone, an actual spiral-bound notebook, covered in handwriting — and he was flipping through it as he walked, talking before he sat down, which gave Doug the impression that the conversation had started in another room and Charlie had carried it in here like a tray he hadn't put down.

"Doug. Doug, Doug, Doug." He said the name like he was testing the weight of it. He sat down across from Doug and leaned forward on his elbows. "Okay. I'm just going to say it. I've watched the toast — Maya, how many times have I watched the toast?"

One of the two people who had followed Charlie into the room looked up from her laptop. She was younger than Kelsey, maybe twenty-five, and had her hair pulled back tight and a pen wedged behind her ear that she kept reaching up to touch, as if checking it was still there. She wore sneakers too, but hers looked like they'd actually been used for walking, and there was a thin leather cord around her wrist with a single bead on it that she turned between her fingers when she wasn't typing. "You said fourteen," she said.

"It's more than fourteen. It's probably — that doesn't matter. The number doesn't matter. What matters is, every time I watch it, I see something new. And that's the mark of — that's, like — that's the thing."

The other person was a man in his early thirties with a beard trimmed so precisely it looked like it had been done with a level and a tattoo on his forearm of what appeared to be a line graph, though of what data Doug could not determine. He'd been nodding since he entered the room. He had not yet stopped. He had a notebook too, but his was leather-bound and expensive-looking, and he wrote in it with a pen that had a cap, which he removed and replaced between notes with a soft clicking sound.

"So." Charlie put both hands flat on the table. "Here's what I'm seeing. And tell me if I'm off base, because I want this to be a conversation, not a pitch. I want this to be us, in a room, just — figuring out what this thing is."

He paused. Doug waited. Charlie appeared to be waiting too, as if "tell me if I'm off base" had been a genuine request for permission to continue.

"You can go ahead," Doug said.

"Right. So what I love about the toast — and I know what I'm about to say is, like, a word that gets thrown around in this building, I know that. But I mean it specifically. What you did in that toast was — you were *authentic*. You were *vulnerable*. And the comedy wasn't — it wasn't material. It wasn't jokes. It was a man standing in, like, total emotional — it was — okay." He restarted. "You were in this space of honesty, and the comedy was a byproduct. Not the point. The byproduct. Does that make sense?"

Doug considered this. He'd been standing at a microphone. He'd been drunk. "I think so," he said, which was not true.

"And what excites me," Charlie said, building on his own sentence before it had finished settling, "is what that means for the special. Because we're not looking for a comedy special. We're looking for a Doug Bridge special. We're looking for — and this is the frame, this is what I keep coming back to, Maya, what's the —"

"Dad-core?" Maya said, not looking up.

"Dad-core, yes, but the bigger one."

"The masculinity conversation."

"The masculinity conversation. That's the frame." Charlie turned back to Doug. "You are, whether you know it or not, at the center of the masculinity conversation right now. The internet didn't just make you viral, Doug. The internet looked at you and said, here's a different way to be a man. Not the polished guy, not the — no offense to, what's his name, your daughter's —"

"Brendan," Doug said.

"Brendan. Not the Brendan model. Not the guy who has the right thing to say at the right time. You're the guy who says the wrong thing at the wrong time and *means it*. That's the conversation. That's the whole thing."

Charlie sat back. He looked at Doug with the expression of a man who had placed a large bet and was watching the wheel spin. The nodding man — his name, Doug would learn later, was also Brendan, which was a coincidence that Doug did not have time to process — nodded harder. Maya touched her pen.

Layla, who had been on her phone in a chair against the wall since they'd sat down, looked up. "And the energy around this is insane, babe. Just — Charlie, tell him about the numbers."

"The numbers." Charlie waved this away with his hand. "Eighty million views, that's not a video, that's a cultural — but I don't even want to talk about numbers. I want to talk about Doug. I want to talk about your life."

"Okay," Doug said.

"So walk me through it. A day. What does your day look like?"

Doug thought about his actual days. He woke up at 6:40. He showered. He ate toast or a banana. He drove to Mutual Fidelity. He processed claims. He ate lunch with Roy at the sandwich place they'd been going to for nine years. He processed more claims. He drove home. He ate dinner. He watched something on television or he didn't. He went to bed.

"It's pretty normal," he said.

"What's normal? Give me specifics."

"I go to work. I process insurance claims. Residential, mostly. Water damage. I eat lunch. I come home."

"And dinner? What's dinner?"

"Cereal, most nights."

Charlie went still. It was a specific kind of stillness — the stillness of a man who has been digging through rubble and hit something solid, who understands he should not move too fast or it might shift. The nodding man stopped nodding. Maya's hand froze on the way to her pen.

"Cereal," Charlie said.

"Yeah."

"For dinner."

"It's faster than cooking. And I'm the only one eating, so it doesn't —" Doug didn't finish the sentence because he wasn't sure where it was going and because the sentence, wherever it was going, was going somewhere he didn't want to be in this conference room.

"What kind of cereal?"

"It depends. Raisin Bran, usually. Sometimes Cheerios if they're on sale."

"Raisin Bran." Charlie said the words the way a jeweler might say the name of a stone. "And you eat this — where? At a table? On the couch?"

"Standing at the kitchen counter."

Charlie put both hands on top of his head, which was the gesture of a man watching something leave the park. "Standing at the counter," he repeated, mostly to himself. He looked at Maya. She was already typing.

"Okay, so — here's where I'm going with this." Charlie leaned forward again. "And this is just an idea, I'm just riffing, but — what if part of the special is your actual life? Not recreated, not dramatized. Your *actual* apartment, your *actual* routine. We put a camera in your kitchen and we film you eating Raisin Bran standing at the counter. We film you at your desk at — where do you work?"

"Mutual Fidelity."

"At Mutual Fidelity, processing claims. We film you driving to work. We film the *real stuff*. And then the live portion, the performance, that's you being — you. Talking about being you. The way you did at the toast."

Doug thought about his apartment. He thought about the kitchen counter with its stain near the sink from a coffee mug he'd left too long, which he cleaned every Sunday with a sponge he replaced monthly. The Ikea bookshelf in the living room with the books Roy had lent him over the years, none returned, arranged not by author or title but by the order he'd received them, which was a filing system only he understood. The one art print on the wall above the couch — a lithograph from a gallery on High Street that Megan had bought during one of the phases of their marriage when she was trying to make the apartment feel like somewhere you'd choose to live, and which she'd left behind when she moved out, either because she didn't want it or because she'd forgotten it, and Doug had never asked which because both answers would have been bad in different ways. The second bedroom, the one he called the office even though there was no computer in it, just boxes from the move twelve years ago, still packed, stacked against the wall in a configuration that had settled into permanence, sealed with packing tape that had yellowed but not split.

"It's not very interesting," Doug said.

"That's the POINT, Doug." Charlie said this with both hands open, palms up, as if receiving something. "That's the whole thing. You're not interesting in the way that interesting people *try* to be interesting. You're interesting because you're *not trying*. The apartment, the cereal, the insurance job — that IS the show. We don't need to add anything. We just need to point a camera at it."

"You want to show my apartment on Netflix."

"I want to show your LIFE on Netflix."

"My life is pretty boring."

"Doug." Charlie put his hand on his chest. "I need you to hear me when I say this. Boring is the most exciting thing in entertainment right now. Boring is — Maya, the anti-aspiration thing, didn't we just —"

Maya looked up from her laptop. "The anti-aspiration conversation." She had a small scar on her chin, barely visible, and when she talked to Charlie her voice had the careful patience of someone who had been in many rooms like this one and had learned that the rooms ended faster if she supplied the words Charlie was reaching for.

"Exactly. People are tired of watching people live better lives. They're tired of aspirational. They want — they want *cereal*. They want standing at the counter in a normal apartment. They want a man who drives a — what do you drive?"

"A Civic."

"A Civic. Who drives a Civic and eats cereal for dinner and goes to work at an insurance company and has been doing this for — how long have you been there?"

"About fifteen years."
