"Fifteen years. They want *that*, Doug. Because it's real. And we don't have enough of — there's not enough real right now."

Doug looked at the succulents on the windowsill. One of them had a brown spot on one leaf, and he had the urge to tell someone.

"I could show you the apartment," he said. "If you want to see it. I've got pictures on my phone from when I filed my own renter's claim a couple of years ago. Pipe burst in the unit above me. I documented everything — the water damage, the affected square footage, the baseboards. I've got before and afters."

Charlie looked at Layla. Layla looked at Charlie. The other Brendan — not that Brendan, a different Brendan, one who worked in content development and had a line graph tattooed on his arm and who said "totally" as connective tissue between other people's sentences — started nodding again, vigorously, pen cap clicking.

"He's incredible," Charlie said to Layla.

"I *told* you," Layla said, putting her phone down for the first time since the meeting started.

"We don't need to see pictures of your apartment, Doug." Charlie was shaking his head and smiling. "We're going to come to your apartment. We're going to bring a small crew to Columbus, Ohio, and we're going to film you living your actual life. That's the project."

"What would I do?"

"What you always do."

"I process claims."

"Yeah."

"You want to film me processing claims."

"I want to film you *being you*, and if being you means processing insurance claims, then yeah. We film you processing claims."

Doug thought about what processing claims actually looked like — him sitting at his desk reading documentation, making phone calls to policyholders, typing notes into the system, occasionally driving to a property to take photographs of water stains and mold patterns and warped flooring. He thought about explaining to a homeowner that their twenty-year-old carpeting had a pre-loss actual cash value of approximately $1.40 per square foot, and the specific expression people made when they heard this number, which was the expression of someone learning that the thing they'd walked on every day was worth almost nothing.

"The claims are confidential," he said.

"Sure, yeah. We can work around that. Obscure names, shoot from angles that don't show documents — that's production stuff, that's not your problem."

"The policies are proprietary. Mutual Fidelity probably wouldn't —"

"Doug." Charlie held up one hand. "We'll handle it. That's my side. Your side is being Doug."

"I'm always Doug."

"I know," Charlie said, and his voice dropped for a moment from its performance register into something quieter, almost tired, as if he'd been Charlie Falk, Development Executive, for the full meeting and was, for one second, just a man in a room who could hear what he'd been saying and knew what it sounded like from the outside. He looked down at his notebook, flipped a page, and the page was covered in his own handwriting but he didn't read it. Then he picked up his pen and the moment passed. "Okay, so — content test. Immediate next step. We'd want to shoot some preliminary footage. Nothing formal. We film you reacting to your own video. We film you at work. We film you eating dinner."

"Cereal."

"Cereal. Yes. We film the cereal." Charlie was back. Fully, completely back, and the enthusiasm was genuine, which was the part that Doug could not get his head around. Charlie was not being sarcastic. He was not mocking. He was sincerely excited about the prospect of filming a fifty-two-year-old insurance adjuster eating Raisin Bran alone in his kitchen in Columbus, Ohio, and this sincerity was more disorienting than mockery would have been, because mockery Doug could have understood. Mockery was the entertainment industry's version of depreciation — a reasonable adjustment for wear and use. This enthusiasm was something else entirely. It meant someone believed Doug's cereal was worth something, and Doug did not know how to assess worth without a depreciation schedule.

The meeting went on for another thirty-five minutes. Charlie outlined the content test timeline — a small crew would come to Columbus in two weeks, three people, "super low-key, you won't even notice them," which Doug doubted because he always noticed when people were in his apartment, which was part of why he lived alone. Maya asked about his schedule and whether he had any dietary restrictions, a question that confused him until he realized she was planning meals for the crew, not for him. The other Brendan took notes on his phone and said "love that" after nearly everything Charlie said, which was a phrase Doug had never heard used as a standalone response to a declarative sentence. He counted. Brendan said it nine times. He said "totally" eleven.

When the meeting ended, Layla and Charlie stood in the hallway talking while Doug waited near the elevator holding his visitor badge and looking at a framed poster of a show he'd never heard of. He could hear fragments — "momentum," "window," "the authentic thing is so big right now," "we've gotta move before the cycle turns" — and the language was the same as in the meeting but faster, stripped of the pauses and the looking-at-Doug moments, the way a claim looks different in the adjuster's notes than it does in the policyholder's living room. Doug realized that what he'd been sitting in was not a meeting but a presentation, and the presentation had been for him, designed to make him feel something specific, and it had worked. He felt something specific. He just didn't know what to call it.

---

The flight home left at 7:15 p.m. Doug was at the gate by 5:30 because he didn't know what else to do with the afternoon and because being early for a flight was the kind of behavior that made sense to him in a day where very little else had. Layla had offered to "grab a late lunch, or — oh, there's this incredible thing at LACMA, have you ever been?" and Doug had said no and then said he was fine and taken the black car back to the hotel and packed his bag and checked out, and Marin-or-Maren had said "Safe travels, love" and he'd left the matte black key card on the counter and walked out into the afternoon light that was still, even now, too sharp, too precise, too much like light that had been designed rather than had just happened.

He'd left *The Surrender Experiment* on the nightstand. He'd read two more pages while packing. The author had given up all personal preferences and let life guide his decisions, and this had apparently led him to build a successful software company, which seemed to Doug like a result that required at least some personal preferences about software, but he was not in a position to evaluate another man's surrender.

LAX was LAX. He ate a turkey sandwich at a place near the gate that charged $16 for turkey and Swiss on sourdough, which was roughly twice what the sandwich from his and Roy's place cost, and the sandwich from his and Roy's place came with a pickle. He sat at the gate and watched planes taxi on the tarmac and thought about what Charlie Falk had said. Not the specific words — those were already blurring the way witness testimony blurred in a disputed claim, the details smoothing into impressions — but the shape of it. They wanted to film his life. His apartment, his cereal, his Civic, his desk at Mutual Fidelity. They wanted the things he considered evidence of a life that had settled into its permanent dimensions, and they wanted them precisely because they were that evidence. The thing he would have depreciated was the thing they were appraising at full value.

He didn't know what to do with this. He bought a water and a pack of peanut M&M's from a kiosk where nothing cost less than $4.50, and he boarded the plane, and this time he had a window seat because Layla had rebooked his return flight, and he pressed his forehead against the cold plastic and watched Los Angeles shrink below him — the grid of streets, the low buildings, the freeways like rivers running cars instead of water — and then the mountains, which he hadn't known were there, which had been behind the city the whole time and which he had never once seen from the ground.

The plane crossed the desert, which was brown. Then something that was probably Nevada or Utah, also brown but differently — drier, more committed to its brownness, not apologizing the way the scrub near LA did, with its scattered bushes that looked like they were trying. Then the land turned green in patches, fields Doug recognized as agriculture even from thirty-seven thousand feet, and the patchwork became the Midwest, and the Midwest was flat, and the flatness was something he had never thought about as a quality. It had just been a fact. The way you didn't think about the temperature of the room you were in until you'd been in a room that was too hot.

Columbus appeared below him as the plane descended. He could see the grid of it, the low sprawl, the highways he drove every day reduced to gray lines on a surface of green and brown. The Scioto River was a dark thread. The neighborhoods were indistinguishable from this height — Clintonville, where he lived, and Worthington, where the diner was, and the Short North and German Village and Bexley and all the small named places that made up the geography of his daily life, all of them flattened into the same even surface, none reaching very high, none insisting on anything.

It looked right. He couldn't have said exactly why. It looked like a place that had enough room for what it was and didn't need more. It looked manageable. It looked, from thirty-seven thousand feet, like something a person might choose.

This was new. Doug had never thought of Columbus as a choice. He'd gone to Ohio State because it was affordable and close to Dayton. He'd stayed because he got the job at Mutual Fidelity and the job was fine. He'd lived in Clintonville because the apartment was available and the rent was $1,150 a month, which was reasonable for a two-bedroom, and the commute was eleven minutes in normal traffic. None of these were choices. They were the absence of choices, which was a different thing, or which he'd always assumed was different, until he sat in a glass room in Los Angeles and watched a man in expensive sneakers become ecstatic about his cereal. The absolute unreasonableness of that enthusiasm — caring about Doug's Raisin Bran, his kitchen counter, his Civic — had done something to the things themselves. It had made them visible. Not better, not special, just visible, the way the frame around a painting didn't change the painting but did tell you someone thought it was worth framing.

The plane landed. The wheels hit the runway and the cabin lurched and the passengers clapped, which Doug had never understood — you didn't clap for your bus driver, you didn't clap when you pulled into a parking space — but tonight the impulse made a kind of sense. He was back. Back felt different.

He got his bag from the overhead bin. He walked through the jet bridge and through the terminal, which was smaller than LAX in a way that felt proportional rather than insufficient. He walked to the parking garage where his Civic had been sitting for two days, and the car was gray and streaked with the salt residue of a Columbus March, and it started on the first try the way it always did, and the heater came on, and the seat was cold and then it wasn't.

He drove home on 670 and then north on High Street and then into Clintonville, and the trees were still bare, the way they'd been when he left, because two days was not enough for spring to arrive in Ohio. The streetlights made the familiar shapes of his neighborhood — the houses, the parked cars, the sidewalks he walked sometimes at night when he couldn't sleep. He pulled into his spot behind the building and turned off the engine and sat in the dark for a moment with his hands still on the wheel.

His apartment was exactly as he'd left it. The Ikea bookshelf. The art print above the couch. The kitchen counter with the stain by the sink. The second bedroom, door closed. He didn't open it. The boxes were in there, arranged in their permanent configuration, same as always, same as twelve years.

He opened the cabinet above the stove and took out the Raisin Bran. He poured a bowl. He stood at the counter and ate it, and the cereal tasted the same as it always tasted, which was fine, which was what cereal tasted like. He thought about Charlie Falk saying *That's the SHOW, Doug* and he looked at his kitchen and tried to see what Charlie saw. What he saw was a bowl, a spoon, a man, a counter, a stain, a clock that read 11:47 p.m. Eastern, an apartment in Clintonville, Ohio, with all its doors exactly where they should be.

He rinsed the bowl and set it in the drying rack, which held one bowl, one spoon, one coffee mug from this morning. He went to bed. The shower curtain in his bathroom was vinyl, translucent, and it pulled all the way closed on a curved rod, and he pulled it closed even though he wasn't showering, just because it was there and it did what it was supposed to do.


# Chapter 7

Simone Barr arrived on a Tuesday at ten in the morning, which was the time they'd agreed on, and this fact alone distinguished her from every person Doug had dealt with since the video. Layla was always twenty minutes late or forty minutes early. Charlie Falk had called twice at 6 a.m. Pacific, which was 9 a.m. in Columbus and therefore, in his mind, reasonable. Simone rang the buzzer at 9:59 and was standing in the hallway when Doug opened the door at 10:01, holding a laptop bag over one shoulder and a paper cup of coffee that wasn't from anywhere he recognized.

She was shorter than he'd expected, though he didn't know what he'd expected. He'd Googled her the night before and found a headshot on a management site — at least five years old, different hair — and a few credits listed on IMDb without photos. Three late-night shows. He didn't know which ones. He didn't watch late-night.

"Doug," she said. Not a question.

"Yeah. Come in."

She stepped inside and looked around the apartment the way Doug looked at a water-damaged house before he opened the claim file — measuring, not judging, but in a way where the measurement itself was a kind of judgment. She didn't comment on the Ikea bookshelf or the art print Megan had left behind or the cereal bowl he'd forgotten to wash that morning. She set her bag on the kitchen table, sat down, pulled the lid off her coffee, and blew on it once.

"You want some coffee?" Doug asked. "I've got a —"

"I'm good." She took a sip. Her nails were short and uneven, trimmed without a mirror, and her left thumb had a callus along the side from years of pressing a pen too hard. No rings. Her hair was pulled up with a clip that was handling maybe sixty percent of its job. She wore a gray sweater with a small hole near the cuff of the left sleeve that she hadn't fixed or hidden, just wore, the way you wear something when you've stopped noticing it. Doug noticed it.

He sat across from her. The kitchen table was small enough that their knees nearly touched, and he moved his chair back an inch. Simone didn't register this.

"So," Doug said.

"So." She opened her bag and took out not a laptop but a yellow legal pad, the kind you could buy at any office supply store for three dollars. She also took out a pen — black, nothing special — and placed them on the table beside the coffee. She didn't write anything. She looked at Doug.

"How does this work?" he asked.

"We talk. I listen. Sometimes I write things down."

"What things?"

"Things that work."

"How will you know?"

"I'll know."

Doug didn't know what to do with his hands. He put them on the table, then in his lap, then back on the table. The refrigerator hummed. Outside, a car passed with a bad muffler — the scrape of metal on asphalt — and he thought, automatically, that if the muffler detached and struck another vehicle the liability would fall to the city for road maintenance, not the driver, unless the driver had been previously cited for the exhaust condition. This was not a useful thought. He had it anyway.

"Tell me something funny," Simone said.

Doug looked at her.

"Funny," he said.

"Yeah."

He thought about it. He was aware of himself thinking about it, which made it worse — the same problem as trying to fall asleep by concentrating on falling asleep. He thought about jokes he knew. He didn't know many. Roy had told him one last month about a horse walking into a bar, and Doug couldn't remember the punchline, only that it involved the horse ordering a specific drink and the bartender either commenting or not commenting on the horse being a horse. He couldn't remember which version was the joke.

The silence went on.

Simone didn't fill it. She sat with her coffee and her legal pad and her pen and she waited. She had the patience of someone who had watched a lot of people sit in exactly this chair and produce exactly this silence. The quiet passed through uncomfortable into something worse — a zone where the silence itself had become the subject, where every second that passed without Doug saying something funny was another second of evidence that he could not say something funny, evidence that was accumulating in the record like unanswered interrogatories.

He looked at the kitchen window. The glass needed cleaning. There was a smudge at eye level that had been there since he'd leaned his forehead against it during a phone call with Layla three weeks ago, and he kept meaning to wipe it and kept not wiping it, and by now the smudge had become a feature of the window rather than a failure of maintenance.

He cleared his throat.

"I don't really — I don't have anything."

"I didn't ask you to prepare anything. I asked you to tell me something funny."

"That's the same thing."

"It's not."

Doug looked at the blank legal pad. The blankness felt personal, like a claim form with no entries — not absence of proof, but proof of absence.

"Okay," Simone said. She shifted in the chair. "Tell me about your day yesterday."

"My day."

"Yeah. What happened."

"I went to work."

"So tell me about work."

Doug had been telling people about work for twenty years and nobody had seemed interested, including, on many days, Doug himself. But Simone was sitting there with her pen and her pad and the clip in her hair slowly losing its grip, and she was waiting, and he didn't have anything else to offer.

"There's a claim I've been working on," he said. "Homeowner in Westerville. He had an above-ground pool in his backyard. Intex, twenty-four feet, the ones with the metal frame. They retail around seven hundred dollars. He'd had it three summers."

Simone didn't write anything. She held her coffee.

"The wall failed. Middle of the night, nobody outside. September, so the pool was still full — about fifteen thousand gallons. All of it went sideways into the neighbor's yard."

"Okay."

"The neighbor had roses. Hybrid teas. Seventeen plants. She'd been growing them for eleven years. She had receipts for the original plants, the fertilizer, the trellises, the pruning equipment, the soil amendments, the pH testing kits. She had photographs from every June going back to 2013. She had a log of every bloom cycle."

Doug paused. He'd said "log" and the word had a weight he didn't expect, sitting in his kitchen with a woman he'd known for twelve minutes. Everything he was describing — the documentation, the receipts, the careful recording of something that mattered to the person who kept the records — sounded like something else when he heard it out loud. He moved on.

"Total documented loss, forty-two hundred dollars. That's the plants, the infrastructure, and the lost growing season. She filed a claim."

"On whose policy?"

Doug looked at her. Nobody asked that. People always asked what happened to the roses.

"That's exactly the problem," he said. "She filed on her own homeowner's policy. But her policy covers weather events, fire, vandalism, accidental damage from covered perils. A neighbor's pool wall failing isn't weather. It's not vandalism. It's a third-party structural failure from a product with a known defect in the frame connectors."

"So she's not covered."

"Not under her own policy. The pool owner — his liability coverage has a sublimit on property damage that caps at two thousand, and even that's arguable because the policy language says 'sudden and accidental discharge.' You could make a case that a wall failing from a known manufacturing defect isn't accidental, it's negligence. And once you're in negligence territory, his insurer might deny the whole thing."

Simone's coffee had stopped moving. She was watching him with an expression he couldn't read — not boredom, not the polite attention people gave when Doug talked about insurance at parties, which was part of why he'd stopped going to parties.

"So you've got a woman with forty-two hundred dollars in dead roses and no coverage path. His insurance might deny, hers definitely denies, the manufacturer has a class action pending but it won't resolve for three years, and this woman is standing in her yard looking at seventeen dead hybrid teas and she calls me — she calls me personally, because I handled her kitchen fire claim in 2019 and she trusts me — and she says, 'Doug, someone owes me for my roses.'"

He stopped. He didn't know why he'd told this story. It wasn't a bit. It wasn't material. It was the kind of problem that made his job interesting — the jurisdictional tangle that most people found deadly boring and Doug found genuinely absorbing, the way a puzzle is absorbing when all the pieces exist but none of them fit the same picture.

Simone laughed.

Not a big laugh. It started as a breath through her nose and then her mouth opened slightly and her shoulders moved and there was a sound that was, unmistakably, a person being amused.

Doug stared at her.

"Why are you laughing? It's a real claim. She lost eleven years of roses."

"I know."

"There's no coverage path. I spent three days on the phone with two adjusters and a claims attorney trying to find one. It's a genuine jurisdictional problem."

"I know it is."

"So I don't understand what's funny."

Simone was still almost smiling — not broadly, just the residual expression of someone who had found something she'd been looking for in a place she hadn't expected. She picked up her pen for the first time and wrote something on the legal pad. Doug couldn't read it upside down. He thought he saw the word "roses" but it might have been something else.

"What are you writing?"

"Notes."

"About what?"

"About you."

Doug sat with that. The refrigerator hummed. The smudge on the window caught the thin spring light and held it.

"I still wasn't trying to be funny," he said.

"I know," Simone said. "That's the whole thing."

---

They met four times that first week. Tuesday through Friday, same table, same time. Simone brought her own coffee every morning — always a paper cup, always black, always from somewhere different, as if she were conducting a private survey of every coffee shop in Clintonville. Doug offered to make some the second day and she said, "I don't trust anyone else's coffee," and Doug understood this immediately because he didn't trust other people's claims documentation either, not until he'd verified the source.

The sessions had a pattern, though it took Doug until Thursday to see it. Simone would ask him something. Doug would try to answer the way he thought a comedian should — broader, louder in some unspecifiable way, with a setup and a turn and something at the end that was supposed to make a person laugh. And it would die. Not spectacularly. Not in a way that felt like failure so much as absence — the energy would leave the room the way air leaves a tire, slow and total, and Simone would sit there watching it go with the expression of someone who'd known it would happen and had let it happen anyway because the letting was part of the process.

Then she'd ask something else, or wait, or look out the window at the street, and Doug would forget that he was supposed to be performing and start talking like a person — like Doug — and something would happen. Not every time. Not on command. But enough that Simone's legal pad was filling up, page by page, with things he'd said that she considered worth recording and he considered just talking.

Wednesday she asked about his apartment.

"What about it?"

"Describe it for me."

"Two bedrooms. Kitchen. Bathroom. There's a bookshelf in the living room, the Ikea one."

"What's on it?"

Doug looked at the bookshelf from the table. He could see the spines from where he sat. "A book about the history of the insurance industry that I got at a library sale. I think I paid two dollars. A novel Roy lent me in July that I haven't started. The user manual for my dishwasher."

"Why's the dishwasher manual on the bookshelf?"

"The drawer where I keep manuals is full."

"What's in the drawer?"

"The manual for the microwave. The manual for the TV. The manual for a blender I don't own anymore. I donated the blender to Goodwill in 2018 but I kept the manual because it was filed with the others and removing it would've left a gap in the folder."

Simone wrote something down. Doug watched her write it and felt the specific discomfort of someone finding the ordinary facts of his life notable. He kept the manual for a blender he'd given away eight years ago. That was filing. That was consistency of records. He didn't see what else it was.

"What's the second bedroom?" she asked.

"Office."

"You work in there?"

"No."

"Computer?"

"No."

"What's in it?"

"Boxes."

Simone waited. Doug recognized the technique — the silence that people fill because people can't stand silence, the interviewer's move of leaving a space the subject will step into. He'd done it himself, sitting across from policyholders who'd left something out of the paperwork, waiting for the omitted detail to surface on its own. He knew what Simone was doing. He walked into it anyway.

"They're from the move," he said. "Twelve years ago. I unpacked most of the apartment but the second bedroom — I put the boxes in there and shut the door. I haven't opened them."

"You don't know what's in them?"

"I know what I use. I haven't needed whatever's in those boxes in twelve years. If you don't file a claim within the policy period, you lose — " He stopped. He'd done it again. He'd answered a question about his life in insurance language, as if the boxes in the second bedroom were subject to a statute of limitations and twelve years of not opening them constituted an expired filing window.

Simone didn't laugh this time. She wrote something long — two lines, maybe three — and Doug had the feeling that whatever she'd written mattered more than the roses.

Thursday she asked about Roy.

"He's my friend. We work together."

"Tell me about him."

"He's been at Mutual Fidelity longer than me. I don't know exactly how long because he doesn't talk about it. He eats the same sandwich every day at lunch. Turkey, mustard, no tomato. He's gotten that sandwich for the entire nine years I've known him. I asked him once if he'd ever thought about trying something else on the menu and he said he'd found what he liked and didn't see any reason to keep looking."

"And?"

"And nothing. He said it and ate his sandwich."

"But you remembered it."

Doug shifted in the chair. "I remember things people say. It's — you have to, in claims work. Policyholders tell you things and you need to know which details matter later."

"So Roy's sandwich order matters later."

"I didn't say it matters. I said I remembered it."

But it did matter, and Simone either knew this or guessed it, because she wrote it down and then looked at him with the expression she'd been wearing all week — diagnostic, appraising, the look of someone identifying a pattern in a data set rather than sharing a moment with a person. It bothered Doug. Not because it was unkind but because it was accurate. She was treating him the way he treated a claim file with documentation that didn't quite add up — looking for the discrepancy, the thing present in the evidence but missing from the narrative, the gap that would explain why the numbers didn't reconcile.

Friday, something shifted.

"I've been thinking about this," Doug said. He'd been thinking about it all week, sitting across from Simone, failing to be funny on purpose, watching her write down the things he said when he wasn't trying. "I should watch some comedy. Study it. How people build a set, the structure, the rhythm. I should learn the form."

Simone put her pen down. She did this when she was about to say something she'd been holding. The pen going flat on the table was a signal Doug had already learned to read, like Roy's long pause before answering — the tell that the next thing would carry weight.

"Don't," she said.

"Why not?"

"Because you'll try to do what they do."

"I know I can't do what they do. I'm talking about the mechanics. The structure."

"The mechanics aren't your problem."

"Then what is?"

Simone leaned back. The hair clip had come loose on one side again and she reached up and fixed it without looking, the automatic gesture of someone who'd fixed the same clip a thousand times. Through the kitchen window, the street was gray and damp, the trees along the sidewalk showing the first cautious suggestion of leaves — not quite committed, the way Columbus handled the transition out of winter, a slow negotiation between seasons where neither side fully conceded until sometime in May.

"You think funny is something people do," she said. "A skill. A performance. You watch someone do a tight set on TV and you think, that's what funny looks like, and I don't look like that. And you're right, you don't look like that."

"Right, so —"

"I'm not done. The toast worked because you weren't doing anything. You were standing up drunk saying true things. No form. No structure. Just a man with too much whiskey saying what he'd been carrying for twenty years, and the room could tell. That's why eighty million people watched it."

"So what, I need to drink before every show?"

"You need to not be trying." She picked up her coffee cup, looked into it, found it empty. "Which is the hardest thing I can ask you, because every time you sit at this table and try to be funny for me it dies. And every time you forget you're trying and just talk about your life — the roses, the boxes, Roy's sandwich — something happens. And you don't believe me."

She was right. He didn't believe her. The things she was calling material — a coverage dispute, a blender manual, a man who'd eaten the same sandwich for nine years — weren't funny. They were his life. They were the ordinary mechanics of a man who lived alone and worked in insurance and had organized his existence around the principle that things should be documented, filed, and kept in order. That wasn't comedy. That was procedure.

"The special is supposed to be sixty minutes," Doug said. "I can't go up there and talk about filing systems for an hour."

"Why not?"

"Because that's not what they're paying for. They want the toast."

"The toast was seven minutes of you talking about your life. Not a bit, not a routine — your marriage, your daughter, the table, twenty years of things you'd never said out loud. Your life. The filing systems, the roses, the sandwich — it's the same material. Different room."

"It's not the same."

"Why?"

"Because the toast was — " Doug stopped. He didn't know how to finish the sentence. The toast was what? Real? He was real right now, sitting at his kitchen table with a comedy writer and a dripping faucet and a legal pad full of things he'd said that she thought were worth something and he thought were just how he talked. But it wasn't the same. The toast had been reckless. A man with no guardrails saying things that cost him something — his dignity, his privacy, his daughter's wedding reception. This was careful. This was Doug being Doug, and Doug being Doug had never, in fifty-two years, been enough to hold anyone's attention for more than the time it took to process their claim and send them the check.

"Can't you just write me material?" he said. "Jokes. Bits. Something I can rehearse."

Simone set the empty cup on the table. "I could write you ten minutes of solid, professional stuff by Monday. Setups, tags, callbacks. Tight, structured, the works."

"Okay. Good."

"And it would be dead. Because it wouldn't sound like you. It'd sound like a writer's version of you, and the audience would feel the gap the way you feel a crack in a windshield — you can't say exactly why it bothers you, but something's wrong and you can't stop looking at it."

She paused. She flipped the legal pad back a few pages, read something she'd written on Tuesday. Her lips moved slightly as she read — a habit, Doug thought, of someone who'd spent twenty years turning speech into text and text back into speech, the constant translation between what people say and what works on paper and whether those ever quite line up.

"You're not a comedian, Doug."

"Yeah. I know."

"No — you know it as a failing. You hear 'you're not a comedian' and you hear 'I can't do this thing everyone wants me to do.' I'm saying something different. You're not a comedian the way a documentary isn't a feature film. It's not a lesser thing. It's a different thing. The way you talk about roses like they're a tort case, the way you keep a manual for a blender you gave to Goodwill — that's a voice. It's not a comedy voice. It's your voice. And it happens to be funny, and you can't see it because you've spent your whole life watching other people be funny and none of them sounded like you."

Doug looked at his hands on the table. He had the hands of a man who typed claim reports and occasionally tightened the bolts on a kitchen faucet and did nothing else that required physical skill or dexterity. The faucet, in fact, had been dripping for two weeks — a forty-cent washer, a fifteen-minute fix. He hadn't done it because the drip had become part of the apartment's operating noise, like the refrigerator hum and the radiator's occasional tick and the specific silence of a room where one person lives and does not expect visitors.

He wanted to believe her. He wanted to believe that the way he described coverage gaps and blender manuals and boxes he hadn't opened in twelve years was material — that the specific way he moved through the world, documenting and filing and assessing, was itself the thing that would fill sixty minutes on a stage. But he'd watched the toast video dozens of times now, and the man in that video was not this man. That man was out of control. This man — sitting at the kitchen table with a dripping faucet and a clean window and a legal pad full of someone else's observations about his personality — was the opposite of out of control. This man had spent his entire adult life making sure nothing got out of control, and it had worked, mostly, in the sense that nothing had, and also in the sense that nothing had.

"I'll think about it," he said.

"Sure." Simone closed the legal pad. She put the pen in her bag. She stood and put on her jacket — olive-green canvas, worn soft at the elbows, the kind of jacket that had gone in and out of style enough times that it had given up and just become a jacket. "Same time Monday?"

"Monday."

At the door she stopped and turned back. "The woman with the roses. Did she ever get her money?"

"Sixteen hundred. I found an umbrella policy on the pool owner that everybody'd missed. Not full recovery, but it was something."

"How'd she take it?"

"She said I was the only person who took it seriously."

"The claim?"

"The roses. She said I was the only person who took the roses seriously. There's a difference, she said, between taking the claim seriously and taking the roses seriously." He paused. "I didn't understand the distinction at the time."

"And now?"

Doug looked at Simone. She was standing in his doorway with her bag and her jacket and the clip in her hair that had held up better today than yesterday, and she was asking him a question that wasn't about comedy or material or the special, or maybe it was about all of those things and also none of them.

"I still don't," he said. "But I think about it."

Simone looked at him for a moment — not the diagnostic look, not the assessment. Something else, something he didn't have a filing category for. Then she left.

Doug sat at the table. The apartment returned to its default state — quiet, still, the sound of a space resuming the shape it held when nobody was watching it. He looked at the chair where Simone had been sitting. There was a faint impression in the cushion that would be gone by the time he got home from the office.

He washed the cereal bowl from that morning. He stood at the sink and looked out the window — the smudge was still there; he'd only imagined wiping it — and he thought about the woman in Westerville and her roses. How she'd made a distinction between the claim and the roses as though they were different things, and how, standing in his kitchen with the faucet dripping and the legal pad gone and the week's sessions sitting in him like something he hadn't figured out how to process yet, he thought maybe they were. The claim was the money. The roses were the eleven years she'd spent on her knees in the dirt, and the photographs every June, and the pH testing kits, and the fact that she'd built something alive and specific and irreplaceable and then fifteen thousand gallons of water had destroyed it overnight, and what she'd wanted from Doug wasn't sixteen hundred dollars. She'd wanted someone to understand what had actually been lost, and sixteen hundred dollars was as close as the system could get.

He picked up his keys. He drove to Mutual Fidelity. He had claims to process and Roy was expecting him for lunch and the sandwich place wouldn't change and neither would Roy's order and that was, for the moment, the only piece of his life that didn't seem to require him to be something he wasn't.


# Chapter 8

Doug had been inside Brendan and Megan's house exactly four times in twelve years. He knew this the way he knew the deductible on a standard HO-3 homeowner's policy -- it was a number that had settled into his memory through repetition and mild dread. The first time was Kelsey's high school graduation party, where he'd stood in the backyard holding a paper plate of potato salad and talking to the father of one of Kelsey's friends about the traffic pattern on I-270, which had been a real conversation for approximately seven minutes before the man went to get another beer and didn't come back. The second was a logistics meeting about the wedding seating chart, during which he sat at the kitchen island and Megan produced a laminated diagram and they agreed on everything in twenty minutes because there was nothing left between them that was worth arguing about. The third was delivering a tax form Megan needed urgently, which required him to step inside for ninety seconds while she found an envelope, and during those ninety seconds he had noticed that the kitchen faucet was new. The fourth, three years ago, he'd waited in the living room for Kelsey to get ready for dinner and Brendan had given him a beer and asked about his work, and Doug had described the documentation process for residential water damage claims until Brendan's expression passed through interest, past politeness, and arrived at something closer to medical concern.

Now there was a fifth time, and Doug was sitting in the Civic in the driveway at 6:52 p.m. on a Tuesday in early April, eight minutes before he was expected, watching light move behind the kitchen windows.

The invitation had come from Megan by text, which was how they communicated -- texts about logistics, occasional emails about Kelsey, the rare phone call when tone of voice mattered more than efficiency. *Brendan and I would love to have you for dinner Tuesday. He thinks it would be good for all of us to sit down together. No agenda.* The phrase "no agenda" was the kind of thing Megan said when there was definitely an agenda, and the agenda was the reasonable management of a situation she had already assessed and organized into a plan that would be presented as spontaneous. Doug had typed *Sure, Tuesday works* and stared at the words for forty seconds before sending, making sure the message landed in the narrow band between agreeable and dead.

He could have said no. He had weighed the contingencies -- mapping the outcomes, estimating the exposure. Saying no would require explaining why. The honest explanation was that Brendan's house made Doug feel like a depreciated asset standing next to a full replacement. He couldn't say this to Megan, who would contextualize it into something manageable. He couldn't say it to Brendan, who would take it seriously and respond with such careful warmth that Doug would want to walk into the Olentangy River. So he was here, in clean khakis and a blue button-down he had ironed for the first time since November. The iron had spit rust-colored water on his first shirt, which meant switching to this one, which had a slightly too-tight collar he could feel against his throat every time he swallowed.

The evening air was cool. April in Columbus still had winter in it -- the trees on Brendan's street had that uncertain look, buds visible but not committed, as if they were waiting for a second opinion. In Upper Arlington, people had opinions about their landscaping and the money to act on them. The lawns were already green. Doug's building in Clintonville had a shared strip of grass that someone mowed when it became embarrassing.

At 6:58 he got out of the car. The driveway was concrete, recently sealed, that dark even sheen that meant someone had paid to maintain something that would have been fine without maintenance. Two cars: Megan's Subaru and Brendan's silver Lexus. Doug's Civic, parked behind the Lexus, had a coffee stain on the passenger seat and a Mutual Fidelity parking tag hanging from the rearview mirror. He left the tag. Removing it would have been a statement about being seen.

The front door opened before he reached it. Brendan must have seen the headlights. He stood in the doorway wearing a blue apron over a grey crewneck sweater, a wooden spoon in his left hand, and the expression of a man who was genuinely pleased to see someone he had every reason to pity. Brendan was fifty-four and looked like a person who had arrived at his own life without taking any wrong turns -- tall, graying at the temples in the way that men in movies were permitted to gray, his posture easy, his hands the steady hands of an orthopedic surgeon who spent his days putting broken things back together. Doug's hands typed descriptions of other people's damage.

"Doug. Hey. Come in, come in."

They shook hands. Brendan's grip was firm and unhurried and communicated nothing except competence, which was enough. Doug stepped inside and the house took him in with warmth and the smell of rosemary and something citrus -- lemon, maybe, and something else underneath that he couldn't name because Doug was not a man who identified herbs with confidence. He knew the smell of wet drywall after a pipe burst. He knew the chemical signature of smoke damage versus grease fire damage. Rosemary and lemon were outside his professional range.

The hallway was wide enough that two people could walk side by side without touching. Doug's hallway in Clintonville was not. There was a photograph on the wall he didn't remember from the last visit -- Megan and Brendan on a beach somewhere, both wearing sunglasses, Brendan's arm around her shoulder with the settled weight of a man who had been touching this woman for a decade and still had reasons. Doug looked at the floor. The hardwood was clean and uniform, no visible wear at the thresholds or turning points. In claims, you could date a floor by where it wore first -- the path between bedroom and kitchen, the spot by the front door where shoes came off, the traffic triangle from fridge to stove to sink. This floor gave nothing away. It was either new or someone had paid to make the age invisible.

"How was the drive?"

"Fine," Doug said. "No traffic." This was true but also the smallest possible answer to a question that deserved even less thought than he was giving it. The drive had been twelve minutes of negotiating with himself about whether to stop at the Shell station on Henderson for gum, as if gum would make this evening tolerable.

"Good, good." Brendan walked toward the kitchen and Doug followed because the alternative was standing in the hallway. "Can I get you something? I've got a red open -- a Cotes du Rhone, it's good -- or beer, sparkling water, whatever."

There it was. Doug had run this scenario during the drive, mapping the contingencies like an adjuster with a contested claim. He would be offered alcohol. He would say yes or no. Saying no would be a statement -- about the toast, about the eight whiskeys at the wedding, about the video that eighty million people had watched him give because of what happened after those eight whiskeys. Saying no would put the evening on a footing where everyone was performing awareness of Doug's relationship with alcohol, which was not a relationship he wanted examined. Saying yes meant monitoring himself, counting sips, performing control.

"Wine sounds good," he said. "Just a glass."

Brendan poured without ceremony from a bottle already breathing on the counter. The glass was the oversized kind that wine people used because the shape was supposed to matter, and it was heavy in Doug's hand. He took a sip. It was good. This was the worst possible outcome, because bad wine would have been one small deficiency to file away, one tiny crack in Brendan's general competence. The wine was good and the glass was heavy and the kitchen was the kind of kitchen that appeared in the magazines his mother left on her coffee table at Maple Glen, prices circled in pen, her opinions written in the margins beside photographs of rooms she would never occupy.

The kitchen had a stone-topped island, a range with six burners -- Doug counted, because counting was what he did when he didn't know what else to do -- and copper pots hanging from a rack, arranged by size, their bottoms darkened from use. None of it was decorative. Brendan cooked in this kitchen, which was different from merely having an expensive kitchen, and the difference was the part that Doug found hardest to dismiss. A nice kitchen you didn't use was just money. A nice kitchen with wear patterns was a life being lived.

Four lamb chops sat on a wooden cutting board, thick-cut, resting. The juices had pooled along the grain of the wood, pinkish-brown. There was a small bowl of green sauce and a sheet pan of roasted potatoes, golden, their edges slightly charred. Doug calculated the cost reflexively, an adjuster's reflex -- the lamb alone was probably forty dollars, maybe forty-five depending on the butcher. His own dinner the previous night had been a bowl of cereal he'd eaten standing at the counter, approximate cost sixty-three cents including the milk.

"Smells good," he said, because it did and because he had been standing in another man's kitchen in silence for what was beginning to feel like an appraisal.

"Thanks. New marinade -- harissa and lemon. Megan found the recipe." Brendan prodded one of the chops with a fork, then stopped himself and set the fork down. It was a small gesture of self-correction, and Doug found it unreasonably humanizing. Even Brendan had doubts about his lamb. This was new information. Doug didn't know where to file it. "You're not supposed to poke them," Brendan said, mostly to himself. "It lets the juices out. I always forget." He had a tea towel thrown over one shoulder, not draped but bunched, the way someone did it when they'd been cooking for an hour and had forgotten it was there. His left thumb had a small cut near the nail, recently healed, and his watch -- expensive, Doug could see that much -- had a scuff on the face that said it was worn for function, not display.

Megan came down the stairs. She was wearing a dark blouse and jeans, small gold earrings, her hair down. Doug noticed -- with the detached precision he wished were genuinely detached -- that she looked good. Not good in any way that required comment or seemed to ask for it. Good because she had organized her life to the point where appearance was one more thing she managed without visible effort. She had a particular way of entering a room, unhurried, as though everything on the other side of the threshold already knew she was coming. She'd always had it. When they were married he'd found it attractive. Now it was just a fact about her, filed under general information, cross-referenced with nothing.

"Doug. Hi." She kissed his cheek, a contact so brief it could have been imagined. Her perfume was different from what she'd worn when they were married -- lighter, less sweet. Twelve years was enough time to change your perfume several times over. He wondered, with the part of his brain that catalogued things uselessly, whether the change had happened all at once or gradually, and whether Brendan had bought it or whether she'd chosen it herself, and then he stopped wondering because the answer was none of his business in a way that had been formalized by the Franklin County Domestic Relations Court.

"Thanks for coming," she said. "I know it's --" She let the sentence find its own ending, which was a Megan move, giving the other person room to fill the silence with whatever version was most comfortable.

Doug didn't fill it.

"Sure," he said. He held up his wine glass, a gesture that communicated nothing except that he had hands and they were occupied. "Brendan got me the wine."

"I see that." She smiled. It was not the smile he'd heard on the phone two weeks ago when he'd called to apologize, the one that was organized and fair and slightly instructional. This one was smaller. Tired at the corners, maybe. Or maybe he was reading things that weren't present because reading absent details was, professionally speaking, what he did.

She poured herself a glass from the same bottle. They stood in the kitchen, the three of them, arranged around the island with the lamb resting between them, and Doug could feel the evening organized around him in a way he could sense but couldn't name -- as though the dinner were a claim and he were the claimant, and Brendan and Megan were the adjusters, conducting their assessment with professional warmth.

They sat down in the dining room. Megan had set the table with cloth napkins and actual plates -- not the paper plates from the graduation party, not the casualness of a pizza night. The table was dark wood, rectangular, and seated six. There were three of them. Doug sat at one end, Brendan at the other, Megan in the middle on one side, which put her equidistant from both men in a way that was probably how she always sat and felt architectural. The three empty chairs made the room larger than it needed to be.

Brendan served. He placed a lamb chop on each plate deliberately -- the chop angled, the potatoes arranged in a crescent, the green sauce drizzled rather than spooned. Doug watched this and thought about his own three paper plates, the ones he used when he was too tired to wash a real dish, which was most nights.

"This looks incredible," Megan said. She meant it, and it did.

Doug cut into the lamb. It was pink in the center, tender, the harissa giving it a slow heat that arrived after the first chew and stayed. He swallowed and the food was, in any honest assessment, the best thing he'd eaten in recent memory, and this fact settled into the evening like one more piece of evidence in a claim he was losing.

"How's it going?" Brendan asked. The question was broad enough to mean anything -- the job, the video, the fame, the specific experience of sitting in this house -- and Doug chose the narrowest reading available.

"Busy at work. We've had a run of burst pipe claims from the thaw."

"Right, spring." Brendan nodded. "We see the same thing -- slip-and-falls, this time of year. The ice-melt transition." He was trying to meet Doug on professional territory, which Doug recognized as generous and which made the income gap between an orthopedic surgeon's slip-and-fall cases and an insurance adjuster's burst pipes feel like a distance measured in different units entirely.

"Doug's been working with a comedy writer," Megan said. The topic shift hit like a draft from an opened window. He hadn't told Megan about Simone -- Layla must have, or Kelsey, or the internet. There had been a line in one of the smaller articles.

"Simone Barr," Megan added. "I looked her up. She's written for some pretty impressive shows."

"She's good," Doug said. "We're working on material."

"What's that like?" Brendan asked. He had his fork halfway to his mouth and paused there, genuinely considering. "Working with someone on material, what do you actually do?"

"We sit at my kitchen table. She asks me questions. I talk. Sometimes she writes things down."

"Huh." Brendan ate his bite. "Is it helping?"

"I don't know yet."

They ate. The sound of silverware on plates, of three people chewing. Megan's knife made a small scraping noise against the plate and she adjusted her grip, a reflex so automatic she probably didn't notice it. Doug noticed. He noticed the scrape and the adjustment and the fact that Megan still held her knife the European way, blade facing in, the way she had held it through eighteen years of their marriage and twelve years of not being married, and the fact that this had not changed was both meaningless and exactly the sort of detail his brain stored without permission.

"So," Brendan said, and the word carried the weight of someone about to bring up the subject everyone had spent forty minutes navigating around. "The video. I just want to -- can I say something about it?"

"Brendan," Megan said. It wasn't sharp. It had the quality of a line that had been discussed beforehand, a planned intervention that was supposed to sound spontaneous.

"No, I just --" He set his fork down. "I watched it again. Last week. And I know we've talked about this, and I know it's been discussed to death, but I want to say, to you directly, that I think it was raw. It was incredibly raw and real, and I think there's something in it that most people can't access."

Doug moved a potato from one side of his plate to the other.

"I don't mean that in a --" Brendan glanced at Megan, then back at Doug. "I'm not analyzing it. I'm just saying, there was a willingness in it to just... say things."

"I was drunk," Doug said.

"I know."

"Eight whiskeys."

"I know. But the whiskey didn't put those things there. It just --"

"Removed the filter," Doug said, because everyone said this, and saying it first was faster than hearing someone else arrive at it.

"Yeah."

Megan touched her left earring, rotating it between thumb and forefinger. Doug didn't know if this was a new habit or one she'd always had that he'd lost track of in twelve years of not sitting across from her at dinner.

"I think what Brendan's trying to say," Megan said, "is that we both found it meaningful. Even though the circumstances were --" She selected. "Complicated."

"It was our daughter's wedding," Doug said.

"It was," Megan said. "And the toast was a lot of things. It was inappropriate, and it was embarrassing, and it was also -- some of it -- honest in a way that I think both of us needed to hear." She delivered this in sequence, each clause arriving in order, the way she delivered everything -- organized, fair, structurally sound. It was the voice she used when she was being reasonable, which was different from being kind and different from being honest. Being reasonable was Megan's craft. She was excellent at it. Doug had lived with it for eighteen years and it still made him feel like a water stain someone was trying to manage.

"Can we talk about something else," Doug said. It was not a question.

"Of course," Megan said. "Absolutely."

They talked about something else. Brendan mentioned a medical conference he was attending in May, something about shoulder reconstruction techniques. Megan talked about a fundraising gala for the nonprofit where she was coordinating donor tables, seating logistics, a silent auction that required insurance, and Doug found himself briefly engaged by the insurance question before realizing she had not actually asked for his professional opinion and was describing the event socially, not seeking a coverage assessment. He listened to the details and found them mildly soothing, the way other people's operational problems were soothing when they were bounded and specific and not yours.

Brendan asked about Doug's current caseload, and Doug, grateful for the redirect, described the burst pool from a few months back -- the above-ground wall that had split in February, the flood that destroyed the neighbor's seventeen hybrid tea roses, the question of whose policy covered what when the damage crossed a property line through third-party structural failure. He explained it with the thoroughness the subject deserved. The pool owner's liability didn't extend to the neighbor's landscaping under the standard policy. The neighbor's policy covered weather events but not structural failure from an adjacent property. Seventeen hybrid teas, four thousand two hundred dollars, documented with receipts and photographs.

"So nobody's covered," Brendan said.

"There's a gap. Neither policy was designed for this scenario."

"And the roses are just... gone."

"The roses are destroyed. They were hybrid teas. You can't just replant hybrid teas, you have to buy mature stock, and the soil's been contaminated by the pool chemicals, so there's a remediation question on top of the replacement question." Doug said this with the care the information warranted, and Brendan was listening with what appeared to be genuine interest, which was either a credit to Brendan's social skills or a sign that the dynamics of insurance coverage gaps were more compelling than Doug had been led to believe.

"That's kind of tragic," Brendan said.

"It's a claims issue," Doug said. "But yeah."

Megan stood and began clearing plates. She had a system -- her plate, then Brendan's, then reaching for Doug's with a questioning look. Doug had eaten everything. The lamb, the potatoes, the sauce, gone. His plate was clean the way hungry people's plates were clean, which was not the same as polite people's plates, and he realized with a small flush of something he couldn't name that he should have left a bite behind. Leaving food signaled ease, and ease was the one thing he had not managed to perform all evening.

His wine glass sat where he'd set it down during the main course, still more than half full, the wine at body temperature now. He had been holding it and setting it down and holding it again for almost an hour, and the heat from his hand had changed it into something warm and flat. He left it on the table.


Brendan made coffee with a machine Doug could not identify -- not a standard drip maker, something with a chrome lever and a pressurized element and a sound like a small, expensive argument with physics. The coffee came in small cups. It was strong and slightly bitter and Doug held his with both hands because the cup was warm and the living room, where they had moved, was cooler than the kitchen.

The living room connected to the dining room through an archway wide enough that the two rooms were almost one. The furniture was the kind that did not call attention to itself while costing more than Doug's rent -- a deep sofa in a dark fabric, two armchairs, a coffee table with a single book and a ceramic bowl that held nothing. Doug sat in one of the armchairs because the sofa invited proximity and the armchairs were their own territory. Brendan took the sofa. Megan sat at the other end of it, legs curled under her, holding her coffee cup with both hands and watching the conversation the way Doug watched a claim where the documentation didn't line up -- attentively, waiting for the detail that would make everything make sense.

There was a bookshelf against the far wall with actual books, arranged by some system that suggested they'd been read and returned to a place that mattered -- not by color, not by size, just where they went. Two framed prints, warm-toned, abstract. The lamp beside the sofa gave a yellow light that made the room feel smaller and warmer than it was. Doug's apartment had one art print, the one Megan had left behind when she moved out. He had never asked whether she'd left it on purpose or simply hadn't wanted it. Asking would produce an answer, and the answer would tell him something about how thoroughly she'd thought about leaving, and he didn't need to know.

"I'm glad you came," Brendan said. He was sitting with one ankle crossed over the opposite knee, coffee balanced on the armrest with a steadiness that probably came from years of holding small instruments in high-pressure environments. The posture of a man who was home. "I know this isn't --" He paused. "I know the three of us in a room together isn't exactly natural."

"It's fine," Doug said.

"I just think with everything going on, the video and the attention, it seemed like the right time to just... sit down. Clear the air a little."

Doug held his coffee. The cup was small and warm and probably cost more than his lunch. He was careful with it.

"I appreciate that," he said. He did, in the way you appreciate someone offering to carry something for you when they don't realize it's on fire.

"Can I ask you something?" Brendan said.

Doug didn't say yes. He waited.

"What are you going to do with all of it? The fame, the Netflix thing, the whole... trajectory. What's the plan?"

"There's a special. They're taping it at the Lincoln Theatre. Simone and I are putting material together."

"Are you enjoying it?"

Nobody had asked Doug this. Layla asked if he was ready. Simone asked if he was working. Kelsey asked if he was okay. Not one of them had asked whether he was enjoying it, probably because the answer was so obviously tangled that the question itself seemed reckless.

"Parts of it," Doug said. "The working with Simone. She gets what's happening better than I do."

"And the performing part?"

"I haven't performed yet. Not really. There's a corporate event coming up. Indianapolis."

"Well." Brendan drank his coffee. Set the cup down on the armrest. Picked it up again. Doug had never seen Brendan fidget before, and the motion was small enough to have been nothing, but Doug's entire professional life was built on noticing small things that might have been nothing and establishing whether they were. "I've been thinking about what you did. At the wedding."

Doug's body prepared to hear, for the thousandth time, someone's interpretation of the toast. He had a rotation of responses organized by function: deflection (he was drunk), minimization (it wasn't planned), redirect (the lamb was excellent). He queued the deflection.

"And I don't mean the speech itself," Brendan said. He uncrossed his legs and leaned forward, elbows on knees, and the shift in posture changed the geometry of the room. He was closer now, and his voice was quieter. Doug had noticed this about Brendan before -- the man got quieter when something mattered more, which was the opposite of most people Doug knew, including himself. "I mean -- okay. Look." He rubbed the back of his neck with one hand. "I gave my toast first. You remember that."

"I remember."

"I worked on that toast for two weeks. I showed it to Megan. I showed it to a friend of mine. I timed it. I planned where I'd pause, where I'd let myself get emotional." He stopped rubbing his neck and folded his hands together between his knees. "I cried at the reception. You saw that. Everybody saw that. And I'd also cried in rehearsal, at the exact same spot. Both times. The same line, the same pause, the same --" He made a small gesture with his hand. "Production."

Doug looked at the coffee table. He looked at the book lying face-down on it. He could see part of the cover, something abstract in blue and white. He couldn't read the title from this angle. This bothered him in a way that was completely out of proportion, a small unknowable thing in a room full of larger ones.

"After my toast," Brendan said, "you stood up. And I remember thinking -- I had the exact thought, sitting back down -- 'He's going to say something short. He's going to congratulate them and sit down.' Because that's what I would have done. If I were --" He stopped. Doug watched him navigate a sentence with edges. "If I were at the wedding, in your position. If I'd been through what you'd been through, and I was sitting there at the table, and I'd just watched someone else give the toast everyone loved, I would have said 'Congratulations to Kelsey and Tyler' and I would have sat the hell down. That's all I'd have been able to do."

The room was very quiet. Megan's hands tightened around her cup, a pressure Doug could see in the way her fingers shifted against the ceramic, though her face was unchanged.

"I couldn't have done what you did," Brendan said. "And I don't mean the toast. I don't mean the jokes or the stories or any of the specific things you said. I mean standing up. Standing up in front of a room full of people who knew -- who all knew the situation, who knew that I'm --" He stopped again. The quiet version of Brendan was working hard now, choosing words the way Doug chose them when he was filing a complicated claim and every term had legal weight. "In front of people who had already decided what your story was. And saying anything at all. Anything. I couldn't have done that. I wouldn't have had --" He shook his head. "I just wouldn't have."

Doug stared at the book on the coffee table. The blue and white cover. The title he couldn't read. Behind him, from somewhere deeper in the house, he could hear the kitchen faucet dripping -- a slow, rhythmic sound, a loose washer or a worn valve seat, something a homeowner would notice and fix on a weekend, and Brendan probably already had a plan for it, and the plan would work, because Brendan's plans worked.

Brendan had said the first thing, in all their encounters across twelve years of shared custody logistics and holiday overlaps and the single beer in the living room, that Doug could not categorize. It was not generous. It was not condescending. It was not the practiced kindness of the man who had won, or the warmth of someone who was good at making people comfortable, or the careful diplomacy of the replacement husband who understood his position in the triangle. Brendan was telling Doug that he saw, in the drunk and ruinous toast, not the comedy or the pathos or the viral content but the act itself. The standing up. And Brendan, who had the wife, the house, the career, the composure, the tears that arrived at the rehearsed moment -- Brendan was saying he didn't have whatever it was that made a man stand up in a room where everyone already knew he'd lost.

Doug turned this over the way he turned a claim with unusual documentation, looking for the inconsistency, the angle where the story wouldn't hold. He couldn't find one. Brendan meant it. The man who had everything was telling the man who had a two-bedroom apartment and a Civic and a bowl of cereal most nights that losing required something that winning didn't, and he was saying it on his own sofa, in his own house, with his wife three feet away, and he was sincere.

Doug did not know what to do with this.

"The lamb was really good," Doug said.

Brendan looked at him. Megan looked at him. Doug looked at his empty coffee cup, which was small and warm and sat in his hands like something he had been given and hadn't earned.

"I mean it," he said. "The harissa. With the lemon. It worked."

Something shifted in Brendan's face. Not disappointment. Not confusion. A settling -- the recognition that what he'd offered had been received and processed into the only form Doug could manage, which was a statement about food. A specific, factual, evaluative statement about a lamb chop. This was what Doug had. This was what he could do.

"Thanks," Brendan said. He leaned back. "I'll send you the recipe."

"Okay."

Megan set her coffee down and stood. "Let me wrap up some leftovers for you," she said. The sentence was ordinary and also the most transparent rescue Doug had ever witnessed, and he was grateful for it with a gratitude he would never mention to anyone alive.

She went to the kitchen. Doug heard the refrigerator open, the tear of aluminum foil, the specific domestic sounds of someone packaging food in a kitchen they knew by heart, and these were sounds he had heard Megan make a thousand times during their marriage -- foil pulled from the roll, the drawer shut with a hip, the particular rhythm of wrapping something up -- and he was hearing them now from a room away, in another man's house, and the distance between the two versions of those sounds was twelve years and a court filing and the undeniable fact that the kitchen she moved through now was better than any kitchen they had shared.

Doug stood. Brendan stood. They were on opposite sides of the coffee table with its face-down book.

"Thanks for coming," Brendan said. "I mean it. I know this was..." He searched. "A lot."

"Thanks for doing it," Doug said, and he meant the dinner and the lamb and the wine and the thing Brendan had said and the not making any of it worse than it needed to be.

"Anytime," Brendan said, and he meant it, and Doug found even this generosity exhausting, because the man followed through on everything, including kindness.

Megan came back with a foil-wrapped package in a plastic bag. She handed it to Doug. The bag was warm. She walked him to the hallway, to the front door, to the threshold where his visit had started and now ended.

"Drive safe," she said. Her hand rested on the doorframe. The gold earring caught the hallway light. "And Doug?"

He turned.

"It's all right to not have it figured out yet. The performing, the fame, all of it. It's all right to still be working on it."

She said it the way she said everything -- considered, composed, delivered from a position she'd already arrived at. But underneath the structure there was something less organized, something that was just a woman in a doorway talking to a man she'd been married to for eighteen years, and whether it was genuine or rehearsed or the residue of a closeness that had never fully evaporated, Doug could not determine. He didn't have enough information to assess it. He might never have enough.

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

He walked to the Civic. The driveway was dark, the sealed concrete smooth underfoot. He set the warm bag of leftovers on the passenger seat, on top of the coffee stain, and sat for a moment with his hands on the steering wheel. The house glowed behind him, its windows lit. Brendan and Megan were in there, probably clearing the coffee cups, probably beginning the quiet post-mortem that couples performed after a social event that had involved a difficult guest. Doug was the difficult guest. He was the situation being managed, the claim being processed, the variable that Megan had organized and Brendan had tried to reach and that nobody in the house, including Doug, fully understood.

He drove through Upper Arlington, through its quiet streets with their maintained lawns and sealed driveways and houses where people ate lamb on a Tuesday. He turned onto the highway and then into Clintonville, where the houses were closer together and the porches had the particular cluttered look of people who used them. He parked in the lot behind his building, where the pavement was cracked and the lines were faded and the space was his because he always parked in the same one.

He carried the leftovers inside. He set them in the refrigerator, which contained a half-gallon of milk approaching its date, a block of cheddar with one corner going dark, and a jar of mustard with maybe one more serving before it was empty. The warm foil package sat on the middle shelf and looked like it had been sent from a different life.

He stood in the kitchen. His kitchen. The light over the stove was on -- he left it burning at night because the apartment felt less empty with one light going, which was not something he had ever said to anyone or planned to. The kitchen was small. The counter was laminate, beige, a color that had been chosen by someone who didn't care what color the counter was and would never be asked. There was no island, no stone, no copper pots. There was a toaster with a broken lever that he had to hold down manually, and a coffee maker that took seven minutes for four cups, and a cereal bowl drying in the rack that was the same cereal bowl he used every night.

Brendan could not have done what Doug did. Not the toast. Not the performance. The standing up. The being there, drunk and finished and surrounded by people who knew the whole story, and opening his mouth anyway. Brendan, who practiced his tears, who rehearsed where to pause, who had the life and the kitchen and the wife and the steady hands -- Brendan was saying he did not have whatever was in Doug that had produced that moment. And he was not saying it to be kind. He was saying it because it was true, or because he believed it was true, and Doug could not tell the difference because there might not be one.

Doug did not know how to value this. He was a man who assessed damage for a living, who documented loss, who determined what things were worth after the disaster had already happened. He knew how to price a ruined floor, a burst pipe, seventeen drowned roses. He did not know how to appraise what Brendan had given him, which was the suggestion that losing required something winning didn't, and that Doug had it, and that this was not pity or performance but an observation from a man who was, in his own careful way, admitting to a gap in his own coverage.

He unwrapped the foil. The lamb was still warm. He ate it standing at the counter, in his kitchen, under the stove light, with his hands.

It was still good.


# Chapter 9

Doug had practiced the set fourteen times. He knew this because he'd kept a log — not intentionally, not in a spreadsheet, just hash marks on the back of a Mutual Fidelity envelope that sat on his kitchen counter between the toaster and the jar of pens he never used. Fourteen times pacing between the bathroom and the kitchen, which was roughly eleven steps and approximately the distance Simone had said a small stage would give him. The first five times he'd read from the pages she'd printed out and annotated in her small, exact handwriting — *pause here*, *this is where they laugh*, *don't rush the transition*. The next five from memory, checking the pages only twice. The last four he hadn't needed the pages at all. Twenty-five minutes of material, organized into three sections with callbacks and what Simone called a "button" at the end, which was comedy terminology for a closing line that left the audience feeling finished.

Doug could deliver all twenty-five minutes to his refrigerator with confidence.

The refrigerator had never been to an afternoon session on pharmaceutical sales incentives. The refrigerator had never consumed four drinks at an open bar. The refrigerator had not come to the keynote expecting to see the guy from the wedding toast video, the one the internet had declared a comic genius, the one eighty million people had watched and decided was saying something important about fatherhood or marriage or American masculinity or whatever the latest thinkpiece had settled on. The refrigerator was a 2019 Kenmore with a dent on the lower left panel where Doug had hit it with a suitcase, and it had been an excellent audience.

Layla had called the night before. "Okay, so, quick rundown. It's Astelline Pharmaceuticals, they do like anti-inflammatories or something, I don't know, it doesn't matter. Regional sales conference, about four hundred people, they've been in sessions all day and they want to be entertained. You're the after-dinner keynote. They've seen the video, they love you, they're primed. Thirty minutes, in and out. Fifteen K. You're gonna be great."

"What if I'm not great," Doug had said.

"Then you're still getting paid fifteen thousand dollars, babe. Think about that."

Doug had thought about it. Fifteen thousand dollars for thirty minutes. He made sixty-one thousand a year before taxes at Mutual Fidelity, which came to $29.33 an hour if you divided by the standard two-thousand-and-eighty-hour work year, which Doug had once done on a slow Tuesday. Fifteen thousand dollars for thirty minutes was five hundred dollars a minute. It was $8.33 per second. Every second he stood on that stage, whether he was funny or silent or dying in front of four hundred strangers, was worth what his turkey club at the sandwich place cost. This did not make him less nervous. It made him nervous and aware that his nervousness was expensive.

He drove to Indianapolis on a Thursday afternoon in late April, two and a half hours on I-70 through landscape that transitioned from Ohio's version of flat to Indiana's version of the same flat with different highway signs. He'd left at three, telling Janet he had a personal appointment, which was technically true in the way that all insurance language was technically true — the statement was accurate and the picture it painted was incomplete. He listened to the radio for the first hour and turned it off because every song either had too much energy or reminded him of something and he couldn't afford either.

The Indianapolis Convention Center was a building that looked like it had been designed by a committee whose only instruction was "large." Doug parked in a garage that charged eighteen dollars, which was not unreasonable for downtown parking but which he noted because he noted the cost of things. It was how his mind organized the world. Some people noticed architecture or other human beings. Doug noticed that the parking garage had a rate structure that charged by the half-hour for the first three hours and a flat fee after, and that the flat fee was a worse deal if you were staying less than four and a half hours, which he intended to.

Inside, the convention center smelled like carpet cleaner and the particular staleness of air that had been circulated through the same building for three days of corporate programming. Banners for Astelline Pharmaceuticals hung from the ceiling — a teal logo, a tagline he couldn't read from this distance, and a photo of a woman smiling while doing yoga, which he assumed had something to do with the anti-inflammatory product line. People in lanyards moved through the hallways with the purposeful drift of the professionally conferenced, carrying tote bags filled with branded items they would never use.

A woman met him at the backstage entrance. Her name was Denise. She was the events coordinator, and she had a clipboard, a headset looped around her neck, and a blister on her left heel that she was trying not to favor.
