Doug could tell because she shifted her weight to the right every few seconds and winced slightly when she forgot about it. She walked him down a corridor with exposed pipe runs along the ceiling and gray industrial carpet that was a different, worse gray than the carpet in the public areas.

"So we've got you on at eight," Denise said. "Dinner wraps around seven-thirty, then there's a little bit of remarks from our VP of sales — Brian, he'll be quick, he's been told to be quick — and then he introduces you. Sound good?"

"Sure," Doug said.

"The video screens will have your name up. You want any slides or anything?"

"No."

"Great. There's water on the podium and we've got a handheld mic or a lavalier, whatever you prefer."

"Handheld," Doug said, because Simone had told him to use a handheld. She'd said it gave you something to do with one of your hands.

"Perfect." Denise checked her clipboard, flipping to a page she'd already looked at. "Is there anything you need? Green room's got some snacks, water, sodas. I think there's a fruit plate that's been sitting there since this morning, so maybe skip the fruit plate."

"I'm fine," Doug said.

"Okay great. I'll come get you about five minutes before." She paused. Her expression shifted into something less professional and more person-shaped. "My daughter watches your video like once a week. She's fourteen. She thinks you're hilarious."

Doug didn't know what to say to this. "That's nice of her."

"She made her stepdad watch it and he didn't think it was that funny, which she took as a personal attack on her judgment." Denise smiled in the way people smile when they're telling you about their family and don't have time to tell you about their family. "Anyway. Five minutes before. I'll come grab you."

Doug sat in the green room, which was not green but beige, and which was not a room so much as a partitioned section of backstage with a folding table, four chairs, a mirror ringed with bulbs of which three were dead, and the fruit plate Denise had warned him about. The grapes had moved through ripe and past resigned into something approaching geological. He didn't touch them.

He could hear the ballroom through the wall. Four hundred people, their collective sound arriving as a generalized hum — the same frequency as any crowd in any large room, many conversations producing a single indistinct noise. It was like standing next to a machine he couldn't see. He tried to pick out individual voices and couldn't. They were all one thing. In forty minutes he would stand in front of that thing and try to be funny, and it would either laugh or it would produce the other sound, the one that was patience disguised as silence.

He took the pages out of his jacket pocket. The material. Twenty-five minutes, printed on three sheets in twelve-point type because Simone had told him he shouldn't need to squint. He looked at the first line. He knew the first line. He knew every line. The problem was not the lines.

His phone buzzed. A text from Simone: *Don't rush the opening. Let the room come to you.*

He typed: *OK.*

A second text: *You know this material. Trust it.*

He put the phone facedown on the table. Through the wall, the crowd hummed. Someone laughed — a big laugh that carried through the partition and arrived in the green room as a muffled bark. It was the sound of someone being funny in front of those four hundred people, and that someone was Brian the VP of sales delivering his pre-dinner remarks, or someone at a table telling a story about their regional numbers, or a man doing an impression of his boss, or any of the thousands of small human interactions that produced laughter without effort or preparation or fourteen rehearsals between a bathroom and a kitchen.

Doug looked at the fruit plate. He picked up a grape and squeezed it between his thumb and forefinger. It did not give.

---

Brian, the VP of sales, turned out to be a large man with the energy of someone who had been told to keep it brief and resented the instruction. He wore a suit jacket over a polo shirt, which Doug noticed because it was a choice that committed fully to neither formality nor casualness and achieved a third thing that was worse than both. Brian did six minutes of remarks about regional performance metrics and market penetration trends and then pivoted to the introduction with the practiced ease of a man reading from a teleprompter while pretending he wasn't.

"So, uh, we've got a really special treat tonight," Brian said, and Doug, standing behind the stage-left partition, could hear the speech through a monitor speaker mounted on the wall. "I think most of you have probably seen the video — I mean, who hasn't, right? Eighty million views, the whole thing. And I just wanna say, as someone who gave a best man speech at my brother's wedding that, uh, did NOT go viral — I've got nothing but respect." Laughter. Brian was getting more laughs in his introduction than Doug was going to get in his entire set, and Doug knew this the way he knew a claim was going to be denied before he finished reading the documentation — the evidence was already organized, he just hadn't written it up yet.

"Please welcome, the man, the legend, the guy your wife has been showing you on her phone — Doug Bridge!"

Doug walked out.

The applause was genuine and warm, the sound of four hundred people who had already decided they liked him. The lights were brighter than the green room or his apartment or anywhere he had ever practiced, and they turned the audience into a dark, applauding mass with individual faces visible only in the first three rows. The mic was heavier than the one Simone had borrowed for their sessions at his kitchen table. It was warm from Brian's hand. Doug held it and walked to the center of the stage and the applause faded and the room waited.

"Thank you," Doug said. "Thanks for, uh. Thanks."

Good start.

He looked at the audience. Front row: a table of eight, conference badges still on, water glasses and wine glasses in various states of empty. A man with a red tie who was already smiling, committed to the evening. A woman with reading glasses pushed up on her forehead who looked ready to laugh. These were friendly faces. Doug focused on the man in the red tie and tried to speak to him, the way Simone had suggested — find one person and talk to them.

"So Layla — that's my manager, which is a sentence I still can't believe I'm saying — she told me this was a pharmaceutical sales conference." Pause. The room waited. "And I work in insurance. Which means we're both in the business of telling people why the thing they thought was covered isn't actually covered."

A laugh. Not a big laugh. A recognition laugh — the sound of people who got the reference and appreciated the effort without being surprised by any of it. It was fine. It was what Simone had said a first joke should do: establish his presence, signal to the room what kind of speaker he was going to be. The problem was that the room had already decided what kind of speaker he was going to be, and it wasn't this.

He moved to the second bit. This was about risk assessment — how insurance adjusters and salespeople both spend their careers calculating the odds that something will go wrong, except salespeople bet it won't and adjusters bet it will, and this fundamental difference in outlook was also a pretty good description of his first marriage.

Nothing.

Not hostile nothing. Not awkward nothing. Just the sound of four hundred people waiting for the thing they came for, which was not this. The man with the red tie was still smiling, but it was the same smile, held in place like a light fixture. The woman with the reading glasses had taken them off her forehead and put them on, which meant she was now looking at Doug with the clarity of corrected vision, and Doug was not improved by clarity.

He could feel it. The room deciding. It wasn't sudden — it was like watching a claim file accumulate documentation, each piece of evidence building toward the same conclusion. The audience was polite. They had been flown here, or driven, and their company had paid for the dinner and the drinks and the conference and the keynote entertainment. They were disposed to enjoy themselves. They wanted him to be good. Doug could feel them wanting it, and their wanting was worse than hostility because it meant they were going to keep trying, and he was going to keep failing, and both sides were going to pretend none of this was happening for as long as the social contract held.

He started the third section. This was the one about claims — specific claims he'd processed, the absurdity of the situations, the way people's small disasters revealed how they lived. Simone had told him this was his strongest material. Sitting across his kitchen table, she had laughed at these stories. She'd written notes in the margins. She had said, "That's good. That's the real thing."

Doug told the story about the man in Westerville whose above-ground pool wall had split open and flooded his neighbor's yard. He described the damage assessment, the coverage question, the way the neighbor's homeowner's policy covered weather events but not what the industry classified as "third-party structural failure." He was giving them the setup. The button — that was Simone's word for the payoff, the closing beat — was about how the pool owner and the neighbor had been friends for eleven years and the friendship ended not over the flood itself but over the question of whose insurance company should pay for the rosebushes.

He delivered the button. A few people laughed. A cluster at a table in the middle made a sound that was generous. But a woman in the third row — not the woman with the reading glasses, a different woman, younger, blazer, conference lanyard — looked down at her phone. She didn't pick it up. She just glanced at it, the way you glance at a door when you're thinking about leaving a room. It lasted one second. Doug saw it and it was louder than anything else happening.

He was rushing now. He could hear himself — the sentences coming faster, the pauses collapsing, the space Simone had built into the material caving in as Doug tried to get through it before the room finished deciding about him. He skipped the section about depreciation. It was his bridge from the claims material into the personal material, and without it the transition was abrupt, a hard turn from insurance stories into stuff about his apartment, and he heard the abruptness as the words came out but couldn't fix it because the words were already happening and they were the right words in the wrong order with no room to breathe.

A man near the back of the ballroom coughed. It was a real cough, a throat-clearing — the man probably had a tickle from the convention center's three-day accumulation of recycled air and needed to cough and coughed. But in the room, in the gap between Doug's sentences, the cough expanded to fill the space and became the loudest sound in the ballroom. Doug heard it the way he heard water damage behind drywall — the small specific signal that told you the problem was bigger than what was visible.

He tried the bit about his apartment. The cereal dinners. The Ikea bookshelf. He'd rehearsed this section and it had sounded fine in his kitchen, but "fine in rehearsal" was a phrase that meant nothing, like "pre-existing condition" or "adequate coverage" — technically descriptive and practically useless. The audience gave him something on the cereal line. Not a laugh. A sound. A collective half-exhalation that acknowledged what he'd said and signaled that they were still there, still trying, still willing to give him the benefit of whatever this was. It was generosity and it felt like charity and Doug could not tell the difference.

He repeated a line. He didn't intend to — he'd already said it in the claims section and now it came out again in the apartment section, the same phrase about "assessing the damage," and he heard it leave his mouth and recognized the repetition and the recognition sat in his chest like a piece of food that hadn't gone down properly. The audience didn't react. They may not have noticed. Or they had noticed and were being kind about it, which was functionally the same as not noticing except worse.

He was at nineteen minutes. He had six minutes of material left. He had the section about Roy, which Simone had liked, and the closer, which was structured to end on a coverage-gap metaphor that was supposed to land as both funny and genuine and give the audience the sense of completion that a performance was supposed to provide.

Doug looked at the room. The room looked back. The man in the red tie had stopped smiling. The woman with the reading glasses had her arms crossed, not in hostility but in the way people cross their arms when they've settled into waiting. The phone woman had put her phone away, which might have been encouraging except that putting it away was a commitment to sit through the rest, not a sign of engagement. The cougher had not coughed again. The ballroom was suspended in the particular stillness of an audience that wanted the thing to be over without wanting to be the one to end it.

"I think that's probably enough," Doug said.

Someone in the second row started clapping. The sound spread through the room the way all polite applause spreads — not a wave but a series of individual decisions to participate in the fiction that the performance had concluded on its own terms. Within three seconds it was a full room of applause. It was respectful. It was sustained for approximately the right duration. It was the exact volume and length appropriate for a keynote speaker who had done his best and fallen short, and every person in the room understood this and Doug understood they understood it and they knew he knew and the applause continued anyway because that was what you did. It was the social contract holding.

Doug said, "Thank you," and walked offstage and the lights came up and four hundred pharmaceutical sales representatives began the complicated process of deciding whether to feel sorry for him or irritated that the entertainment hadn't delivered or generous enough to pretend it had been fine.

Backstage, Denise was waiting with a water bottle. "Great job," she said, and the speed with which she said it — quickly, brightly, the verdict composed before the evidence was in — told Doug everything.

"Thanks," Doug said.

"Brian's gonna do a couple minutes of wrap-up and then we're done for the night. There's a shuttle to the hotel at nine-fifteen if you need it."

Doug did not need the shuttle. He needed to be in his car, alone, driving east on I-70 toward Columbus, but it was nearly nine and he had a room at the Holiday Inn Express on West Washington Street and a pizza he'd ordered on his phone during Brian's introduction, which meant the pizza would arrive before he did, which meant it would be sitting in a hallway getting cold, and he thought about this with the specific focus of a man who could not think about what had just happened and so thought about pizza instead.

---

The Holiday Inn Express on West Washington Street was four stories of beige siding with a parking lot that was three-quarters empty. Doug's room was on the third floor. The hallway smelled like the hallway of every hotel he'd ever been in, which was not many — he was not a person who stayed in hotels — but the smell was universal: cleaning solution, carpet adhesive, and the faint residual presence of every person who had ever walked this corridor with a keycard and a particular reason for not being home.

The pizza was outside his door, in a box on the carpet. He brought it in. It was from a place called Tony's Original that he'd found on his phone while Brian was talking about Q2 sales targets. The pizza was cold. Not room temperature — cold in the way that food gets when it sits on thin carpet in a hotel hallway in late April in Indiana. The cheese had congealed into a single continuous surface, and the crust had stiffened into something between bread and the box it came in. Doug ate two slices standing at the desk, which was bolted to the wall, because there was nowhere else to stand that wasn't the bed or the bathroom. The pizza was not good. It was adequate, in the way Roy used that word — a classification that contained neither complaint nor praise but simply placed the thing in its correct category and moved on.

The room had a king bed, a desk built into the wall, a television mounted at an angle that suggested someone had once tried to steal a previous one, and a bedspread with a pattern. Doug looked at the pattern. Overlapping diamonds in brown and a color that the manufacturer had probably called "sage" on the order form but which was the color of nothing, or the color of a decision made in a conference room by people whose job it was to select bedspread patterns for three hundred and seventy Holiday Inn Express locations and whose mandate was: give offense to no one. The pattern achieved this. It was inoffensive the way polite applause was inoffensive — it acknowledged the situation without responding to it. Doug sat on the bed and the mattress received his weight with the precise lack of resistance that institutional mattresses were engineered to provide. Not comfort. Not discomfort. The mattress equivalent of "fine."

He sat there. The bedspread's sage diamonds arranged themselves around his thighs. The clock on the nightstand said 9:47. There was a lamp bolted to the nightstand, bolted because someone at Holiday Inn corporate had determined that guests could not be trusted with portable lamps, and this struck Doug as a reasonable assessment of human behavior and also the kind of decision he would have made if he were in charge of assessing the risk profile of hotel guests, which, in a way, was not that different from what he did for a living.

In Columbus, Roy would be getting ready for bed. Roy went to bed at 9:45 every night, a fact Doug knew because Roy had mentioned it once, without elaboration or justification, the way Roy mentioned everything — as information, not as an opening for discussion.

Doug took out his phone and called Roy. It rang four times.

"Hello."

"It's Doug."

"I know. Your name comes up."

"Right." Doug paused. "Did I wake you up?"

"No. I was reading. There's a book about the Dust Bowl that's better than you'd expect."

"Huh."

"How'd it go?"

Doug looked at the bedspread. He looked at the pizza box on the desk. He looked at the lamp bolted to the nightstand and the television bolted to the wall and the desk bolted to the wall and the whole room, which was bolted to the earth and the earth was bolted to nothing and everything was, when you assessed it properly, attached to something that was itself barely holding.

"Bad," he said.

"Bad how?"

"Bad like everyone was polite about it after."

Roy was quiet for a moment. Doug could hear something in the background — a page turning, or maybe just Roy's house being the particular kind of quiet that Roy's house was, which was the quiet of a place where one person lived in a way that required very little of the space.

"Polite is worse than bad," Roy said.

"Yeah."

"You know where you are with bad."

Doug didn't say anything.

"I stopped at nineteen minutes," he said, after a while. "The set was twenty-five. I got to nineteen and told them that was probably enough."

"Was it?"

"Was it enough? I don't know. It was enough for me. I don't think they would've fought for six more minutes of what I was giving them."

"Then nineteen was the right number."

Doug lay back on the bed. The pillow was flat in the way hotel pillows are flat — compressed by the sequential weight of strangers into a shape that accommodated everyone and suited no one. He stared at the ceiling. It had one of those textured surfaces, tiny bumps that looked like a frozen sea viewed from altitude. A water stain in the far corner, brown at the edges, roughly circular. Doug assessed it reflexively: old damage, treated but not properly remediated. No active leak. Someone had painted over it and called it done. The most common mistake in residential damage repair — cosmetic treatment applied to structural failure. You couldn't see the problem anymore, so the problem was solved. Except it wasn't.

"I got paid fifteen thousand dollars tonight," Doug said.

"For nineteen minutes."

"The contract was for thirty. I did nineteen."

"That's still a lot of money for nineteen minutes."

"Seven hundred and eighty-nine dollars a minute. I did the math in the parking garage."

"Of course you did."

They were quiet for a few seconds. Doug could hear Roy breathing, steady and unhurried, the way Roy did everything.

"Are you driving back tonight?"

"No. I've got a hotel room. Holiday Inn."

"How is it?"

Doug looked at the room. The bolted lamp. The bolted television. The desk that was part of the wall. The pizza box with its grease-translucent lid. The bedspread pattern that had surrendered during its own design process.

"It's a Holiday Inn in Indianapolis," Doug said.

"Right."

They sat on the phone. The silence between them was the kind of silence they shared at their adjacent desks at Mutual Fidelity when neither of them had anything to say and neither felt the obligation to produce something — the silence of two people who had been in proximity long enough that the absence of conversation was a form of conversation. It was adequate.

"Roy."

"Yeah."

"The material doesn't work. Simone and I put together twenty-five minutes, and it's structured, and it's got callbacks and a closer that's supposed to land. And I can do it for my kitchen. I've done it fourteen times for my kitchen. But it's not — it isn't the thing."

"What thing?"

"The thing from the video. The thing they came for. They came to see the guy from the toast and I can't — " Doug stopped. He picked up the cold pizza crust from the nightstand where he'd set it and looked at it and put it down again. "I can't do it on purpose. The video was — I was drunk. I was angry about Brendan's toast being so goddamn perfect and I said things I never meant to say out loud. You can't rehearse that. You can't put it in a set list."

"No."

"Simone knows. She knows the material isn't the real thing. But she doesn't know what else to try, and I don't know what else to try, and tonight I stood up in front of four hundred people and did the material and it was like — " Doug looked at the ceiling. The water stain. The cosmetic repair. "It was like filing a claim you know is going to be denied. All the documentation is right, and the forms are filled out, and the loss is real, and you submit it and you know it's coming back rejected because the underlying thing isn't covered. The structure is sound and the answer is still no."

"You should get some sleep," Roy said.

"Yeah."

"Drive back in the morning. Don't drive tonight."

"I won't."

A pause. Then Roy said, "Doug."

"Yeah."

"Nineteen minutes is nineteen more than most people manage."

Doug wasn't sure if this was comfort or observation or just Roy's way of placing a fact in its correct location before closing the file. With Roy, the distinction was often academic.

"Good night, Roy."

"Night."

Doug hung up and set his phone on the nightstand, next to the bolted lamp. He took off his shoes and his dress shirt and lay on the bedspread in his undershirt and pants. He did not get under the covers. Getting under the covers would be a concession that this was where he was sleeping tonight — a Holiday Inn Express in Indianapolis with a cold pizza and a stain on the ceiling and the specific residue of having stood in front of four hundred people and been not enough — and he was not ready to concede it even though it was true and the evidence was documented and the claim, such as it was, had been filed.

He stared at the ceiling and thought about the performance. Not the whole thing — he couldn't hold the whole thing in his head at once, the way you couldn't look at all the damage in a flooded house simultaneously and instead had to move room to room, assessing each space on its own terms. He thought about the woman looking at her phone. One second. One glance downward. He thought about the cough that filled the silence like water finding the low point. He thought about the polite applause spreading through the ballroom — each person individually reaching the same conclusion and documenting it with their hands. He thought about Brian in his suit-jacket-and-polo saying *the man, the legend* to a room that wanted a legend and got a man.

He thought about the toast. He hadn't watched the video in weeks. He'd stopped because each viewing made it less like something that had happened to him and more like something that had happened to a different person who shared his face and his voice and his specific complaints about his ex-wife's new husband's toast. The drunk man in the video had no idea he was being watched. That was the whole thing. That was what made it work and what made it unrepeatable — the total absence of awareness, the complete vacancy of performance. The man in the video was saying true things because the whiskey had dissolved the membrane between what he thought and what he said, and there was no audience in his mind, just the room and the people and the things he'd carried for years that suddenly had nowhere to be except out loud. You couldn't get there sober. You couldn't get there by trying. You couldn't get there by standing between a bathroom and a kitchen fourteen times and making hash marks on an envelope.

Doug had tried to be the man in the video. For nineteen minutes, in front of four hundred people who wanted him to be that man, he had tried, and the trying was the thing that killed it. The toast had worked because it hadn't known it was a performance. The keynote had failed because it couldn't be anything else.

He lay on the bedspread and the sage diamonds pressed into the backs of his arms. The room was quiet. Outside the window, Indianapolis was doing whatever Indianapolis did at ten o'clock on a Thursday night in late April. Doug could hear traffic, faintly, and the air-conditioning unit cycling on with a rattle in its housing that suggested a maintenance issue someone had decided wasn't worth fixing. He knew the sound. He'd heard it a hundred times in claim inspections — the small mechanical complaint that would become a large mechanical complaint if ignored long enough, but which, for now, was just the sound of a system doing its job imperfectly, which was the sound of most systems, which was the sound of everything.

The fifteen thousand dollars would arrive as a direct deposit within fourteen business days, per the contract he'd read in full because he was a man who read contracts. It would land in his checking account alongside his Mutual Fidelity paychecks and look like any other money, because money was money, and fifteen thousand dollars earned standing in front of strangers in a ballroom being politely endured spent the same as fifteen thousand dollars earned sitting at a desk processing claims, which was both a comfort and not.

He closed his eyes. The television remote was under his hip, pressing its buttons into the meat of his thigh. He did not move it. The pizza box sat on the bolted desk. His dress shirt was draped over the bolted chair. His shoes were on the floor, placed neatly next to each other because Doug placed his shoes neatly even in rooms he would leave in the morning and never return to. This was not a virtue. It was a habit. It was who he was, and who he was had not been enough tonight, and the room held this fact the way rooms hold all the facts that happen inside them — without opinion, without comfort, without assessment.

Down the hall, the ice machine dispensed a load into its bin. The sound was mechanical and sudden — a clatter of cubes falling into a steel basin — and then the compressor hummed and the machine settled and the hallway was quiet again. No one had requested the ice. No one was coming for it. The machine produced it anyway, on a timer, through the night, whether anyone needed it or not.


# Chapter 10

Doug drove to Dayton on a Saturday because Saturdays were when Gloria expected him and because Gloria did not accommodate deviations from her expectations. The drive was seventy miles on I-70, flat and straight, the fields on either side still brown but showing the first green suggestion of spring, as though Ohio were considering it but hadn't committed. Doug's Civic needed an oil change. The indicator light had been on for eleven days and he'd been meaning to deal with it but hadn't, and the orange glow on the dashboard was the kind of small failure he understood — quantifiable, fixable, ignorable for a while longer.

He was not thinking about Indianapolis. He was specifically and deliberately not thinking about the Holiday Inn bedspread or the nineteen minutes that should have been twenty-five or the polite applause that was worse than silence. He was thinking about oil changes and exit numbers and whether he should have brought Gloria flowers, which he hadn't, because the last time he'd brought Gloria flowers she had said they were the wrong kind and set them on the counter and never mentioned them again.

Maple Glen Senior Living looked like what it was — a one-story complex off Route 35 with a parking lot that was always half-empty and landscaping that someone maintained without enthusiasm. The sign out front had a maple leaf in the logo. There were no maple trees on the property. Doug had pointed this out to Gloria once. She had said, "There's a motel on Route 40 called the Paradise. There is no paradise on Route 40."

The woman at the front desk was named Angela. She wore a lanyard with a photo ID that showed a version of herself smiling more broadly than the one behind the desk. She said, "Here to see your mom?" the way she said it every time, and Doug said, "Yes, thank you," and signed the visitor log. The hallway smelled like institutional carpet and something that was supposed to be lavender but had given up halfway.

Gloria was in her room, sitting in the recliner that she'd brought from the house in Kettering when she moved in three years ago. The recliner was brown. The room was beige. Gloria was wearing a blue cardigan that Doug remembered from at least 2018 and a pair of white sneakers that were so clean they looked ceremonial. She was watching the local news with the closed captions on because the volume on her television had been set to a level that satisfied her and did not change regardless of programming.

"You're late," she said.

Doug looked at the clock on her nightstand. It was 11:07. He had said he'd be there at eleven.

"Seven minutes," he said.

"Your father was never late."

"I hit traffic on 35."

"Harold drove on 35 for thirty years and was never late."

Doug sat on the edge of her bed because the only other chair had a stack of crossword puzzle books on it that had achieved structural integrity and he did not want to be responsible for dismantling them. The bed was made tight enough to suggest military training that Gloria had never received.

"You look thin," she said. "Are you eating?"

"I eat."

"You eat cereal. Cereal is not eating. Cereal is what you give children when you don't want to cook."

"I eat other things."

"Name one thing."

"I had a sandwich yesterday."

Gloria considered this. "What kind?"

"Turkey."

"From where?"

"The place near work."

"The place with Roy."

"Yes."

"He's a strange man, Roy. Not bad. Strange."

Doug waited. He knew why she'd wanted him to come this weekend specifically, and he knew she'd get there, and he also knew that rushing Gloria was like trying to rush an insurance claim through underwriting — it did not speed the process and it annoyed everyone involved.

"I watched it," Gloria said.

She didn't say what. She didn't need to.

"How many times?" Doug asked.

"Four. The first time to see what the fuss was about. The second time because I missed part of it when Ruth called me in the middle of it. The third time because Patricia wanted to see it on my television because hers is too small. The fourth time because I wanted to make sure I was right about what I thought the first time."

"Were you?"

"I'm always right about what I think the first time. That's why I think it."

She picked up a notepad from the arm of the recliner. It was a lined notepad from the Dollar General, the kind with a spiral binding at the top, and Doug could see her handwriting on it — small, precise, tilted slightly to the right. Gloria had taken notes. She had watched her son's drunken wedding toast on the internet and she had taken written notes.

"Your posture," she said. "You were bent over the whole time. Shoulders forward, chin down. Like you were trying to make yourself smaller. Your father stood straight when he talked, even when he was saying something stupid. You stood like you were apologizing for being there."

Doug said nothing.

"What you said about Brendan. About his toast being" — she glanced at the notepad — "'the kind of speech you give when you've been practicing in the mirror.' That was mean. He didn't deserve that." She paused. "But you weren't wrong. I watched his toast too. He cried at exactly the right moment. Nobody cries at the right moment. He's a surgeon. Surgeons rehearse. That's the whole job."

"He didn't rehearse his toast."

"He rehearsed. Trust me."

She turned the notepad over. There was more on the back.

"The part about the table," she said, and her voice changed — not softer, because Gloria's voice did not soften, but slower, which for Gloria was the closest thing. "What you said about sitting at that table and knowing nobody was going to sit across from you again and that you'd made your peace with it but that making your peace with something isn't the same as being at peace."

Doug's hands were on his knees. The bedspread under his palms was thin and pilled and he pressed into it.

"That was real," Gloria said. "Everything else — the whiskey talking, the crowd laughing, the business with the microphone — that was noise. But the table. That was the first honest thing you've said in front of other people since you were a boy."

She put the notepad down on the arm of the recliner and squared it against the edge the way she squared everything.

"Are they paying you?" she asked.

"For what?"

"For whatever it is you're doing now. The speeches. The television."

"Yes."

"How much?"

Doug considered not answering. He considered it for approximately one second, which was the maximum amount of time deception survived in Gloria's presence.

"Fifteen thousand for the one in Indianapolis."

Gloria's eyes moved slightly — the calculation was nearly visible, the arithmetic of a woman who had balanced household budgets for forty years on Harold's GM salary and could tell you what a gallon of milk cost in any year you named.

"For how long?"

"Thirty minutes. It was supposed to be thirty minutes."

"And how long was it actually?"

"About nineteen."

"So they paid you fifteen thousand dollars for nineteen minutes of talking."

"Yes."

"How much do you make at the insurance company in a month?"

Doug told her.

She nodded. The nod contained a complete financial assessment and something that was not quite approval but was adjacent to it in the way that Gloria's emotions were always adjacent to their names but never exactly where you expected them.

"That's decent money," she said. "For standing up there looking like you wanted to be somewhere else." She studied him. "Did it go well?"

"No."

"How bad?"

"People clapped because they felt sorry for me."

"That is bad."

"Yes."

"But they still paid you."

"They still paid me."

Gloria folded the notepad and set it on the arm of the recliner. "We should go to lunch," she said. "It's pork chop day. The pork chops are terrible but Ruth gets upset if we're late and she's saving seats."

---

The Maple Glen dining room had the acoustic quality of a room where sounds didn't land properly — forks on plates, the hum of a heating system that was still running in late April even though the weather outside had turned mild, conversations held at the volume of people who had lived long enough to stop worrying about being overheard. The carpet was green. The tables were round, six chairs each, and every table had a small vase with a plastic flower in it — the same plastic flowers that had been there every time Doug visited, slightly dusty, aggressively cheerful in a room that was neither.

Ruth was already at the table. She had saved four seats by placing her handbag on one chair and a folded newspaper on two others, and she waved at Gloria with the urgency of a person directing an aircraft onto a carrier deck. Ruth was eighty-one, wore clip-on earrings — gold circles that she adjusted every thirty seconds whether they needed it or not — and had been married three times, which gave her opinions about relationships the weight of field research.

"He's here," Ruth said to Gloria, as though Doug were a delivery she'd been tracking.

"He's here," Gloria confirmed.

"I saw you on the computer," Ruth said to Doug. "My grandson Nathan showed me. He's in Cincinnati. He put it on his phone and then we watched it on the computer because the phone is too small and I can't see anything on those phones, I don't know how anyone does."

"Did you," Doug said. He sat down.

"You were funny. I didn't know you were funny. Gloria never said you were funny."

"Gloria doesn't think I'm funny."

"Gloria doesn't think anyone is funny. I thought you were funny. You should do one of those dancing shows. The one where they put regular people with the professionals. You have a nice presence."

Gloria made a sound that was technically a response but functionally a door closing.

Patricia arrived with her lunch tray already loaded — pork chop, mashed potatoes, green beans, a roll, a cup of coffee that she held with both hands even when the tray was on the table and there was no practical reason to hold it that way except that Patricia held her coffee with both hands the way other people crossed their arms. She was seventy-nine, a retired English teacher from Dayton Public Schools, and she still corrected people's grammar reflexively — a habit she either didn't notice or had decided was a public service. She wore a red turtleneck and her reading glasses on a beaded chain around her neck, and she looked at Doug with the measuring expression of a woman who had spent thirty years grading.

"I watched the video," Patricia said. "Twice."

"Everyone watched it twice," Gloria said. "Or four times."

"It's a cry for help," Patricia said. She sat down and placed her napkin on her lap with the precision of a woman setting a table for the dean. "The entire performance is a cry for help. The drinking, the public confession, the humor used as a deflection mechanism for genuine emotional distress. It's textbook."

"It's not textbook," Gloria said. "There's no textbook."

"There are many textbooks. I read one last month about emotional avoidance in adult men. The patterns are quite clear—"

"Doug doesn't need a textbook. Doug needs to eat." Gloria looked at Doug's tray. "Is that all you're eating?"

Doug had taken a pork chop, potatoes, and a dinner roll. "This is plenty."

"Take more potatoes. They put butter in them. It's the only thing they do right here."

Deirdre was the last to sit down. She moved carefully but not uncertainly — a tall woman, thin, with white hair that she wore pinned back and the posture of someone who had spent her working life sitting up straight and had never stopped. She was eighty-three. She had been a bookkeeper for a car dealership in Springfield for thirty-one years and had moved to Maple Glen after her husband Ernie died and her house became, in her words, too many rooms for one person. She wore a green wool sweater with a small moth hole near the left cuff that she either hadn't noticed or had decided wasn't the sweater's fault. Her eyes were pale blue and they didn't miss things.

"Hello, Doug," she said.

"Hello, Deirdre."

She unfolded her napkin. The table settled into the rhythm of eating, which at Maple Glen meant the specific pattern of forks and talk and the pauses between talk that were comfortable rather than empty, the silences of people who had known each other long enough that silence wasn't a thing to be filled.

"Ruth thinks you should go on a dancing show," Gloria said to Doug.

"I heard."

"Ruth is wrong. You can't dance. You couldn't dance at your own wedding. You shuffled."

"That's true."

"Your father couldn't dance either. It's not a criticism. It's hereditary."

Ruth adjusted her earrings. "I'm just saying he has a nice presence. On the video. People like him."

"People liked him because he was drunk," Patricia said, taking a precise bite of her pork chop. "That's not the same as presence. That's disinhibition."

"I wasn't that drunk," Doug said. He was that drunk. Everyone at the table knew he was that drunk. Gloria didn't even bother to look at him.

"Eight whiskeys," Gloria said. "You said it in the video. Eight. Your father drank bourbon and he never had more than two. Three at Christmas."

"I don't usually drink that much."

"You don't usually do anything. That's your whole situation." Gloria cut her pork chop into precise squares. She ate pork chops the way she did everything — methodically, completely, without apology. "You do the same things every day in the same order and then one night you do something completely different in front of people with cameras."

"It was about a hundred and forty people."

"It was on the internet. It was eighty million."

Doug ate his potatoes. The mashed potatoes at Maple Glen were actually decent — Gloria was right about the butter — and he focused on them with the attention of a man who preferred potatoes to conversation. Ruth talked about her grandson Nathan's job in Cincinnati, which involved something with computers that she described as "he does the internet, I think he makes the websites." Patricia mentioned that her daughter in Chicago had called twice last month, which Patricia reported with a specific count and no commentary, though the count itself was the commentary — twice in a month was not enough and everyone at the table knew it and nobody said it. Deirdre ate quietly and nodded at things other people said, which was her version of participating and which carried more agreement than most people's whole sentences.

It was Ruth who brought the conversation back. "What did your daughter think?" she asked Doug. "About the video."

Doug put his fork down. "She wasn't thrilled."

"Of course she wasn't thrilled," Patricia said. "It was her wedding. Her father got inebriated and—"

"Patricia," Gloria said.

Patricia stopped. She picked up her coffee cup with both hands and held it like a verdict she was keeping to herself.

"Kelsey is fine," Gloria said. She said it to the table but she was looking at Doug. "She's her father's daughter. She'll be angry and she won't tell anyone why until it's too late. That's the Bridge way. We hold things and hold things and then drop them in the wrong room."

Doug didn't answer. He ate his roll. It was dry and needed butter but he wasn't going to get up to get some because leaving the table meant the conversation would continue without him and he didn't trust what Gloria would say in his absence. He could hear the dining room around them — a man two tables over laughing at something, a woman asking for more water, the particular ambient hum of a building where fifty people lived and ate and sat and waited.

"I thought it was sad," Deirdre said.

The table went quiet. Not silent — the dining room around them continued its low-grade murmur — but their table went quiet, which was different. When Deirdre spoke, people listened, because Deirdre speaking was an event. She could go an entire meal saying nothing and her presence still registered more than Ruth's commentary or Patricia's diagnoses.

"I thought it was the saddest thing I've ever seen," Deirdre said. She was looking at her plate, not at Doug. Her fork was resting on the edge. "And also funny. Both at the same time. I didn't know those could be the same thing, but they were."

Doug felt something in his chest that was not a tightening or a catch or any of the words people used for this. It was more like the feeling he got sometimes when he read a claim and the documentation was honest — a person describing exactly what happened to their house, their car, their property, without exaggeration or strategy, just the truth of the loss — and the honesty of it made the loss more real, not less. Deirdre had seen something he hadn't intended anyone to see, and she'd said it the way she said everything, which was plainly and only once.

"Deirdre's right," Gloria said. And that was the end of it. She returned to her pork chop. Ruth started talking about a program she'd watched about the royal family. Patricia corrected Ruth's pronunciation of "Buckingham." The table moved on, the way tables do, carrying the weight of what was said underneath the ordinary business of eating and talking and being alive on a Saturday in April.

---

After lunch, Doug walked Gloria back to her room. The hallway was wide enough for two people and a wheelchair, which was the architectural statement of Maple Glen — everything built for the body's eventual limitations, every doorway a little wider than necessary, every handrail bolted to the wall at exactly the right height. Someone had put Easter decorations on the bulletin board near the nurses' station — paper eggs in pastel colors and a construction-paper rabbit that looked like it had been made by someone who had a general idea of rabbits but hadn't committed to the details.

Gloria sat in her recliner. Doug sat on the bed again. The crossword books hadn't moved. Through the window he could see the parking lot and beyond it a strip of grass that ended at a chain-link fence, and beyond the fence the back of a Wendy's.

"Do you want coffee?" Gloria asked.

"I'm fine."

"I have instant. It's terrible but it's what I have. Harold used to make real coffee. After he died I switched to instant because there was no point in making a whole pot for one person, and I've been drinking terrible coffee for fifteen years and I don't see any reason to change now."

"You could get one of those single-cup machines."

"I don't want a machine. I want terrible coffee or no coffee. Those are my options and I've chosen."

Doug leaned back against the wall. The wall was cool through his shirt. Gloria's room was small — bed, recliner, dresser, a closet with a sliding door that stuck halfway. On the dresser were three framed photographs: Doug's parents' wedding photo, a picture of Kelsey at her high school graduation, and Harold in his backyard, standing next to a row of tomato plants, squinting into the sun with the expression of a man who was satisfied with his tomatoes and did not require the photograph to validate this.

"Your father," Gloria said.

She said it the way she said most things — without transition, without preamble, as though the previous conversation had ended and this new one had already started without needing anyone's permission.

"I want to tell you something about your father. Not because it's a secret. It was never a secret. It just never came up, and now it's coming up."

Doug waited.

"The Novaks had us over for dinner. This was — I don't know the exact year — 1989 or 1990. You were in high school. Frank and Jean Novak. They lived on Ackerman, the yellow house with the fence that was always leaning. Frank had just retired from GM. Early retirement. He'd been talking about it for two years — all the things he was going to do. Travel, learn to paint, fix the fence, plant a garden. He had a list. He brought up the list at every dinner party we went to for two years."

Gloria paused. Not for effect. To remember.

"So we're at dinner. Jean made a ham. It was always ham at the Novaks'. There was a salad and dinner rolls and Frank had bought wine, which was unusual because Frank didn't usually buy wine, but he was retired now and he was trying things. Frank starts talking about the list again. He was going to go to Spain. He and Jean were going to go to Spain. Everyone at the table is nodding and smiling and asking questions about Spain, and Harold is sitting there eating his ham and not saying anything. Which wasn't unusual. Harold didn't talk at dinner parties unless he had something to say, and he usually didn't."

She looked at the photograph on the dresser. Harold and his tomatoes.

"And then Harold says — just says it, in the middle of the ham — 'Frank, you're not going to do any of that.' Just like that. Not angry. Not trying to be cruel. Harold wasn't cruel. He was just — he said what he saw. He told Frank, 'You're not going to Spain. You're not going to fix the fence. You've been making that list for two years because the list is easier than admitting you don't know what to do with yourself. And everyone here knows it, and nobody's going to tell you, so I'm telling you.'"

Gloria's hands were still in her lap. Her voice hadn't changed register. She was telling it the way you'd tell someone what you had for breakfast — flat, factual, without performance.

"The room stopped. Jean put her fork down. The Millers — that was Donna and Jim Miller, they were there too, Donna had brought a dessert, a pie, I forget what kind — the Millers just sat there. Frank looked at Harold like he'd been slapped with something he couldn't see. And the worst part — I could see it on every face at that table — was that Harold was right. Everyone in the room knew Frank wasn't going to Spain. Everyone knew the list was Frank's way of not sitting with the truth of what retirement actually was, which was that he didn't have anything left and he was scared. Harold just said it out loud, at the dinner table, over the ham."

Doug didn't move. The bedspread was thin under his hands. He could hear the television murmuring next door, someone else's afternoon, someone else's room.

"We left early. Jean walked us to the door and said good night and she was furious — I could see it in how she held the door, right at the edge — but she was also something else. Something I didn't have a word for then. Maybe she was relieved. Maybe she was angry that she was relieved. I don't know. Jean was complicated."

Gloria looked at her hands. Her fingers were thinner than Doug remembered them being, the knuckles more pronounced. She wore the wedding ring she'd worn for forty-three years. She had not taken it off after Harold died and Doug had never asked her about it.

"In the car, Harold didn't say a word. He drove the whole way home — eighteen minutes, I know because I watched the clock — and he didn't say one word. He held the steering wheel at ten and two the way he always did and he looked at the road. He knew what he'd done. He wasn't sorry, exactly. He just knew. He knew it was the wrong place for the right thing, and he didn't know how to undo it and he wasn't sure he would have undone it if he could."

She stopped again. Doug could hear his own breathing.

"I reached over and held his hand. On the steering wheel. He didn't look at me. I didn't say anything. I held his hand for the rest of the drive and we pulled into the driveway and he turned off the car and we sat there for a minute and then he went inside and went to bed. We never talked about it. Not that night. Not the next morning. Not ever."

She picked at the arm of the recliner where the fabric had worn down to a smoother surface underneath, the spot where her left hand rested every evening.

"Frank never went to Spain," she said. "He sat in that house for fourteen years until he died. Jean sold the house to a family from Kettering. But that wasn't the point." She looked at Doug. "And I'm not going to tell you what the point is, because if I have to tell you, then you're not Harold's son, and you are."

She looked at the clock on her nightstand.

"It's almost two. You should go. The traffic on 70 gets bad if you wait."

Doug stood up. His legs had stiffened from sitting on the low bed with his back against the wall. Gloria stood too, slower, using the armrest, and for a moment he saw her body's negotiation with standing — the way she braced, steadied, settled into her feet — and he looked away because she would not have wanted him to see it.

"Thanks for lunch," he said.

"The pork chops were terrible."

"They weren't bad."

"They were terrible. Don't patronize me about pork chops." She straightened her cardigan. "Bring me those butter cookies next time. The ones from the German bakery on Far Hills. Not the ones from the grocery store. The grocery store ones taste like dust."

"I'll bring the cookies."

"From the German bakery."

"Yes."

"And get your oil changed. I could hear your car from the window when you pulled in."

Doug didn't think that was possible. He also didn't think Gloria was wrong about much. He leaned down and kissed her on the cheek. Her skin was dry and cool and smelled like the soap she'd used his entire life, a specific pharmacy brand in a green wrapper that he could never remember the name of and that he would recognize anywhere.

"Bye, Mom."

"Drive safe. Not fast. Safe. There's a difference and your father never learned it."

He walked down the hallway past the Easter decorations and the nurses' station where Angela was eating a yogurt and reading something on her phone. He signed out in the visitor log. The entry above his was from two days ago — someone named Hernandez, visiting room 14. The parking lot was warmer than when he'd arrived, the afternoon sun on the asphalt, and his Civic ticked with heat when he opened the door.

He sat in the car for a while before starting it. The oil change light was on, orange and patient. Through the windshield he could see the front of Maple Glen — the sign with the maple leaf, the parking lot, the landscaping, the building where his mother lived and ate terrible pork chops and told the truth to anyone who sat still long enough.

He started the car. The engine caught on the first try, which was more than his father's Buick had ever managed, though Doug didn't know why he thought of that now. He pulled out of the lot and turned east toward 70.

The drive home was seventy miles. Exit 38, exit 44, exit 52. The fields were green now, or getting there, the ground soft and uncertain the way Ohio was in spring — not committed, not convinced, but moving. He did not turn on the radio. He thought about his father driving home from the Novaks' in silence, his hands at ten and two, and his mother reaching across the seat. Eighteen minutes. He thought about the toast, and the table, and four hundred pharmaceutical reps in Indianapolis clapping because they felt bad for him. He thought about the Novaks' ham and about Frank's list and about the sound a room makes when someone says the thing that was underneath everything.

He drove. The oil change light glowed. He had his mother's eyes and his father's habit of saying the true thing in the wrong room, and he had not chosen either of these and could not return them, and the highway was flat and straight and exactly long enough.


# Chapter 11

They'd been at it for three weeks, four days a week, at Doug's kitchen table. Simone had given up on jokes somewhere in the first week, and what they did now didn't have a name, or at least not one Doug recognized from any category of professional work he'd ever participated in. She asked him questions. He answered them. She decided which answers were something and which were nothing, and the criteria for that decision remained, to Doug, as opaque as the underwriting guidelines Mutual Fidelity revised every January without telling anyone what had changed.

The recorder sat between them — a small silver thing she'd carried from New York, scratched along one edge, a strip of masking tape on the back where she'd written "WORK" in black marker, though Doug didn't know what other kind of recording she might need to distinguish it from. She turned it on every morning when she arrived at nine and off again somewhere around one or two, and in between she listened in a way that made Doug aware of the specific sounds his own voice made — the pauses, the filler words he hadn't known he used, the places where a sentence died and he had to start a new one from scratch.

The table was the same one he'd bought from the IKEA in West Chester twelve years ago when he'd moved into the apartment. Birch veneer, four legs, no distinguishing features. It had a water ring from a mug he'd set down sometime in 2016 and never dealt with. Simone put her coffee on the same spot every morning, directly on the ring, which Doug noticed and did not mention because mentioning it would require acknowledging that he noticed where she put her coffee, which felt like more than the situation called for.

"Tell me about the smell," Simone said. This was a Tuesday, the third week. She had her hair up, held with a pen she sometimes pulled out to write something and then stuck back, so that by noon her hair listed slightly to the left. She was wearing a flannel shirt with a button missing at the cuff, and she'd rolled the sleeves past her elbows. There was an ink stain on the inside of her left forearm that had been there since at least Thursday.

"What smell?"

"You said water damage has a smell. Yesterday, when you were talking about the house in Gahanna. You said you can tell how long the water's been standing by the smell."

"That's not — it's not really a smell thing," Doug said. "It's more of a timeline. You walk in and there's this —" He paused. He was looking at the table. "Okay. So within the first twenty-four hours, it smells like wet. Just wet. Like a basement, or a towel you left in the washer too long. And people are still in their houses at that point, still walking around, still thinking they can fix it with fans and a dehumidifier from Home Depot. The one that costs a hundred and forty dollars. Everyone buys that one."

"Okay."

"By forty-eight hours, if the water's been standing — in the walls, under the floorboards, in the insulation — it starts to turn. It goes from wet to something organic. Not rot yet. Pre-rot. It smells like earth, kind of. Like potting soil that's been sitting in a bag in the garage all winter."

"Pre-rot," Simone said.

"And then by day four or five, it's actual rot. You don't need to test for mold at that point. Your nose knows. The homeowner's been sleeping in that air for days and they've adjusted to it, the way you adjust to anything if you're in it long enough, but you walk in from outside and it hits you. It's sweet and musty and underneath there's something sharp, like wet cardboard that's been in the sun. And the homeowner is standing there asking if their floors can be saved, and you're breathing through your mouth trying to figure out the scope of the remediation, and the answer about the floors is almost always no, but you don't say that in the first five minutes."

Simone was writing something in her notebook. She didn't look up. "Why not?"

"Because they're standing on the floors. The floors they picked out. They're standing there in the house that smells wrong and the floors are soft under their feet and they already know. They just need you to not say it yet."

Simone stopped writing. She looked at Doug for a long moment. Her expression was the one she wore when she was deciding whether something was material or just a thing that happened. The distinction mattered to her in a way Doug couldn't fully follow — it seemed to involve some internal algorithm that processed what he said through filters he had no access to.

"That's good," she said. "Keep going."

"There's nowhere to go. That's what happens."

"Tell me about the dehumidifier. The hundred-and-forty-dollar one."

"It doesn't work. Not for real water damage. It's fine for a damp basement, but if you've got standing water in your walls, it's like trying to bail out a pool with a coffee cup. But people buy it because it's something to do. They need to be responding. They need to be the person who went to Home Depot and came back with equipment."

"Mm."

"I had a family in Reynoldsburg last year — March, pipe burst, five inches of standing water in the finished basement. They'd had it finished two years before. New carpet, new drywall, the whole thing. Twenty-two thousand dollars. I have the number because I looked at the original receipt. And they'd gone out and bought two of those dehumidifiers and a box fan and they had them all running in the basement and the husband was down there with a Shop-Vac and the wife was upstairs on the phone with her sister and the kids were at school and it was —" Doug stopped. He picked at the edge of the table's veneer where it had started to lift. "It was just a Tuesday. Just a regular day where everything they'd spent two years building was wrecked and they were trying to fix it with equipment that cost three hundred dollars total."

Simone sat back. She tapped her pen on the notebook twice — a habit Doug had catalogued along with the pen-in-hair thing and the coffee-on-the-water-ring thing. He was an adjuster. He noticed things. It was the only way he'd ever processed a room.

"What," Doug said.

"Nothing. That's material."

"It's a claim. I'm describing a claim."

"I know what you're describing." She crossed something out in her notebook and wrote something else. "What else do you know about that costs a hundred and forty dollars and doesn't work?"

Doug thought about this. "My reading glasses."

Simone's mouth moved but she didn't laugh. She wrote it down.


The material that worked came out sideways, always. Doug couldn't produce it on command — Simone had stopped asking him to be funny after the first week, when he'd sat through forty-five minutes of silence punctuated by two attempts at observational humor that she'd received with the expression of a physician hearing symptoms that didn't match the chart.

What worked was Doug talking about the things he actually knew. Insurance. The office. Roy. The specific mechanics of ordinary life as experienced by a man who'd been paying close attention to ordinary things for fifty-two years without realizing that attention was itself a quality.

On Wednesday, Simone asked about the cereal.

"What kind?"

"Raisin Bran. Store brand, not Kellogg's. Kellogg's is $4.89 and the store brand is $3.19 and they're the same cereal. Same raisins, same flakes, same sugar content. I've done the comparison."

"You've compared them."

"The Kellogg's has six more raisins per serving. I counted once."

Simone looked at him. "You counted the raisins."

"It was a Sunday."

There was a silence. Outside, the sound of a car door on the street, and then nothing.

"And this is dinner," Simone said.

"Most nights. It's fast, it doesn't generate dishes. One bowl, one spoon. You rinse them and you're done. A real dinner — a cooked dinner — is a plate and a pan and a cutting board and a spatula and by the time you've cleaned up, you've spent more time on the aftermath than on the meal. The ROI on cooking for one person is terrible."

"The return on investment of dinner."

"If you factored in your time at even minimum wage, every meal you cook alone costs about thirty dollars."

"You've done that math."

"Yes."

"Why is cereal better at night?" Simone asked. "That's the part I want. Not the economics. Why do you like it better as dinner than as breakfast?"

Doug looked at the water ring on the table. "Because in the morning it's what you're supposed to eat. You eat cereal for breakfast and it's just — it's breakfast. It doesn't mean anything. But at night, it's a decision. You're choosing not to cook. You're choosing not to order something. You're choosing the simplest possible version of a meal because the day is over and you don't need to perform anything for anyone, including yourself. Cereal at night is — it's honest. It's what dinner looks like when nobody's watching."

Simone didn't say anything for a long time. She was looking at her notebook but she wasn't writing.

"Yeah," she said. "That's something."

She asked about Roy's sandwich.

"Turkey on wheat. Mustard. No tomato. He's been ordering the same thing for nine years. One time they put tomato on it by accident and he peeled it off and set it on the edge of his tray and didn't mention it. He just removed it and ate the sandwich. The next time we went in, his order was correct. I don't know if he said something between visits or if the guy behind the counter noticed. It was never discussed."

"What does Roy think about all of this?"

"He didn't watch the video."

"I know. But what does he think about you doing this — the sessions, the Netflix thing, the whole trajectory?"

Doug considered this. "Roy thinks what he thinks. He doesn't — he's not going to have an opinion and then share the opinion with me so I feel something about his opinion. If I told Roy I was quitting to do comedy, he'd say 'Okay.' If I told him I was staying at Mutual Fidelity forever, he'd say 'Okay.' The okay would be the same both times."

"And that's comforting."

"I didn't say that."

"I'm saying it."

Doug looked at the water ring. "Yeah. It is."

This was the kind of moment that kept happening. Doug would say something about Roy, or about a claim, or about the unit price of store-brand raisins, and the room would shift slightly, like the air pressure had changed. Simone would be looking at him in a way that made him aware he'd given away more than he'd intended. Not more than he'd meant — he meant all of it. But more than he'd intended to put into the space between them.


The material that failed was simpler to diagnose but harder to stop producing.

On that same Wednesday afternoon, Simone asked Doug to tell her what it was like being famous.

"I'm not famous."

"You're on the internet. Eighty million views. People recognize you."

Doug tried. "It's — I mean, people come up to me. At the grocery store, or at the gas station. They want to take pictures. I don't mind it, it's fine, they're being nice. One guy at the Kroger in Clintonville asked me to do the toast for him, just right there in the produce section, and I said I didn't remember it, and he said 'Come on, man,' like I was holding out on him. I was buying bananas."

Simone was watching him the way she watched him when the real Doug was retreating and the narrating Doug was arriving. Doug could feel it happening — could feel the sentences becoming organized, could feel himself describing the experience of fame rather than being in the experience of fame, and the difference was total and deadly.

"That's —" Simone started.

"That's nothing. I know."

"It's not nothing. It's just not you. That's a story about being famous. It's not your story. Your story is the bananas."

"I don't have a story about bananas."

"You have a story about everything. You just told me you counted raisins on a Sunday. But when I ask you about fame, you give me 'people come up to me.' That's not Doug. That's what anyone would say."

The toast attempt was worse. Thursday of the third week, Simone asked him to talk about the toast directly.

"What do you want to know?"

"Anything. What you remember, how it felt, what you think about it now."

Doug took a breath. "It was at Kelsey's wedding. I'd had — well, the video. Brendan gave this toast that was —" He stopped. He could feel the shift happening, the thing Simone was always listening for: the moment when Doug stopped talking and started narrating. When his voice moved from the kitchen table to some middle distance where he was composing rather than speaking.

"He gave a toast that was really good," Doug said, and the air went out of it. Even he could hear it. The sentence sat there on the table between them like something left too long in the sun.

"Okay," Simone said. "And then."

"And then I got up and I —" Doug looked at his hands. "I don't really remember getting up. I remember the microphone was heavier than I expected. And I remember —"

"You're being boring," Simone said.

Doug stopped.

"You know you're being boring, right? You can feel it."

"I can feel it."

"What you just did — the part about the microphone, about Brendan's toast — that's you trying to tell the story of the toast. You're narrating. And when you narrate, you die."

Doug sat with this. Outside, a car passed with its windows down and something with bass coming from the speakers, the low pulse of it pressing against the kitchen window and then fading. April in Clintonville. The trees along the street had buds that looked swollen and irritated, like they hadn't decided whether spring was worth the effort.

"So what am I supposed to do with it?"

"Nothing. You're boring when you try to be interesting. That's the whole diagnosis."

"That's a terrible diagnosis."

"It's the most useful thing I've ever told anyone I've worked with." Simone uncapped her pen. "The insurance stuff, the cereal, the dehumidifier, Roy's sandwich — that's you. You're not trying to be anything. You're telling me what you know. And the gap between how seriously you take it and how strange it actually is — that's where the comedy lives. But the minute you try to talk about the toast, or about being recognized at the Kroger, or about what any of this means, you become a man with opinions about himself, and that man is —"

"Boring."

"Worse. Boring is generous. Self-commentary is where comedy goes to die. Everyone who tries to talk about their own fame becomes the same person. Doesn't matter who you are."

Doug felt something give — not in his chest, not in his throat, somewhere less anatomical than that. It was the feeling of a claim he'd been working for weeks that suddenly resolved. The documentation lined up. The coverage was clear. He was boring when he tried to be interesting. He was interesting when he wasn't trying. These were facts, as solid and specific as a depreciation schedule, and they were terrifying because the material that worked — the insurance stories, the cereal arithmetic, the Roy details — was his actual life. Performing that material in front of strangers meant letting them see the life. Not the viral-toast version, not the internet's Doug, but the real inventory: a fifty-two-year-old man who counted raisins on a Sunday because there was nothing else in the apartment that needed counting.

They broke for lunch. Simone went to the sandwich place around the corner — she'd been going there since her second week in Columbus and ordered a different sandwich every time, which Doug found inefficient. He ate a granola bar and drank water and stood in his kitchen looking at the Ikea bookshelf and the art print Megan had left behind, a watercolor of something that might have been a bridge or might have been a pier, the kind of art that committed to nothing, and it occurred to him that Megan had probably left it because she didn't want it, not because she was leaving him a piece of herself, and this thought arrived twelve years late and landed with the small, precise weight of something he'd always known.

Simone came back with a Cuban sandwich and a coffee. She set the coffee on the water ring.

"More insurance," she said.

Doug talked about depreciation. This was Thursday, or maybe still Wednesday — the sessions had started to bleed together, one afternoon into the next, the same table, the same recorder, the same quality of light shifting across the kitchen as the hours passed. He talked about what happens to the value of a couch over time — how a couch you bought for twelve hundred dollars is worth, after ten years, maybe two hundred, and the difference between what you paid and what it's worth now is called depreciation, and it's the most honest math in the world because it accounts for the fact that things wear out, that use diminishes value, that the couch you're sitting on is not the couch you bought.

"And that's how it works with everything," Doug said. "Not just couches. Appliances, roofing, carpeting, your water heater, your HVAC system. Everything has a schedule. Everything depreciates at a predictable rate. You can look it up. There are tables."

"And marriages?" Simone said. She said it casually, like it was the next item on a list, but her pen was still.

Doug looked at the recorder. "There's no table for that."

"But."

"But if there were." He paused. "If there were, you'd have your replacement cost and your actual cash value. Replacement cost is what it would take to get the same thing new. Actual cash value is what it's worth now, after everything. After the years and the arguments and the Tuesday nights and the way someone loads the dishwasher and the sound they make when they're sleeping and all of it. The actual cash value is what's left when you subtract the depreciation."

"And nobody puts that in the paperwork."

"Nobody puts that in the paperwork."

Simone wrote something. She didn't look up.


The second scene happened on a Friday. They'd been at it since nine and it was after four and the light in the apartment had changed — the flat white of morning had given way to the amber of late afternoon, angling through the kitchen window and laying a stripe across the table that had moved, over the hours, from the recorder to Simone's notebook to the empty space between their hands.

They were both tired. Simone had stopped taking notes an hour ago. The recorder was still running but neither of them was performing for it anymore — the thing about recording everything, Doug had learned, was that eventually you forgot the machine was there, and forgetting was the point. The machine was patient. The machine didn't need you to know it was listening.

Simone was asking about the apartment. Not the apartment as material — she'd already harvested the cereal, the bookshelf, the art print, the second bedroom with the boxes — but the apartment as a place a person lived.

"How long have you been here?"

"Twelve years. Since the divorce."

"And you moved here right after."

"Within a month. Megan — we sold the house. Split it. I found this place online. I didn't see it in person before I signed the lease. The pictures were accurate."

"That's a very insurance-adjuster way to pick an apartment."

"The pictures were accurate."

Simone smiled. Not the diagnostic smile she used when something was material. Just a smile. She was tired, and when she was tired her face relaxed out of its professional attention into something less deployed. She'd mentioned once, in a moment that wasn't part of any session, that she hadn't been sleeping well in Columbus because her body was still on New York time. It was only an hour's difference, but the hour mattered, she said. She'd been carrying that sleeplessness for three weeks and it showed in how she sat in the afternoons — less perched, more settled, as if fatigue had finally convinced her the chair was adequate.

"How did you know it was over?" Simone said. "Your marriage."

Doug looked at her. This wasn't a material question. The recorder was on but Simone wasn't glancing at her notebook. She was looking at him, and the quality of the looking was different from the diagnostic attention she'd been bringing to the insurance stories and the cereal and Roy's sandwich order for three weeks. This was a person asking another person a question because she wanted to know.

"The conversation, or —"

"Whatever it was. The moment that made it real."

Doug didn't answer right away. He could feel the pull toward narrating — toward telling the divorce story in the organized way Megan would tell it, with contextualized feelings and measured transitions. He could feel Simone watching him feel it. She was very still. She had a habit, when she sensed something real approaching, of going so quiet that the room seemed to pull inward around her silence.

"We'd been — Megan had been saying things for a while. About how she felt. About the marriage. She said it the way Megan says things, which is organized. She'd say, 'I think we need to acknowledge that this isn't working,' and I'd say, 'What isn't working?' and she'd look at me and I genuinely didn't know what she meant. Or — I knew. I could also truthfully say I didn't know, because knowing and understanding are different coverage levels and I was insured for one but not the other."

Simone didn't react to the metaphor. She waited.

"So she left. One day I came home from work and she'd taken things. Not everything. Her clothes, her toiletries, some books. She took the KitchenAid mixer — four hundred and twenty dollars, I never used it — and she left the art print." He gestured at the wall without looking at it. "We had different stuff by then. Separate accounts. She'd already been moving into the new place incrementally, and the day she took the last of it was just — it was a Wednesday. I came home and I could see what was gone. I walked through the rooms and I could inventory it."

"But."

"But I was fine. I was processing. I knew it was coming. I'd known for months. The paperwork was in progress. I was managing it."

The word sat between them. Managing.

"And then I took a shower. End of the day, I always shower when I get home, I still do. And I got in and her shampoo was gone from the ledge."

Simone did not move.

"I don't remember the brand. Something with green tea in the name. It came in a tall green bottle and she'd been using the same shampoo the entire marriage. Eighteen years. The bottle was always on the ledge. Every morning, every night. Even after she started talking about leaving, even after the conversations and the lawyers and the listing agreement — the bottle was there. It was the last thing of hers in the bathroom."

The apartment was quiet. Outside, late-afternoon sounds — a dog somewhere, the specific pitch of a lawn mower starting up and catching, the seasonal noise of Columbus deciding it was spring. The stripe of light on the table had reached Doug's hand.

"Everything else I could process. The mixer, the books, the clothes — those were items. I could inventory them, I could assess the depreciation, I could arrive at a number and the number would be accurate and I'd be fine. But the shampoo —" He looked at his hand in the light. "You can't depreciate shampoo. It's a consumable. It has no residual value. It's just gone, and the ledge where it sat is empty, and every time you take a shower you see the empty space on the ledge where it was. And after a while you don't see it anymore, because you adjust, because that's what you do, but for the first — I don't know. Six months. A year. Every shower."

He stopped. The recorder hummed between them, so quiet it was nearly the sound of the room itself.

Simone picked up her pen. She did not put it in her hair. She opened her notebook to a clean page and she wrote something. She wrote for a while — longer than she'd written for any single thing Doug had said in three weeks of sessions. She wrote in small careful letters that Doug couldn't read from across the table.

She didn't laugh. She didn't say "That's material" or "Keep going" or "That's something" or any of the phrases she'd been using for three weeks to sort his life into piles.

She wrote it down.
