Doug watched her write and felt the thing he'd been circling since the first session: the understanding that there was no wall between the material and the man. The insurance stories were his loneliness. The cereal was his loneliness. Roy's unchanging sandwich was his loneliness. And now the shampoo was his loneliness too, written down in Simone's notebook in her small letters, and everything in the notebook was material, and material was what went on stage, and what went on stage was what strangers would see, and the thing he'd spent twelve years sealing behind the measured, documented, depreciated details of his ordinary life was going to be visible to anyone who bought a ticket or pressed play.

He was going to be known.

The pen stopped. Simone looked up. Her eyes were red at the edges — fatigue, or something adjacent to fatigue, the way tired sometimes has other things packed inside it. She closed the notebook. She placed the pen on top of it. She did not put it back in her hair.

"We should stop for today," she said.

"Okay."

Neither of them moved for a while. The light had shifted past Doug and was climbing the wall behind him where the art print hung — the watercolor bridge or pier, the thing Megan hadn't wanted.

"I've never told anyone that," Doug said. "The shampoo."

Simone nodded. She reached forward and clicked the recorder off. The sound was small and precise.

"I know," she said.

They sat in the kitchen as the light went. Simone finished her cold coffee. Doug did not offer to make more because more coffee at five in the afternoon was not something he would think to offer, and Simone knew this about him by now, had absorbed enough of Doug's rhythms over three weeks of shared table to know that his hospitality had limits that were not about generosity but about the fact that certain things did not occur to him.

Outside, the lawn mower had stopped. The neighborhood had settled into that particular quiet of a spring Friday evening — not silence, but the soft negotiations of people arriving home, car doors and mailboxes and the specific distant television sounds of houses where other people lived with other people.

Simone gathered her notebook and her recorder and put them in the canvas bag she carried. The bag had a coffee stain on the bottom and a pin on the strap that said "WRITE DRUNK EDIT SOBER" — a Hemingway misquote she'd told Doug she hated but hadn't removed because a friend gave it to her and the friend died three years ago and the pin was the only thing of his she'd kept. She'd mentioned this once, in the second week, casually, the way she released personal details — in small pieces, unprompted, and then she moved on before the weight could settle.

"Same time Monday," she said.

"Yeah."

She stopped at the door. She didn't turn all the way around — half-turned, hand on the knob, bag over one shoulder.

"Doug."

"Yeah."

"The shampoo. I'm not going to shape it into a bit or slot it into a set. That's yours. But I want you to know that it's the most precise thing you've said, and precision is everything you've got. That's your whole mechanism — you notice the exact thing, not the general thing, and when you say it people recognize it as true, and recognition is what makes it work. That's the engine."

Doug stood by the kitchen table. He didn't say anything, because there was nothing to say that wouldn't diminish what she'd said. He'd learned this from years of standing in people's damaged living rooms — sometimes the only correct response to someone telling you something important was to let the importance stay in the air between you until it either dissolved or became permanent.

"See you Monday," he said.

The door closed. Doug listened to her footsteps on the stairs, the outside door, then nothing. The apartment was quiet the way it was always quiet — had been for twelve years — but the quality had changed. The recorder was gone. The notebook was gone. Simone's coffee cup was in the sink, which he'd wash later. Her pen cap — the cap, not the pen — sat on the table where it had rolled when she was packing up. He picked it up and put it on the counter and left it there.

He went to the bathroom. He didn't turn on the shower. He looked at the ledge. His own shampoo sat there — a drugstore brand, nothing with green tea in the name, $3.79 per bottle, roughly two and a half months per bottle at his rate of use. He'd calculated this once. The math was the only way he'd ever found to hold the things he couldn't hold any other way, to make them small enough and specific enough to fit inside the precise, measured architecture of his understanding, where everything had a value and every value had a schedule and the schedule told you what things were worth and you could live inside those numbers and the numbers would not leave.

He went back to the kitchen. He took out the box of store-brand Raisin Bran and poured a bowl and stood at the counter and ate it. The milk was two days from expiration but still good — he checked, he always checked — and the raisins were adequate and the bowl was exactly what dinner looked like on a Friday night when you'd just told someone the truest thing about your life and she'd written it down and not laughed, and you were going to have to decide what that meant, but not tonight. Tonight was cereal and a clean bowl and a rinsed spoon and the pen cap on the counter that you weren't going to move.

He washed the bowl. He washed the spoon. He washed Simone's coffee cup and set it in the drying rack next to his own.

He left the pen cap where it was.


# Chapter 12

Alex Chen arrived at Doug's apartment on a Tuesday morning in May carrying a canvas messenger bag with a fraying strap, a digital recorder, and a thesis. The thesis was that Doug Bridge represented something about contemporary masculinity — its failures, its accidental honesty, its unwillingness to perform. Alex had explained this over email in language that Doug read twice and understood about forty percent of. He'd agreed to the profile because Layla told him to and because saying no would have required a reason he didn't have.

Alex was thirty-one and had the specific intensity of someone who had chosen journalism when journalism was already dying and had committed to it anyway. He wore a denim jacket that was either vintage or just old — Doug couldn't tell — and he had a pen clipped to the breast pocket that he kept removing, chewing the cap, and replacing. The cap was destroyed, soft plastic cratered by months of thought. He also had allergies. He'd blown his nose three times before Doug got the coffee made, and he kept rubbing his eyes with the heel of his hand in a way that made him look like he'd been crying, which he hadn't. Columbus in May was a pollen event. Doug had processed a claim once involving mold remediation triggered by spring pollen accumulating in an HVAC system, which was a fringe case but not unprecedented.

"So," Alex said. He pressed record on the digital recorder and set it on the kitchen counter between them, next to a box of Grape-Nuts that Doug had not put away. "I want to start with something maybe a little abstract."

"Okay," Doug said.

"What drives you?"

Doug looked at the recorder. The red light was on. "I drive a Civic," he said. "2019. Bought it used with thirty-two thousand miles."

Alex smiled. He had a good smile — the smile of someone who believed his subjects were going to reveal themselves if he waited long enough. "No, I mean — what motivates you? What gets you up in the morning?"

Doug thought about this. His alarm was set for 6:40. He got up because it went off. Before the alarm, he'd been getting up because his bladder woke him around 6:15, but he'd adjusted his water intake after 8 p.m. and that had mostly resolved. He didn't think this was what Alex meant.

"I have a job," Doug said. "The job starts at eight. So I get up in time for the job."

Alex wrote something in his notebook. The notebook was a Moleskine, the kind you buy at bookstores, and its pages were dense with small handwriting that tilted left. Doug noticed the tilt because he'd processed a claim once involving handwriting analysis — a forged receipt, a guy in Reynoldsburg, nine hundred dollars on a pressure washer that didn't exist. The analyst had said left-tilting handwriting indicated introversion. Doug didn't know if that was true for Alex or for the Reynoldsburg guy.

"Tell me about the toast," Alex said.

"Which part?"

"How it felt. That night. Standing up there."

"I was drunk," Doug said.

"Right, but — "

"I'd had eight whiskeys. I don't normally have eight whiskeys. I don't normally have four. The bartender was pouring them tall because the venue was all-inclusive and he'd stopped measuring."

"But what made you stand up? What made you give the toast?"

"Brendan had just given one," Doug said. "My ex-wife's husband. His was very good. People cried."

"And that motivated you to — "

"I don't know if 'motivated' is the right word. I was already standing. Or I was sitting and then I was standing. I don't remember the transition."

Alex wrote something else. Doug could see, upside down, the word "transition" with a box around it. He wondered what Alex thought that word was going to mean in the profile. He had a feeling it was going to mean something different than what Doug meant, which was that he physically did not remember going from chair to upright.

They talked for another hour. Alex asked about Doug's childhood, his marriage, his divorce, his relationship with Kelsey, his job, his apartment, his daily routine, his feelings about fame, his feelings about comedy, his feelings about his feelings. Doug answered each question the way he answered coverage inquiries at work — with the specific information requested and nothing additional. Alex kept waiting for the additional part. Doug kept not providing it.

At one point Alex asked, "Do you think of yourself as funny?"

"No," Doug said.

"But eighty million people watched that video and laughed."

"Some of them were probably just watching. You don't know everybody laughed."

"The comments — "

"I've read the comments. Some of them think I'm an asshole. One guy said I should be arrested for public intoxication. He got twelve hundred upvotes."

Alex paused. He rubbed his eye again. His allergies were getting worse — the morning light through Doug's kitchen window had a visible quality to it, a fine yellow-green dusting on the sill that Doug wiped down every other day and which returned every other day, because that was what May in central Ohio did. Alex had stopped wiping his nose and had moved to a strategy of sustained sniffling that he seemed to think was less noticeable. It was not less noticeable.

"Can I use your bathroom?" Alex asked.

"Down the hall," Doug said. "Door on the left. The door on the right is the office."

"You have a home office?"

"It doesn't have a computer in it," Doug said. "It's got boxes."

Alex hesitated, clearly wanting to ask about the boxes, and then went to the bathroom. Doug heard him blow his nose four times, a sequence that built in intensity like a man trying to clear a drain. Doug sat at the kitchen counter and looked at the recorder. The red light was still on. It was recording the silence, the sound of the refrigerator humming, the muffled honking from the bathroom. Somewhere in a digital file, this would exist: Alex Chen's sinuses and Doug Bridge's Frigidaire, captured for journalism.

---

The plan was that Alex would shadow Doug for two days. The first day was the Tuesday, which was a work day, which meant Alex would see the office, and lunch with Roy, and a session with Simone in the late afternoon. Layla had choreographed this. She'd called it "giving him the full Doug experience," and Doug had not asked what she thought the full Doug experience was because he suspected the answer would involve the word "authentic" and he'd heard that word enough times from enough people who didn't mean the same thing by it.

They took Doug's Civic to Mutual Fidelity. Alex sat in the passenger seat and looked at the interior the way Doug had seen adjusters look at damaged property — assessing, cataloging, forming a judgment that would eventually become a number, except Alex's number was word count.

"How long have you had this car?"

"Three years. Well, three years and five months. I bought it in December."

"Do you like it?"

"It runs," Doug said. "The heat works. The AC works when it wants to. The passenger window sticks in cold weather but we're past that now."

Alex wrote something on his phone. Doug drove.

The Mutual Fidelity parking lot looked the way it always looked — gray, flat, half-full at 7:52 a.m. The building was a three-story rectangle that communicated nothing about what happened inside it, which was the processing of other people's disasters into financial outcomes. Doug had worked there for fifteen years and had never once thought the building was interesting, and it bothered him slightly that Alex was photographing it with his phone as if it signified something.

Inside, Doug introduced Alex to Janet, who shook Alex's hand and said, "We're very proud of Doug," which was the first time in fifteen years that Janet had expressed pride in anything other than the department's quarterly throughput numbers. Doug absorbed this the way he absorbed most unexpected information — as data that didn't fit the existing file, which meant he needed a new file, which meant more work.

Alex sat in a chair Doug borrowed from the break room and watched Doug work. Doug processed claims. A kitchen fire in Grove City — grease, not electrical, which mattered because the homeowner's policy had an exclusion for "appliance failure" and the insured was trying to argue that the stove's ignition system had malfunctioned, when the adjuster's site report clearly showed unattended cooking oil. Doug reviewed the photographs, noted the pour pattern on the stovetop, wrote his assessment. It took forty minutes. Alex watched the entire time. He sat with his notebook on his knee and his pen in his mouth and his eyes slightly red from whatever Columbus was doing to his sinuses, and Doug could feel Alex watching him the way he sometimes felt a claim file watching him — waiting for him to decide something.

"Is this typical?" Alex asked when Doug closed the file.

"It's a Tuesday," Doug said.

"I mean, is this what your days are usually like?"

"They're usually like this. Sometimes there's a site visit. Today there isn't."

A coworker named Diane Posner walked past Doug's desk, paused, and said, "My daughter showed me a TikTok of you. She's fifteen. She said you're iconic." Diane pronounced "iconic" the way someone might pronounce a medical diagnosis they'd just received — carefully, uncertainly, as if it might require follow-up. She was holding a yogurt cup and a plastic spoon, and she had the posture of a woman who had been eating yogurt at her desk so consistently and for so long that the yogurt had become structural — load-bearing, somehow, to the architecture of her workday.

"Tell her thanks," Doug said.

"She wants to know if you're on TikTok."

"I'm not."

"She says you should be."

"I don't know how it works," Doug said, and Diane nodded in a way that suggested this was both an acceptable answer and a missed opportunity, and she continued to her desk with her yogurt.

Alex wrote something in his notebook. Doug didn't look at what it was. He was fairly sure the word "iconic" was going to appear in the profile attributed to a context that made it sound more meaningful than Diane Posner's fifteen-year-old daughter and a TikTok he'd never seen.

Lunch was at the sandwich place. It had been the sandwich place for nine years, and it was called Sal's, though there was no evidence that a person named Sal was or had ever been involved in its operation. The menu hadn't changed since Doug started going there. The booth by the window hadn't changed. Roy hadn't changed. Roy ordered turkey on wheat, mustard, no tomato. Doug ordered ham and Swiss. They sat.

"Roy, this is Alex. He's writing a profile."

Roy looked at Alex the way he looked at most new information — with the measured disinterest of a man who had determined long ago that the vast majority of new information was not going to improve his day. "About what?"

"About me," Doug said.

"Huh," Roy said. He unwrapped his sandwich.

Alex had his recorder on the table. "Roy, can I ask — what was your reaction when you saw the toast video?"

"Didn't see it," Roy said.

"You never watched it?"

"No."

"Are you — can I ask why not?"

Roy took a bite of his sandwich. He chewed. He swallowed. The interval between the question and his answer was long enough that Alex shifted in his seat, which was something Doug had learned not to do years ago. You waited for Roy. Roy did not speed up.

"I don't watch videos," Roy said. "I barely watch television."

"But your best friend went viral. Eighty million people — "

"Doug's my coworker," Roy said. "He's also my friend. But he was those things before the video and he'll be those things after. I don't need to watch a video of him to know what he's like. I've been eating lunch with him for nine years."

"That's — actually, that's a really interesting perspective. Can I quote you on that?"

Roy looked at Doug. Doug knew the look. It was the look Roy gave when someone treated a plain fact as if it were a philosophical position.

"It's your article," Roy said.

They ate. Alex asked more questions. Roy answered them in the fewest possible words, which created a rhythm that Alex seemed to find charming and Doug found normal because it was normal — it was every Tuesday and every Thursday and most Mondays and Wednesdays too. At one point Alex asked Roy what he thought about Doug's newfound fame and Roy said, "I don't think about it," and Alex wrote this down as if it were a revelation instead of a description of the way Roy processed the world, which was by not processing most of it.

Alex asked Doug if his friendship with Roy had changed since the video. Doug looked at Roy. Roy was eating his sandwich. Nothing about Roy had changed since the video. Nothing about Roy had changed since Doug had met him. Roy was a fixed point.

"No," Doug said.

"Not at all? Even — "

"It hasn't changed," Doug said.

Alex wrote something down. Doug ate his sandwich.

The session with Simone was at Doug's apartment at four o'clock. They'd been meeting four days a week for over a month now, and the apartment had accumulated evidence of the work — index cards on the kitchen table with phrases scribbled on them, a digital recorder that belonged to Simone, a legal pad with notes in her handwriting that Doug couldn't read because it was worse than his, which he had not previously thought possible.

Simone was sitting at the table when they arrived. She had a coffee from somewhere and she was reading her phone with the expression of someone who had seen enough of the internet for one lifetime and was seeing more anyway. She looked up at Alex and then at Doug.

"He's the journalist," Simone said. Not a question.

"Alex Chen," Alex said, extending his hand.

Simone shook it. She had rings on three fingers — not jewelry, working rings, the kind that stayed on because taking them off was more effort than wearing them. Her hair was up, held in place by a clip that was losing its structural battle. "Layla told me you were coming. What's the angle?"

"I'm interested in Doug as a kind of — "

"A symbol?"

"I was going to say a case study in — "

"He's not a case study," Simone said. "He's a guy. We're trying to write material." She turned to Doug. "Sit down. Tell me about the thing from yesterday, the one about the deductible."

Doug sat. He was aware of Alex's recorder on the table next to Simone's recorder, two red lights blinking at slightly different intervals, like mismatched turn signals. He felt like a claim being documented from two angles.

He told the deductible story. It was about a man in Hilliard whose roof leaked during a storm and who had filed a claim not realizing that his deductible was five thousand dollars, which meant the first five thousand of damage came out of his pocket, and the total damage was fifty-two hundred, and the insurance company's portion was two hundred dollars. The man had called Doug to ask if this was a mistake. Doug had explained that it wasn't. The man had said, "I've been paying premiums for twelve years." Doug had said, "The premiums purchased the policy. The policy has a deductible." The man had been quiet for a long time and then asked, "So what did I pay for?" Doug hadn't had a good answer because the man was technically right — he'd paid for the idea that something would be there when things went wrong, and something was there, but the gap between the idea and the two-hundred-dollar check was a gap that Doug had spent his career standing inside of, explaining to people why it was the size it was.

Simone listened. She didn't interrupt. When Doug finished she said, "The last part. 'The gap.' That's the bit."

"It's not a bit," Doug said. "It happened."

"That's why it's the bit."

Alex watched this exchange and Doug could see him watching it — the slight forward lean, the pen cap in his mouth, the notebook open on his lap. Doug wondered what Alex saw. Two people at a kitchen table, one of them talking about insurance deductibles, the other one finding comedy in it. He suspected Alex saw something more significant than that. Alex seemed like a person who found significance the way Doug's apartment found pollen — it drifted in through every opening and settled on every surface and could not be kept out regardless of effort.

They worked for another forty minutes. Doug talked about claims and Simone found the moments where the mundane turned specific enough to be strange, or sad, or accidentally precise about something that wasn't insurance at all. Alex took notes. At one point Simone looked at Alex and said, "You're going to make this sound like therapy, aren't you."

"I'm just observing," Alex said.

"Observing is what therapists say right before they ask how that makes you feel."

Alex didn't respond to this. He wrote something down. His pen cap was in his mouth again. He had the look of a man who was composing sentences about what he was seeing, which was different from actually seeing it, and Doug wondered if Alex knew the difference. Journalists, in Doug's limited experience, were like adjusters — they assessed what happened and wrote it into a format that turned mess into narrative. The difference was that adjusters knew the narrative was a reduction. They knew the form left things out. Doug wasn't sure journalists knew that, or if they did, whether it bothered them the way it sometimes bothered Doug when a claim file flattened something real into a number and a determination code.

After the session, Doug and Alex drove to the Kroger on High Street because Doug needed groceries and the profile apparently required footage of Doug buying groceries. "I need eggs," Doug said as they walked in. "And bread. And coffee. The coffee I buy is on sale every three weeks on a cycle, and this is a cycle week, so the timing's good."

"You track the sale cycle?"

"It's a seven-dollar difference. Over a year that's about a hundred and twenty dollars. That's not nothing."

"Right. No, that's — yeah."

Doug walked the aisles. Alex walked next to him. The fluorescent light was the same as always — flat, even, the kind of light that made everything look equally unimportant. Doug put eggs in the cart, then bread, then the coffee, which was in fact on sale, $8.99 instead of $15.99. He picked up a bag of oranges because they looked fine and he'd been eating an orange at his desk every afternoon, something he'd started doing after the Indianapolis trip without thinking about why and had continued doing without thinking about why.

A college kid in a Buckeyes hoodie recognized Doug near the cereal aisle. He stopped, pointed, and said, "Oh shit. Wedding toast guy."

"Hey," Doug said.

"Can I get a photo? My roommate's gonna lose his mind."

"Sure."

Doug stood next to the kid. The kid held up his phone. Doug looked at the camera the way he looked at anything pointed at him unexpectedly — with the expression of a man who had been asked to identify himself in a lineup and was not entirely certain he could.

"Dude," the kid said, looking at the photo. "You look like you're getting arrested."

"I don't know what to do with my face," Doug said. This was true. He had never learned the specific muscular arrangement that photographs required. In every photo Doug had ever appeared in — wedding photos, work ID, driver's license — he looked like a person who had just been told something mildly concerning and was still processing it.

The kid showed the photo to Alex. Alex looked at it and then at Doug and wrote something in his notebook.

They checked out. The cashier, a woman in her sixties with reading glasses on a beaded chain and a name tag that said BEVERLY, scanned his items without looking up and said, "You need bags?" and Doug said, "I brought my own," and this exchange — ordinary, unnarrated, free of subtext — was the most normal thing that had happened all day. Doug was grateful for it in a way that he could not have explained to Alex and did not try to.

In the parking lot, loading grocery bags into the Civic's trunk, Alex asked the question Doug had been waiting for since the morning.

"How has fame changed you?"

Doug put the eggs in last because eggs went on top. This was not a philosophy. It was structural.

"I bought better cereal," he said.

Alex waited. Doug closed the trunk.

"That's it?"

"I used to buy store brand. Now I buy Grape-Nuts. It's not even that much more expensive. A dollar forty, maybe."

"And that's the only change."

Doug thought about it. He leaned against the Civic. The parking lot was warm — the first sustained heat of the year, the asphalt giving it back, the kind of afternoon where the air above the blacktop shimmered if you looked at it right. He thought about the money, which was real. The attention, which was real. The contract with Layla, the sessions with Simone, the Netflix deal, the pharmaceutical conference in Indianapolis where four hundred people had watched him fail and then clapped. He thought about the fact that a college kid had just called him "wedding toast guy" in a grocery store and would post that photo and people he'd never met would see it and form opinions about his face.

He thought about the cereal. The cereal was the only change he could point to and say: that is different. Everything else was either too large to describe or too subtle to name, and Doug did not describe things he couldn't measure.

"The cereal's the main thing," Doug said.

---

The profile published nine days later. Layla texted Doug the link at 6:47 a.m. with three exclamation marks and a fire emoji. Doug was brushing his teeth. He read it standing at the bathroom sink, his mouth full of toothpaste, because he didn't want to wait the thirty seconds it would take to spit and rinse. This should have told him something about how much he cared about what a stranger had written about him.

The headline was: **"America's Most Reluctant Comedian."**

The article was long. It was well-written. Alex could write — the sentences were clean and the observations were specific and the structure moved from the toast to the aftermath to the profile visit with a momentum that carried you forward the way good journalism did. Doug recognized the quality because he read the *Columbus Dispatch* every morning and had a sense for when reporting worked and when it was just organized.

The Doug in the article was charming. He was self-deprecating in a way that suggested quiet wisdom rather than the absence of anything more interesting to say. He was a "midwestern Everyman" — the phrase appeared in the second paragraph — who had accidentally "spoken truth to power" by getting drunk at his daughter's wedding and saying what every man of his generation wanted to say but couldn't. The article called him "the last honest man in America" in a subheading, which was not a phrase Doug would have used to describe himself or anyone, because honesty was not a competitive sport and if it were, Doug would not have placed.

The article described Doug's apartment as "spartan but deliberate, the home of a man who has decided what he needs and discarded the rest." This was generous. Doug had not discarded anything. He had moved in twelve years ago with boxes he hadn't unpacked and had continued living around them the way water goes around rocks — not as a decision, as a path of least resistance. The deliberateness the article attributed to him was the absence of deliberation, which was different, but Alex had not seen it that way because Alex was thirty-one and still believed that a man who owned very little had made a philosophical choice about ownership rather than simply not going to the store.

The article described the lunch with Roy as "a portrait of male friendship in its most elemental form — two men who have sat across from each other long enough that silence carries the same weight as speech." Doug read this twice. He thought about the actual lunch, in which Roy had eaten a turkey sandwich and said almost nothing and Alex had tried repeatedly to make Roy's nothing into something and Roy had let him because Roy did not care what anyone wrote about anything. The article had turned Roy's indifference into philosophy. Roy was not a philosopher. Roy was a man who ate turkey sandwiches and did not have opinions about things that didn't require opinions.

The article described the session with Simone as "a creative partnership that operates on the frequency of recognition — two people who have found, in each other, a collaborator who understands that comedy is not performance but excavation." Doug did not know what frequency of recognition meant. He and Simone sat at his kitchen table and he talked about insurance claims and she told him which parts were funny. It was not excavation. It was closer to sorting mail — most of it was routine, and every once in a while something landed that neither of them expected, and Simone would say "that works" and write it down and Doug would say "that's not a joke" and Simone would say "I know."

The article quoted Doug seven times. Three of the quotes were accurate in the way Doug understood accuracy — the words matched what he'd said and the context matched what he'd meant. The other four were accurate in the way insurance documents were sometimes accurate, where every fact was correct and the total picture was wrong. Doug had said "I don't know how to be a public person" while trying to explain why he didn't have a TikTok account. In the article, the quote appeared beneath a paragraph about the crisis of modern masculinity. It looked like a confession. It was a tech-support complaint.

The section about the grocery store made Doug sound like a character in a movie about a lovably humble man who still shops for his own groceries despite being famous. Doug had been buying his own groceries for fifty-two years. There was no "despite." The fact that the article needed a "despite" — needed the groceries to mean something beyond eggs and bread and coffee at $8.99 — told Doug everything about the distance between the profile's version of him and the actual him. It was the same distance that existed between the internet's version and the actual him, and between the Netflix version and the actual him, and the layers were accumulating now like coats of paint on a wall nobody looked at closely, each one covering the one before, and somewhere underneath all of them was the original surface, and Doug was starting to forget what color it had been.

Layla called at 7:30. "It's incredible," she said. "The engagement's insane. It's already been shared like forty thousand times."

"Forty thousand," Doug said.

"I've had three calls this morning. A podcast wants you. The literary agent's back about the book deal. And — okay, don't freak out — the *Times* wants to do a follow-up."

"The Dispatch — "

"The New York Times, babe. The *Times* Times."

Doug said nothing. He was still standing in the bathroom. The toothpaste had dried on the corner of his mouth. He looked at himself in the mirror — the same face, the thinning hair, the reading glasses on the back of the toilet where he'd left them two days ago, the face of a man who adjusted insurance claims in Columbus, Ohio, and had once gotten very drunk at a wedding. The mirror didn't know about the article. The mirror showed what it showed.

"Doug? You there?"

"Yeah," he said. "I'm here."

At the office, three people mentioned the profile before Doug reached his desk. Janet stopped him in the hallway and said, "I read it. Very thoughtful," in a tone that suggested she was commenting on a eulogy. A woman from accounting whose name Doug could not remember said, "My husband loved the cereal part." Doug thanked her. He thanked Janet. He sat down. He turned on his computer. He opened the claims queue.

Roy was already at his desk. His coffee was half-drunk. His computer was on. He had the same posture he'd had every morning for the entire time Doug had known him — slightly forward, shoulders level, the posture of a man who had found the precise angle at which work was tolerable and had never adjusted.

"Morning," Roy said.

"Morning," Doug said.

"I read the headline."

"Just the headline?"

"It was enough." Roy took a sip of his coffee. He set the mug down. "Three too many words."

Doug waited. He had learned a long time ago that Roy's pauses were not invitations to speak. They were structural, load-bearing, part of the sentence even though they contained no words.

"You're not a comedian," Roy said. "And you're not reluctant. You just haven't figured out how to stop."

Doug looked at Roy. Roy was already looking at his screen. He had moved on. The assessment was complete — delivered in the same flat tone Roy used to flag a discrepancy in a claim file, factual and final and not available for appeal.

Doug turned to his own screen. The claims queue had fourteen items. A burst pipe in Westerville, straightforward. A chimney fire in Dublin. A woman in Upper Arlington whose sump pump had failed during last week's rain — she'd filed her first claim that morning, and her documentation was thorough in a way that suggested fear rather than experience. She'd photographed the basement from four angles. She'd saved the receipt for the sump pump. She'd written a description of events in neat handwriting that went slightly frantic in the margins where she'd added details she'd remembered after the fact.

Doug opened the file. He started reading.

The phone's red light blinked — Layla, probably, or a podcast, or someone who had read the article and wanted something from the man described in it, the charming reluctant comedian with the spartan apartment and the midwestern silences and the quiet wisdom. That man did not exist. But the article was well-written and the internet would share it and people would read it and believe, the way they'd believed the toast, and the version would harden into another layer, and Doug would carry it the way he carried all the others — not as weight exactly, but as accumulating distance between himself and whatever he actually was, a distance that grew every time someone else described him and he recognized nothing.

He processed the claim. The burst pipe in Westerville was PVC joint failure, covered under the standard homeowner's policy, no exclusions applicable. He wrote his assessment. He entered the determination code. He moved to the next file.

The phone kept blinking. Doug didn't answer it. He had fourteen claims and the morning was already half gone and the woman in Upper Arlington was waiting to find out if her ruined basement was covered, which it was, and somebody needed to tell her, and Doug was good at telling people things like that. He picked up the phone. It was Layla. He put her on hold. He dialed the woman in Upper Arlington.


# Chapter 13

The Thai place was on a side street south of campus, a narrow storefront between a dry cleaner and a vacant lot that had been vacant for as long as Doug had been coming here, which was fourteen years. The awning had faded from red to something closer to the color of salmon left out overnight — a specific shade of defeated optimism that Doug noticed every time and never mentioned because there was no one to mention it to. The same ceramic cat sat in the window with one paw raised, though it had migrated from the left side of the sill to the right at some point in 2019, and Doug had registered the move without understanding it. A bell rang when he pushed the door open. It was Saturday, just past noon.

Kelsey wasn't there yet. Doug was twenty minutes early, the same way he'd been twenty minutes early at the diner in Worthington three months ago, and for every parent-teacher conference and dentist appointment and claims assessment in the decades before that. He showed up early the way other people showed up with opinions — automatically, reflexively, because the alternative was arriving to conditions you hadn't assessed.

The woman at the front — Doug had been seeing her for fourteen years and did not know her name, a failure he'd compounded annually until it became irreversible — pointed him toward the booth in the back without being asked. She was wearing the jade bracelet she always wore, and reading glasses that were new since the last time he'd been here, thin gold frames that she looked over rather than through as she walked him to the table. The booth's vinyl had cracked along the right side where it met the wall, a split that had started small and widened steadily, the way cracks did when nobody repaired them. Doug sat on the left side. He always sat on the left side. Kelsey sat facing the door, which she'd done since she was a teenager, and he'd never asked why.

He opened the menu out of habit, not need. He had been ordering the same thing here for fourteen years: the massaman curry, number thirty-seven, which came with a small red sticker on the menu indicating spice, though the menu's rating system stopped at three stickers and the massaman only had one, which Doug interpreted as manageable. It was not manageable. Every time he ordered it, the first bite was fine and the second was fine and by the fourth his sinuses opened and his eyes watered and a warmth moved through his chest that he attributed to the peppers and not to anything else, and he ate the entire bowl every time and drank three glasses of water and never switched to anything milder, because switching would mean acknowledging that he'd been ordering the wrong thing for fourteen years, and Doug did not acknowledge errors of that duration. He carried them forward.

The restaurant smelled the way it always smelled — fish sauce and hot oil and the lemongrass from the tom kha that someone at a nearby table was eating. The kitchen was visible through a narrow window in the back wall, and he could see hands moving, a wok flashing under the hood vent, steam rising. The music was the same instrumental loop it had always been — vaguely Southeast Asian, recorded on what sounded like a Casio keyboard, and it repeated every twelve minutes. He'd timed it once. Kelsey had been sixteen.

She came in at fourteen past noon. Not late — Kelsey wasn't late — but not early either, which for someone who'd driven two hours from Cleveland was a specific kind of punctuality that meant she'd sat in her car in the lot for a few minutes before coming inside. Doug knew this because he did it too. The Bridge approach to difficult encounters: arrive early, sit in the car, rehearse nothing because you don't know what to rehearse, then go in.

She was wearing a denim jacket over a white T-shirt, and her hair was down, which was unusual — at the diner in Worthington it had been pulled back tight, the work configuration, the she-was-there-to-do-something configuration. Down meant something different. Down meant Saturday. Down meant she'd thought about what to wear and decided on something that wasn't armor.

She slid into the booth across from him. She picked up the menu, looked at it for three seconds, and put it down. She had been ordering the pad thai, number fourteen, since she was fourteen, which she'd pointed out once as a coincidence and then corrected herself — she'd started ordering it at fourteen and now she was twenty-eight, so it was half her life, not a coincidence, just persistence.

"The drive was okay," she said, before he asked.

"Good."

"Seventy-one was fine. Construction past Medina but it wasn't bad."

"Medina. Yeah, they've been doing that stretch since —"

"I don't know. A while." She took a sip of the water the hostess had left. The glass had a hairline crack running from the rim to the midpoint, the kind of damage you'd document in a claim but that the restaurant had apparently decided was still within operational tolerance. "How's work?"

"Same. We're doing quarterly reviews next week."

"Exciting."

"It's not exciting. It's the same review I've had for eight years. 'Meets expectations.' Roy gets 'meets expectations' too. He considers it the correct rating."

Kelsey almost smiled. The almost was visible — the corner of her mouth moved and then decided against it, retreating to a neutral position like a quote that doesn't close.

"How's the practice?" Doug asked.

"It's teeth, Dad."

"Right."

The woman with the jade bracelet came to take their order. She wrote nothing down, which Doug found both impressive and faintly unsettling — he could not have processed a single claim without documentation, and the idea that this woman carried the details of multiple orders in her head with no backup suggested a confidence in her own memory that Doug did not share.

Kelsey ordered the pad thai. Doug ordered the massaman curry. The woman nodded and left.

"Tyler says hi," Kelsey said.

"Tell him hi."

"He wanted to come down. I told him this was a dad thing."

"Okay."

She turned the chopstick wrapper over in her hands, creasing it along one edge, then folding it in half, then half again. He watched her hands. The nail on her left ring finger was chipped, a crescent of bare nail showing through the dark red polish, and the wedding ring sat below it — a simple band, white gold, the one Tyler had proposed with. Doug had met the ring at dinner three days after the engagement and had assessed it automatically: white gold, approximately one carat center stone, four-prong setting, retail value probably thirty-eight hundred to forty-five hundred based on the cut grade, which he couldn't determine without magnification. He'd shaken Tyler's hand and said something adequate. He couldn't remember what.

"I read the profile," Kelsey said.

"The thing Alex wrote."

"'America's Most Reluctant Comedian.'" She said it flatly, without inflection, the way you'd read a claims number. "Roy was right about the headline."

"Roy's usually right about things he reduces to one sentence."

"It was a nice piece. It was wrong, but it was nice."

Doug took a sip of his water. "I know."

"You know it was nice or you know it was wrong?"

"Both."

The pad thai arrived first, which was normal — pad thai was faster than curry. The plate was the same oval ceramic it had always been, with a chip at the edge that Doug had noticed approximately forty visits ago and that had not grown, which meant the chip was stable, not structural, a detail that mattered in claims work and not at all in restaurants but that Doug noticed anyway. A wedge of lime sat on the edge. Kelsey squeezed the lime over the noodles without looking, the muscle memory of twenty-eight orders, and stirred it with her chopsticks.

"I keep thinking about the toast," she said. Then she stopped. She set her chopsticks down. "Actually, that's not true. I keep thinking about something else."

Doug's curry arrived. The waitress set it down and the steam carried the smell of coconut and chili and something sweet underneath, tamarind maybe. He picked up his spoon. He did not eat.

"Something from a long time ago," Kelsey said. "From the divorce."

The word landed on the table between them — *divorce* — and sat there next to the soy sauce and the chopstick wrapper she'd folded into a small tight rectangle. Doug felt the air in the booth change, the way it changed in a house when you opened the door to a room that had been closed and the air that came out was different from the air in the rest of the place. Staler. Older.

"Okay," he said.

Kelsey picked up the chopstick wrapper and unfolded it. Folded it again. She was looking at the table, not at him, and her jaw was set in the way it got when she was working something loose — the expression she wore, he imagined, when she was scaling below the gumline and the calculus wasn't giving.

"I was sixteen. You and Mom had been separated for about two months. I was going back and forth. Tuesday and Thursday nights at your place, weekends alternating. The schedule."

"I remember the schedule."

"I know you remember the schedule, Dad." She looked up. Her eyes were direct and dark, and he could see something in them that he'd been seeing since the diner in Worthington — not anger, not the specific furious precision she'd brought that day. Something underneath the anger, older and quieter. "I'm not asking if you remember the schedule. I'm telling you something."

Doug set his spoon down. "Go ahead."

"One of those Tuesdays. I was at your apartment — the first one, the one on Indianola, before you moved to Clintonville. I was doing homework on your laptop because mine was at Mom's. You were at work, or maybe you'd gone to the store. I don't remember. You weren't there."

The Indianola apartment. Doug had lived there for eight months between moving out of the Briarwood house and finding the Clintonville place. It had been a one-bedroom with carpet that smelled faintly of the previous tenant's dog, and the bathroom had a window that didn't lock, and he'd slept on a mattress on the floor for the first three weeks because the bed frame he'd ordered online had arrived with a cracked side rail and the replacement took nineteen days. He remembered all of this. He documented his own life with the same thoroughness he applied to claims.

"I opened the laptop to do my homework," Kelsey said. "And there was a spreadsheet open. An Excel file."

Doug's hands went still on the table.

"Columns," Kelsey said. Her voice had gone quiet — the Kelsey quiet, the one that was precision rather than volume, the one he'd been hearing since she was old enough to figure out that being loud didn't work on him but being exact did. "Date. Event. Description. You had entries going back months. Before the separation. You were tracking things about Mom."

Doug did not move. The curry was sending steam past his face, and he could feel the heat of it on his cheek, and the Casio music had cycled back to its beginning and was starting the twelve-minute loop again, and the couple at the table across from them was laughing about something — a bright, uncomplicated laugh that belonged to people having a different kind of Saturday.

"March fourteenth, Kelsey pickup thirty-five minutes late from school. March nineteenth, forgot to send permission slip for field trip — had to be faxed from office. April second, schedule conflict with dentist appointment, Megan booked during my Tuesday." Kelsey recited these without looking at anything, from memory. She'd been carrying them for twelve years. "You had maybe forty entries. Each one with a date and a description. Some of them had a column that said 'documented' — yes or no."

The restaurant continued around them. A waiter passed with a tray of spring rolls. The kitchen window showed hands plating something green. The bell on the door rang and someone came in and the woman with the jade bracelet led them to a table, and Doug heard all of it the way you hear sounds when you're underwater — present but distorted, arriving through a medium that changes everything.

He remembered the spreadsheet.

He remembered it precisely, the way he remembered every document he'd ever created — the cell formatting, the column widths, the specific shade of blue he'd used for the header row because Excel's default was too dark and he'd adjusted it to match the Mutual Fidelity formatting guide, which was not a relevant consideration for a personal spreadsheet tracking his ex-wife's failures as a co-parent, but he'd done it anyway because formatting was formatting and he couldn't not.

"I didn't read all of it," Kelsey said. "I read enough." She pushed her pad thai to the side, the noodles cooling, the lime wedge darkening at the edges. "I was sixteen. I was doing homework. And I found a spreadsheet where my dad was tracking every time my mom screwed up, and every single entry was about me. About picking me up. About forgetting my permission slips. About scheduling around me."

Doug opened his mouth.

"I'm not done." Not loud. Steady. "I didn't tell you. I didn't tell Mom. I closed the laptop and I did my homework on paper and I never mentioned it. Twelve years."

The folded chopstick wrapper was in her hand, crushed now, a small dense rectangle of paper that her fingers kept working as she talked.

"And what I thought — what I thought at sixteen, sitting in that apartment on Indianola with the carpet that smelled like someone else's dog — was that my dad saw me as evidence."

The word arrived in Doug's chest. Not metaphorically — he felt it in his sternum, a pressure that had nothing to do with the curry or the booth or the cracked vinyl under his left thigh. *Evidence.* That was the word. His daughter had been sixteen years old and she'd found a file on his laptop where he'd turned her into data points, into a record of failures that belonged to someone else but were measured in the unit of her — her late pickups, her missed permission slips, her Tuesday nights.

"Not a person," Kelsey said. She was looking at him now, and her eyes were full but she wasn't crying — she was holding it the way she held everything, with the tight grip and the jaw set and the gaze that was its own form of pressure. "A case file. I was a case file."

---

Neither of them ate for a while.

The pad thai sat on Kelsey's side of the table and the massaman curry sat on Doug's, and the steam from the curry had thinned and the coconut smell had faded to something less insistent, and the Casio music played its loop and started over and the restaurant went on being a restaurant on a Saturday in late April, indifferent to what was happening in the booth by the window.

Doug had not spoken. He was aware of this — aware of the seconds passing, aware of Kelsey sitting across from him with the ruined chopstick wrapper and her untouched noodles and twelve years of carrying something she'd found on a laptop in an apartment that no longer existed. He was aware that he needed to say something and that nothing he said would be right and that saying nothing was also wrong and that these two facts did not resolve into a single clear action. They just sat there side by side like competing coverage interpretations on the same claim.

He could not apologize. He understood this without examining it. An apology would be the wrong instrument — too smooth, too available, the thing you reached for when you wanted to close a file rather than understand what was in it. Kelsey hadn't driven two hours to receive an apology. She'd driven two hours to put the spreadsheet on the table between them, and the spreadsheet was there now, and an apology would be like filing it in a drawer.

"I remember making it," Doug said.

Kelsey was still. Her hands had stopped working the wrapper. She was watching him the way she'd watched him at the diner — the level gaze, the silence that was its own form of waiting.

"It was —" He stopped. He could feel the pull. The narrating Doug, the one who organized and contextualized, the one who would say *It was a difficult period* or *I was going through a lot* or some other framing that would make the spreadsheet smaller than it was. He could feel that version of himself assembling its sentences, and he could feel Kelsey watching him feel it, because she always knew — she always knew — when he was retreating into the orderly version.

He picked up his spoon. He stirred the curry once. He set the spoon down without eating.

"I didn't know what to do with what I was feeling," he said. "After your mom left. I don't mean the marriage. I'm not talking about the marriage. I'm talking about you. The schedule. Every other weekend, Tuesdays and Thursdays. I'd drive to the school to pick you up and you'd be standing there on the curb with your backpack, and then two days later I'd drive you back to your mom's, and the apartment was — the apartment on Indianola was a place I lived and you visited. That was the difference. You visited. And every time you left I didn't know what to do with the space you'd been in."

He looked at his hands on the table. They were the same hands that had typed the spreadsheet — the same fingers that had entered the dates and the descriptions and adjusted the header-row blue because even his grievance had a formatting preference.

"So I documented it. Because that's what I do. Something happens and I don't know what to feel about it, so I write it down. I put it in a column. I give it a date and a description and I file it, and once it's filed I can look at it instead of feeling it. The file is smaller than the feeling. The file fits."

The couple across the restaurant laughed again. A plate broke somewhere in the kitchen — a clean, undramatic sound — and someone said something in Thai and someone else responded, and the normal operations of the place continued without regard for what was being said in the booth by the window.

"I was keeping score because I did not know how to miss you any other way."

He said it, and the sentence sat in the air between them, and he heard himself use *did not* instead of *didn't* — the full form, the careful form, each word placed deliberately because the stakes were too high for shortcuts. He meant every word at its full weight. He had been keeping score. He had not known how. It was the only way he'd had.

Kelsey was quiet. She was looking at the chopstick wrapper in her hand — mangled, creased beyond use, a small ruin of paper that her fingers had been destroying for twenty minutes. She set it on the table next to her plate. She picked up her glass of water and drank. The hairline crack in the glass caught the overhead light.

"That's not an apology," she said.

"No."

"Okay." She put the glass down. "I'm not sure I wanted one."

"I'm not sure one would help."

"No. Probably not."

They sat with it. The music cycled. A family came in — two parents, two small kids, the smaller one in a booster seat that the hostess produced from behind the counter with the efficiency of someone who'd done this a thousand times. The kids were loud in the unconcerned way of children in restaurants, and one of them was asking for spring rolls and the other was asking for Sprite, and neither of them knew that in the booth by the window a father and daughter were sitting in the wreckage of something that had been buried for twelve years and had just been set on the table between the pad thai and the curry.

Doug picked up his spoon. He took a bite. The curry was warm, not hot — it had been sitting too long — and the spice hit differently when the food wasn't steaming, sharper, more direct, without the cushion of heat. His eyes watered. He didn't wipe them.

"Eat your noodles," he said.

Kelsey looked at him. Then she picked up her chopsticks and ate a bite of pad thai, and the noodles were room temperature by now and the lime had gone flat and neither of them commented on it.

"I told Tyler," she said, after a while. "About the spreadsheet. Last year."

"What did he say?"

"He said that was messed up." She twirled her chopsticks through the noodles without lifting any. "And then he said, 'But at least he was paying attention.' Which is such a Tyler thing to say that I almost threw something at him."

"He's not wrong."

"He's not wrong in a way that's not helpful." She ate another bite. "That's kind of Tyler's whole deal. He's never wrong in ways that aren't helpful."

Doug ate his curry. The second bite was worse — the spice was building, accumulating. He drank water. The water didn't help. It never did.

"How is Tyler?" he asked, and he meant it differently than he usually meant it. He usually meant it as a logistical check — Tyler fine, Kelsey fine, Cleveland fine, everything processed and filed. He meant it now as a real question, and Kelsey heard the difference because she looked up from her noodles and studied him for a moment before she answered.

"He's fine," she said. And then: "He's really fine. He's always fine. He's —" She set her chopsticks down. She pressed her hands flat on the table, fingers spread, the chipped nail and the wedding ring and the slight tension in her wrists that meant she was working something out in real time, not reciting something she'd prepared. "He's fine the way a hotel room is fine. Everything works. The bed is made. The towels are clean. The thermostat is set to seventy-two. You walk in and you think, yeah, this is fine. This is exactly what I asked for. And it is. It's exactly what I asked for."

She stopped. She picked up a noodle with her fingers — not her chopsticks, her fingers — and ate it, and the gesture was so stripped of pretense that Doug saw his daughter at nine years old, eating spaghetti with her hands because she hadn't felt like using a fork and he'd let her because Megan wasn't there and when Megan wasn't there the rules about utensils had been suggestions.

"I don't know if I married stability or boredom," Kelsey said. "Or if there's a difference. And I can't figure it out because every time I try to think about it I think about you and Mom, and then I think about the spreadsheet, and then I think maybe boring is fine. Maybe boring is the whole point." She looked at him. "Don't tell me what to do."

"I wasn't going to."

"You were thinking about it."

"I was thinking that I'm the last person who should give anyone advice about marriage."

Kelsey almost laughed. The almost was closer to actual laughter this time — it reached her eyes and her mouth moved and a sound came out that wasn't quite a laugh but was in the neighborhood. "Yeah. You're pretty disqualified."

"The data speaks for itself."

"God. Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"The insurance voice. The thing where you turn everything into a claims form."

Doug looked at her across the table — across the cooling curry and the flat pad thai and the mangled chopstick wrapper and twelve years of a secret she'd carried since she was sixteen and the long, specific history of two people who loved each other in a language neither of them spoke fluently. He thought about the spreadsheet. He thought about the woman at the front with her jade bracelet and her new glasses. He thought about the vinyl crack in the booth that nobody had repaired and the chip in the plate that had been stable for forty visits and the ceramic cat in the window that had migrated from one side to the other for reasons nobody would ever explain.

"I don't know how to turn it off," he said. "It's not a voice. It's just how I think."

"I know," Kelsey said. "That's the problem. And it's also —" She stopped. She pressed her lips together. She was doing the thing she did when something was about to come out that was too close to tender, too close to the thing she wasn't ready to put on the table alongside the spreadsheet. "It's also why the toast made sense. The way you talk. The exactness. It's why eighty million people watched you — because nobody talks like that when they're drunk. And you did."

Doug had nothing to say to this. He ate another bite of curry. The spice had reached the point where it was no longer a flavor but a condition, a state of being, and his nose was running and his eyes were wet and he was not going to switch to something milder because this was the fourteenth year of the massaman curry and he was not a man who changed his order.

"Do you want dessert?" he said.

"Mango sticky rice," Kelsey said, without pause.

Doug flagged the waitress. One order, two spoons. The waitress wrote nothing down.

The mango sticky rice came on a plate that was too large for it — a generous portion by the standards of the dish, which was always small: the mango sliced into a fan and the rice in a mound beside it, glistening with coconut cream, sesame seeds on top catching the light from the fixture above the booth. Kelsey pulled the plate to the center of the table. She took her spoon and cut into the mango. Doug took his spoon and scooped a piece of rice. The coconut cream was sweet and slightly warm, and the rice had the texture of something that had been tended to properly — cooked slow, rinsed, steamed, sweetened — the kind of food that required patience to make and no effort at all to eat.

They ate in silence. Not the loaded silence of the diner in Worthington — not the five minutes of honest nothing that had said everything they couldn't. This was a different silence, easier, the silence of two people who had said the hard thing and were sitting in the aftermath, and the aftermath tasted like coconut and mango and sesame, and neither of them needed it to be anything more than what it was.

Kelsey's spoon clinked against the plate as she reached for the last piece of mango. She got it. Doug didn't contest this. He had never contested the last piece of anything with his daughter. He didn't know if this was generosity or the specific passivity that had shaped most of his relationships, and he decided, sitting in the booth with the cracked vinyl and the faded awning outside and fourteen years of the same orders, that it didn't matter. She wanted the mango. He let her have it.

"I should probably head back before five," Kelsey said. "Tyler's making dinner."

"Tyler cooks?"

"He makes this chicken thing. It's fine."

"Fine like the hotel room?"

Kelsey looked at him. For a second he thought he'd gone too far — she'd handed him something fragile about her marriage and he'd turned it into a callback, which was a comedy mechanic, and this was not comedy. But then she snorted — an ugly, genuine sound, not a laugh, closer to the noise someone makes when they've been hit with something unexpectedly accurate.

"Yeah," she said. "Fine like the hotel room. The chicken is fine like the hotel room."

She put a twenty on the table. Doug reached for his wallet. "I've got it," she said.

"You drove two hours."

"And you listened. That's worth twenty bucks." She slid out of the booth. She stood and pulled on the denim jacket and zipped it halfway — always halfway, as if committing to the full zip would constitute an opinion about the weather she wasn't prepared to hold.

"Thanks for the curry," he said. This was not what he meant, but she would know what he meant, because she always knew what he meant, even when — especially when — he said something else.

"The curry you can't handle and won't stop ordering."

"It's fine."

"It's not fine. Your eyes were watering for twenty minutes."

"That was the curry."

Kelsey looked at him. She didn't argue. She didn't need to.

"I'll call you this week," she said.

"Okay."

"I mean it. I'll actually call."

"Okay."

She walked to the door. The bell rang. She was gone.

Doug sat in the booth. The mango sticky rice plate had one smear of coconut cream left on it, and two spoons, and the remains of a meal that had started as routine and ended somewhere he didn't have a category for. He picked up the check. Thirty-eight dollars. He left Kelsey's twenty and added twenty-two of his own, and the math was wrong — he was overtipping by more than he usually would — but the woman with the jade bracelet had been serving him for fourteen years and had never once asked about the toast or the video or the profile, and he didn't know if this was discretion or indifference, and either way he was grateful in the particular way you're grateful to someone who lets you be ordinary when the rest of the world has decided you're something else.

He walked to the lot. It was half-full, and Kelsey's Civic was already gone — she'd left quickly, the way she left things, cleanly, without lingering in the doorway. The sun was warm on his arms for the first time in months, late April finally asserting itself against the long memory of winter, and the sky was the washed-out blue that Columbus produced in spring — not dramatic, not worth photographing, just blue enough to count.

He got in the car. He did not start it. He sat with the windows up and the key in his lap and thought about the spreadsheet.

Column A: Date. Column B: Event. Column C: Description. Column D: Documented (Y/N).

He had made it in the apartment on Indianola, on the laptop that his sixteen-year-old daughter had opened to do her homework, on a weeknight when she was in the next room and he was at work or at the store or wherever he'd gone that he couldn't remember now, and the spreadsheet was open because he'd been adding to it that morning, because every morning during those months he'd sat at the kitchen counter with his coffee and entered the latest data the way he entered claim data at the office — date, event, description, status — and the routine of entering it had felt like control, and control had felt like the only alternative to the thing he could not name and could not file and could not depreciate on any schedule he knew.

He had turned his daughter into a line item. He had done this because he loved her and did not know how to hold the loving, and documentation was the only container he'd ever trusted.

She had known for twelve years. She had carried it for twelve years — through the rest of high school, through college, through the engagement and the wedding and the toast and the months since the toast — and she'd come to the Thai place on a Saturday in April and set it on the table between the pad thai and the curry, and he'd told her the truth. Not an apology, not a defense. The flat, exact truth that was the only thing he'd ever had to offer and the thing he almost never offered. And she'd heard it, and they'd split the mango sticky rice, and she'd gone home to Tyler and his fine chicken in their fine apartment, and neither of them had mentioned the toast.

He sat in the car for a long time. The parking lot was warm through the windshield and the ceramic cat in the Thai place window stared at the street with its one raised paw and the vacant lot next door was still vacant and the awning was still faded and everything was exactly where it had been when he'd pulled in an hour and a half ago, except that his daughter had driven two hours to tell him the worst thing she knew about him, and he'd sat there and taken it, and what she'd told him was true.

Doug started the car. He drove home to Clintonville. The route was familiar — right on High, left on Arcadia, past the library and the coffee shop and the intersection where a hydrant had burst last December and he'd assessed the resulting water damage to the duplex on the corner, four thousand in remediation, covered under the city's liability policy, closed and filed. He drove past it and the duplex looked fine. You couldn't tell anything had happened. The damage was repaired, the paperwork was complete, and the only evidence that fifteen hundred gallons of water had flooded the ground floor was in a claims file on a server somewhere, a record that documented everything that had been lost and everything it had cost to fix and said nothing at all about what it had felt like to stand in the water.

He parked. He went inside. He didn't eat cereal. He sat on the couch in the living room as the afternoon shifted into evening, and the apartment was quiet, and Simone's pen cap was still on the counter where he'd left it weeks ago, and the boxes were still in the second bedroom, and the water stain on the ceiling was still shaped like Ohio, and everything was where it had always been, and none of it was the same.


# Chapter 14

The rental car was a gray Hyundai Elantra that smelled like the previous renter's choices — a pine air freshener clipped to the vent, dried coffee in the cupholder, the residue of a fast food meal that had happened somewhere between here and wherever that person had been going. Doug adjusted the mirrors, connected his phone to the auxiliary cable even though he had nothing to play, and pulled onto I-71 north toward Cleveland.

Layla had booked the tour in a single phone call while Doug was on his lunch break. Three cities, three comedy clubs, three nights. "Warm-ups," she called them. "Before the big show." She said it the way his supervisor Janet said "touching base," which meant something was about to become his problem. The Netflix taping was in two weeks. Doug did not feel warm.

The highway was flat and gray and familiar — he'd driven this route to visit Kelsey when she first moved to Cleveland, before the visits became less frequent, then occasional, then something they planned over text three weeks in advance. She lived twenty minutes from the comedy club. He had not told her he was coming.

He checked into a Holiday Inn off I-77 at four-thirty. The room was what every Holiday Inn room was: a bed, a desk, a chair that swiveled, a television mounted at an angle that suggested someone had once tried to steal it, and a window that looked onto the parking lot. The bedspread was a shade of maroon that existed only in hotel rooms. Doug sat on it and opened his laptop.

For the past two weeks, he had been watching comedians on YouTube. He watched John Mulaney tell a story about a party and analyzed the rhythm: setup, misdirection, commitment to the bit, callback. He watched Hasan Minhaj shift from funny to political to personal and tracked the transitions. He watched old Carlin specials — Carlin was dead and therefore couldn't be disappointed in what Doug was about to do with his legacy. He took notes. Not comedy notes — structural notes. He wrote things like "pause after second beat — 1.5 sec" and "personal detail + absurd extension = laugh" as if comedy were a claims assessment with measurable outputs.

The club was called Wiley's. It sat in a strip mall between a Great Clips and a vape shop, and the marquee out front read THURSDAY NIGHT SHOWCASE and, beneath it, in smaller letters, FEATURING VIRAL SENSATION DOUG BRIDGE. Doug looked at the words and thought about how "sensation" was also what you felt right before you threw up.

Inside, the room held about a hundred and twenty people, though tonight it was closer to ninety. The stage was low — maybe ten inches off the floor — with a single mic stand and a stool. The lighting was overhead fluorescent, the kind that made everyone look slightly worse than they actually were. A hand-painted sign on the back wall said LAUGH.

The owner, a man named Gerry who had a gray ponytail and a Cavs T-shirt from at least the LeBron era, walked Doug to the back, which was a storage room with a sink and a mirror that had lost most of its silvering. "Good crowd tonight," Gerry said. "We sold forty-two tickets off your name alone, which is — yeah, that's good for a Thursday." Gerry had a bad knee he favored when he walked, and he talked about it the way people with chronic pain do — like the knee was a third person in the conversation with its own schedule and opinions. He'd been running comedy rooms for eighteen years. His ex-wife had been a comic, he said, "back when that meant something different." He didn't explain what different meant.

Doug thanked him and closed the door and looked at himself in the half-mirror. He was wearing a button-down, untucked, and jeans. He had considered what to wear for an embarrassing amount of time. He had settled on this because it was what a comedian would wear — casual, approachable, the costume of someone who just wandered up here. The costume was complete.

He walked onto the stage and took the microphone from the stand the way he'd seen Mulaney do it — a casual lift, as if the mic just happened to be there and he just happened to want it. He looked at the audience. They looked back.

"So," he said. And he paused. One beat. Two. The way Carlin paused. The way a comedian pauses when he's about to say something.

He had nothing to say after the pause. The pause was the entire plan.

He launched into the material, but not his material — a version of it filtered through the comedians he'd studied. He set up the insurance bit with a Mulaney-style digression about being a person who reads every contract. He tried a Minhaj-style shift from the funny to the personal, pivoting from a claims dispute to a brief aside about his divorce that he delivered with practiced casualness that was audible in every syllable. He tried crowd callbacks. He tried working the pauses. He tried the thing where you pretend to lose your thought and then find it, which Carlin did because he was George Carlin and which Doug did because he had watched a man do it on a screen in a Holiday Inn and thought it looked learnable.

None of it was his. It was technically complete — the jokes were there, the callbacks were there, the pauses were in the right places — and the room could feel it. The laughs were wrong. Not absent — wrong. People laughed at his setups instead of his punchlines. They laughed at his pauses, which were supposed to build tension but instead broadcast that he was waiting for something to happen. A man near the bar laughed once, loudly, and then stopped, as if he'd been caught.

Doug did the crowd work he'd rehearsed. "Where are you from?" he asked a couple in the front row. "Parma," the man said. He was wearing a Cleveland Guardians jersey with a mustard stain on the sleeve, and his wife or girlfriend had her hand on his knee in a way that suggested she was either comforting him or preventing him from leaving. Doug had prepared a response about suburbs. He forgot it. "Nice," he said. "Good area."

The room was not hostile. It was patient. It was waiting for the person they'd seen in the video, and instead they were getting a man in a costume, performing the movements of comedy the way someone who'd learned ballroom dancing from a diagram performs ballroom dancing. The feet were in the right places. The music was playing. Nothing was happening.

Eighteen minutes in, a woman near the back shouted, "Do the toast!"

She had curly red hair and a beer she'd been nursing since before Doug came on, and she'd been checking her phone with the resigned frequency of someone waiting for a text that was not going to arrive. She wasn't being mean. She was being honest, which was worse.

The room contracted. Doug could feel it — every person in the audience now unified in wanting the same thing. They hadn't come for bits. They'd come for seven minutes of a drunk man being too honest in front of his daughter's wedding guests, and what they were getting instead was a pantomime.

"I can't," Doug said. "I'm sober."

The room laughed. It was sudden, unguarded — the laugh that happens before anyone decides whether to laugh. Doug did not know it was funny. He had stated a fact. The toast had required eight whiskeys, a catastrophically good toast from Brendan, and twenty years of things he'd never said out loud. What he had tonight was a rental car and a Holiday Inn and a set list he'd annotated like a claims file. He could not do the toast for the same reason a house fire does not happen on schedule.

He did twelve more minutes. They were worse than the first eighteen. He ended by saying "Thank you" to a room that was already reaching for coats and phones, and he walked off past Gerry, who said, "Good energy up there," which was a lie delivered with the professional ease of a man who had told it several thousand times.

In the parking lot, the air was warm for late May. Doug sat in the Elantra and called Simone.

"How was it?"

"Bad."

"Bad how?"

"I tried to do it like a comedian."

A pause. He could hear her doing something — running water, maybe doing dishes. "Yeah," she said. "Don't do that."

"I know."

"What worked?"

"Nothing."

"Nothing at all?"

He thought about the woman and the laugh. "Someone asked me to do the toast and I said I couldn't because I was sober. People laughed."

"That's because it was true."

"It was just a fact."

"Yeah," Simone said. "That's the same thing."

Doug hung up and drove back to the Holiday Inn. He lay on the maroon bedspread and stared at the ceiling and thought about calling Kelsey, who was twenty minutes north of here, asleep next to Tyler in the apartment Doug had helped them move into nine months ago. He did not call her. He did not have anything to say that she would want to hear at eleven-thirty on a Thursday, and the things he might want to say — something about the spreadsheet, something about the toast, something about being in her city and failing at the thing the city thought he was good at — required a kind of honesty he could apparently only access drunk, and he was sober, and he was in a Holiday Inn room that smelled like industrial cleaner and someone else's sleep. He fell asleep with his shoes on, which was becoming a pattern.

---

The drive to Pittsburgh was two and a half hours through northeastern Ohio and across the western edge of Pennsylvania, where the flat farmland wrinkled into hills and the highway curved in ways that required attention. Doug stopped for gas at a Sheetz outside Youngstown. The coffee cost a dollar eighty-nine and tasted how truck stops smelled — burnt, industrial, somehow fine if you didn't think about it. He drank it in the parking lot and watched a man in a hi-vis vest fill up a pickup while arguing on the phone about a dog, or a fence, or both.

Pittsburgh was more vertical than Cleveland, which was more vertical than Columbus, which was essentially flat. The hotel was another Holiday Inn, this one near the airport, and the room was identical to the Cleveland room except that the bedspread was blue and the window faced a different parking lot. Doug hung his shirt in the closet and lay on the bed and stared at a water stain on the ceiling that had the shape of something that refused to be anything recognizable.

He thought about the Cleveland performance. He thought about what Simone had said — that the truth and the fact were the same thing, for him. He understood this and could not use it. Understanding that you were funny when you were just being accurate was different from being able to be accurate on stage. The stage installed an expectation, and the expectation sealed the space between Doug and whatever it was that had happened at the wedding.

The club was called The Comic Attic, which was a lie — it was in a basement, beneath a bar called O'Reilly's, down a narrow staircase that smelled like spilled Yuengling and industrial cleaner. The room held about ninety people. Tonight there were closer to seventy-five. The stage was slightly higher than Cleveland's — maybe eighteen inches — and the extra eight inches shouldn't have mattered, but they did. Doug felt exposed up there.
