The MC, a young woman named Diane who had a sleeve tattoo and a laugh that carried through walls, introduced him as "the man who broke the internet at his daughter's wedding — Doug Bridge!"

The applause was genuine. They wanted him. Some of them had paid specifically for this, had told someone on Thursday that they had plans on Friday night, had driven to this basement bar in Pittsburgh because an algorithm or a friend or their own curiosity had brought them here. Doug felt the weight of their expectation like the air before a storm.

He took the mic. He looked at the room. He opened his mouth.

Nothing came.

It was not nerves, and it was not a blank. His mind did not go empty — it went absent, as if the part of him that produced words had left through a door he hadn't known was there. He could see the audience. He could feel the microphone, which was warm from Diane's hand. He could hear the bar above them, muffled by the ceiling — the bass thump of a jukebox playing something he couldn't identify, the distant crash of someone doing something wrong with a pint glass. His body was on the stage. The rest of him was somewhere else.

One second. Three. Five. The audience shifted. Seven seconds. A man in the front row uncrossed his arms. Nine seconds. A woman near the wall looked at the person beside her. Eleven seconds. Someone coughed, and the cough was not a cough — it was the sound a room makes when it needs someone to do something.

"I had material," Doug said. His voice sounded like it was coming from the ceiling. "I practiced it in the car. In the hotel. I said the words out loud in a Holiday Inn bathroom and they were there. They were real words. I had them."

He paused. This pause was different from the Carlin pause in Cleveland. This was the space where the words should have been.

He started the set. It came out damaged — the bits were present but the connections between them were gone, and Doug moved through them the way you walk through a house after a break-in, checking what was still where you'd left it. The insurance bit was there. The callback about depreciation was there. The transition to the cereal section was missing, so he jumped, and the audience felt the gap and tightened.

Twelve minutes in, he told the pool story.

He didn't plan to. He'd reached the end of what he could remember and the pool was just there — the above-ground pool in Westerville, the wall that split, the water that went everywhere, the neighbor's seventeen hybrid tea roses documented with receipts at forty-two hundred dollars, the coverage gap that nobody's policy addressed. He told it the way he'd told it at his kitchen table when Simone first asked him to be funny — not as a bit, not as material, but as a problem. A genuinely fascinating problem. A problem where a man's pool destroyed another man's roses and the insurance system, which existed to assign responsibility and compensate loss, could not determine whose fault beauty was.

The room leaned in. He could feel it — not laughter exactly, but attention. Attention that was one step from laughter and one step from something else. He was in it. For ninety seconds he was in it, and the room was with him, and the words came not from the set list but from wherever they came from when he wasn't trying, and someone in the third row laughed and the laugh spread and for ninety seconds Doug was the man from the video.

Then he tried to transition. He reached for the next bit — the one about replacement cost that Simone had helped him shape — and the reaching was the mistake. The reaching was the performance reinstalling itself. He could feel the room pull back, a tide going out, and the next six minutes were the debris left behind. He finished. The applause was better than Cleveland — real in places, polite in others, confused throughout.

Backstage, which was a broom closet with a folding chair, Doug sat and pressed his palms against his knees. His hands were dry. His knees were vibrating, a fine tremor he could feel through the fabric of his khakis but that probably wasn't visible. He thought about the ninety seconds. He thought about the eleven seconds of nothing before that. He thought about how the pool story had worked because he'd forgotten he was performing, and how forgetting you were performing was not something you could write into a set list.

He called Simone from the hotel.

"Better," he said.

"Better than Cleveland?"

"The pool story worked. For about ninety seconds."

"And then?"

"Then I tried to do the next bit and it died."

Simone's silence had a quality to it — not absence but attention. She was thinking, and her thinking was audible the way a room is audible when someone is listening hard. "You know what happened," she said. It was not a question.

"I know what happened. I don't know how to make it happen on purpose."

"Yeah," she said. "That's the problem."

---

The drive from Pittsburgh to Detroit was the longest leg — four and a half hours through Pennsylvania and across the narrow top of West Virginia and up through Ohio to Toledo and then south to the city. Doug drove it Saturday morning. The day was warm and the sun hit the windshield at an angle the visor couldn't fix. He stopped for gas outside Akron. The coffee tasted the same as the Youngstown coffee. He bought a gas station sandwich, unwrapped it, looked at it, and rewrapped it. He ate it twenty miles later because hunger was a simpler problem than whatever else was happening to him.

The highway was its own kind of room. I-80 through Pennsylvania was trees and hills and trucks and the same rest stop appearing every forty miles with the same food court and the same families standing in the parking lot staring at their phones with the exhausted solidarity of people who had been in a car too long and were not yet close enough to where they were going. I-75 through Ohio was flat again, the return to flatness after the hills, and Doug drove through it without the radio on, listening to tire noise and the Elantra's air conditioning, which had a rattle that surfaced at exactly sixty-seven miles per hour and disappeared at sixty-eight.

Layla called at noon.

"So how's it going? I heard Cleveland was a little — you know. Finding your legs."

"It was bad, Layla."

"It wasn't bad. Gerry said you had good energy."

"Gerry lies to comedians after they bomb. That's his whole job."

"Okay so it was a learning experience. What about Pittsburgh?"

"The pool story worked for ninety seconds."

"That's great! Ninety seconds is — that's a solid chunk. We can build on ninety seconds."

Doug thought about what Simone would say about building on ninety seconds. He was fairly certain she would say nothing, which would be worse than disagreement.

"I need to focus on tonight," he said.

"Totally. You've got this. Detroit's gonna love you." A pause. "The venue's great, by the way. It's called The Mark. It's got this whole — anyway. You've got this, babe."

He arrived in Detroit at three-thirty and checked into a Holiday Inn that was neither better nor worse than the other two. The bedspread was green. The parking lot had a view of another parking lot. He stood in the shower for a long time, not washing, just letting the water hit the back of his neck where the driving had knotted something he could press his fingers into but not undo.

The Mark was a real comedy club — not a strip mall or a bar basement, but a dedicated room with proper lighting, a real stage, real sound, tiered seating that climbed toward the back. It held about a hundred and fifty, and it was close to full. The MC was a local comic named Terrence who did five minutes of solid material about Detroit traffic that got real laughs from people who had been sitting in that traffic three hours ago. Terrence moved across the stage naturally, worked the room, made it look like something a person could decide to do. Doug watched from behind the curtain and felt the gap between Terrence's ease and his own situation, which was not a gap so much as a different country.

Doug walked on stage. The lights were better here — warm, focused, turning the audience into shapes and outlines beyond the first three rows. He started the set. Not the comedian costume from Cleveland, not the damaged recall from Pittsburgh — something closer to what he and Simone had built. The insurance bits. The cereal bit. He delivered it cleanly. It was fine. The audience was with him in the way an audience is with someone they've decided to give a chance, which was different from the way they were with Terrence, who they'd been with because they couldn't help it.

Three minutes in, a man in the second row started.

"Toast!"

He was maybe forty-five, heavy, wearing a Tigers cap and a polo shirt tucked into jeans — a formality decision that had gone wrong in both directions. He had a beer in each hand, one mostly full and one mostly empty, and the posture of a man who had been drinking since whatever had happened before this and intended to keep going after. He wasn't mean. Doug could see that. He was a man who had paid money to see a specific thing and was not seeing it, and he had decided, with the clarity that four or five beers provide, to ask for it directly.

"Toast! Toast! Toast!"

Doug looked at him. The room tensed. Some people laughed — the nervous laugh of an audience watching something that could go either way. Doug thought about what real comedians did with hecklers. He had watched YouTube videos of this too — Bill Burr dismantling a drunk guy with surgical precision, comics he'd studied at three in the morning thinking, I can learn this.

He could not learn this.

"Hey, man," Doug said. "I appreciate the enthusiasm."

It was the wrong thing to say, and he knew it as the words left. "I appreciate the enthusiasm" was not a comedian's line — it was a supervisor addressing a difficult employee, the register of someone who had processed enough workplace conflicts to know how to manage disruption without resolving it. The heckler blinked and then doubled down: "TOAST! Come on, man, do the TOAST!"

"I don't do the toast," Doug said. "That's not — I'm doing different material tonight."

"We don't want different material!" The man was grinning now. He wasn't angry. He was having the best time of anyone in the room, which was the problem. His enthusiasm had made him the show, and Doug was the straight man in someone else's bit.

"Sir," Doug started, and the "sir" was the second mistake, because comedians don't call hecklers "sir" — adjusters call claimants "sir." The professional register landed in the room like a form letter at a party.

"Sir! He called me sir!" The heckler turned to the woman next to him — his wife or date, who had the expression of someone revising the cost-benefit analysis of this evening in real time. She put her hand on his arm. He shrugged it off, gently but completely. "DO THE TOAST, DOUG!"

The room split. Half the audience was embarrassed for Doug. Half was watching the spectacle with the helpless attention of people watching a fender bender — they didn't want it to happen, but now that it was happening they wanted to see how it ended. Terrence, standing near the bar with his arms crossed, had the look of a man watching something unfold that he'd seen before and could not help with.

Doug gave up on the heckler. He turned back to the room and tried to find the material. It was gone. Not absent like Pittsburgh had been — chased out by the confrontation, the way a burst pipe clears a room faster than a fire alarm. He had the set list memorized and could not reach a single line of it.

He stood on stage in front of a hundred and forty people with no material and a drunk man who wanted a wedding toast and nothing in his head but the last two days.

So he talked about the drive.

He didn't decide to. It was just there — the four and a half hours from Pittsburgh, the gas station coffee that tasted the same in Youngstown and Akron and probably tasted the same in every gas station between the Atlantic Ocean and the Mississippi River. The sandwich he'd been afraid to eat and then ate anyway because hunger was simpler. The Holiday Inns with their color-coded bedspreads — maroon in Cleveland, blue in Pittsburgh, green here — as if someone in a corporate office had decided that the illusion of variety was worth manufacturing three different fabric dyes. The GPS voice that said "recalculating" with the patience of someone who had accepted that humans would never follow directions correctly. The rest stops that all had the same Cinnabon and the same families in the parking lot staring at their phones with the exhausted solidarity of people who were neither where they'd been nor where they were going.

He talked about the highway. About how I-80 through Pennsylvania was beautiful in a way nobody mentioned, because it was just the road you took to get somewhere else, and the beauty was accidental — the hills rolling green because the trees had just come in, and the light hitting the asphalt at four in the afternoon in a way that made you think about how much of the country you were never going to see because you were always on your way to do something in the next city that you couldn't do in the last one.

He talked about the loneliness of rest stops. How everyone at a rest stop was between places, and the rest stop itself was not a place — it was a pause in being somewhere, and the people there were all pausing at the same time without knowing each other, and all of them were going to get back in their cars and continue being alone in the direction they were already going.

He was not trying to be funny. He was describing what had happened to him in the last two days with the same precision he'd use for water damage in a basement — this is what happened, this is the extent, this is what was lost. The heckler had gone quiet. The heckler's wife or date had put her phone down. The room had gathered around Doug's voice, and Doug recognized what was happening because he'd felt it for ninety seconds in Pittsburgh — not laughter, but the attention you give to something true.

He talked for eight minutes. About the coffee. About the parking lots. About how every Holiday Inn in America smelled the same — industrial cleaner and carpet that had absorbed the sleep of ten thousand strangers — and how the sameness was supposed to be comforting but wasn't, because comfort that was identical everywhere was not comfort but a product, and what you actually wanted at midnight in a hotel room in a city where nobody knew you were there was not a product but a place that knew you were in it.

And then he tried to go back to the material.

He could feel himself doing it — the shift from talking to performing, from saying what was there to saying what was planned. He reached for the cereal bit, which was next on the set list, and the reaching killed it. The room loosened. The tension bled out. The last four minutes were the same polite attention that Cleveland had been, and when Doug said "Thank you, that's my time" and put the mic back in the stand, the applause was generous and confused — the applause of people who had seen something they didn't know what to do with, mixed with something they knew exactly what to do with, and the two things hadn't added up to a show.

---

He left Detroit at ten-forty, still in his stage clothes, the parking lot damp from a rain that had come and gone while he was inside. The Elantra's GPS said three hours and twenty-two minutes to Columbus. Doug pulled onto I-75 south and the highway was mostly empty — semis, a few sedans, the occasional minivan with someone asleep in the back seat. The headlights made a corridor of light and the rest was dark Ohio, flat and invisible.

He drove in silence for thirty miles. He thought about the eight minutes in Detroit. He thought about the ninety seconds in Pittsburgh. He thought about the zero seconds in Cleveland where anything he said was genuinely his own. Three cities, three audiences who had paid money to see a man from a video, and the video's man had appeared for a total of maybe ten minutes across three nights. The rest had been Doug performing an imitation of someone performing, which was two layers of falsehood stacked on top of a man who could only tell the truth by accident.

He passed a sign that said TOLEDO 47. He thought about how road signs told you the distance to the next city but not why you would want to go there.

He called Simone at eleven-twenty. She picked up on the second ring.

"How was Detroit?"

"A guy heckled me for the toast. I tried to handle it and made it worse. Then I talked about the drive for eight minutes and the room went quiet."

"Quiet like they were bored, or quiet like they were listening?"

"Like they were listening."

"And then?"

"Then I went back to the material and lost them."

The highway was dark. A semi passed him going north and the wake rocked the Elantra, a push of air he felt through the steering wheel.

"The material doesn't work," Doug said.

"I know."

He waited for more. Simone did not offer more. She let silences do what words couldn't, and usually that was the right instinct. Right now it felt like being left alone in a field.

"So what do I do?" he said. "The taping's in two weeks. I've got thirty-two minutes of material that dies every time I perform it and eight minutes of talking about gas station coffee that works for reasons I can't use."

"You understand the reasons."

"I don't."

"You do. You just don't like them."

He drove past a rest stop — a gas station, a Subway, a building with restrooms, all lit up for nobody. Open twenty-four hours for the ghosts of travelers who had already come through. He thought about what Simone meant. He understood. The gas station coffee worked because he wasn't trying to make it work. The pool story worked in Pittsburgh because he'd forgotten he was on stage. The toast worked because he was drunk and furious and had never intended to say any of it. Everything that worked was accidental, and everything that was planned was dead, and the Netflix taping — scheduled, scripted, cameras and a crew of nineteen at the Lincoln Theatre, a building he had never been inside — required him to do on purpose the thing he could only do by accident.

"I understand the reasons," he said.

"I know you do."

"I just can't use them."

Simone didn't say anything for a long time. Long enough that Doug checked the phone to make sure the call was still connected. The screen glowed in the dark car. Call duration: 4:17.

"Get home safe," she said. "We'll figure it out."

She hung up. Doug put the phone in the cupholder and drove south through dark Ohio. Findlay, then Lima, then somewhere south of Lima where the highway straightened and the land flattened and the dashboard clock read 1:15 a.m. He was tired in a way that was not just the driving. Three cities, three rooms, three versions of failing to be the person eighty million people thought he was. The data was in. The assessment was complete: the damage was structural, not cosmetic, and no amount of rehearsal was going to bring the depreciated value of his performance up to replacement cost.

He pulled into his parking spot in Clintonville just after two. The apartment was dark. He went inside and stood in the kitchen and looked at the cereal on the counter — Cheerios, the yellow box — and did not eat. He was not hungry. He was the specific thing that happens after you have done something badly three times in three different ways and you know that doing it a fourth time will not be the time it starts working, but you are going to do it a fourth time anyway, because a contract says so, and because somewhere inside the bad there were ten minutes where you were yourself and a room full of strangers leaned forward, and you don't know how to get back to those ten minutes, and you don't know how to want them without killing them, and you are standing in your kitchen at two in the morning trying to figure out if wanting something is the same as ruining it.

He went to bed. The ceiling was his ceiling. The sheets were his sheets. The streetlight made the shadow it always made. In two weeks he would stand on a stage in front of 580 people, and whatever had found him in those eight minutes in Detroit and those ninety seconds in Pittsburgh would either find him again or it wouldn't, and he could not practice for it any more than he could practice for a house fire.


# Chapter 15

The bar had high ceilings and the kind of exposed brick that meant it had been something else first. Doug counted fourteen beers on the chalkboard behind the counter while Simone ordered a bourbon neat. He picked one of the fourteen that sounded like a beer and not a statement, and the bartender, who had dark red nails and a silver ring on her thumb, poured it from a tap with a wooden handle.

Nine dollars. Doug did the math on what that would be annually if he came here twice a week, which was a thing he did with any purchase over six dollars. It was not a useful calculation. It was just what his brain did.

They sat at a high-top near the windows on tall stools that didn't have backs, which meant Doug had to decide what to do with his posture. He chose upright. Simone settled onto hers the way she settled into anything — as if she'd already assessed the structural integrity and found it adequate.

She didn't have her notebook.

Doug had been watching Simone write in that notebook for almost three months. She wrote in pencil, quickly, in handwriting that tilted so far left it looked like the words were leaning away from a strong wind. She wrote things Doug said when he didn't know he was saying anything worth writing. She underlined things twice. She crossed things out with single precise lines. The notebook had been on his kitchen table at every session, and now it wasn't here, and the absence registered the way a missing page in a claim file registered — not alarming, but indicative of a different kind of meeting.

It was the last Tuesday in May. The light through the tall windows was long and warm and the sidewalk outside was full of people who didn't seem to be going anywhere in particular, which Doug supposed was what people did in the Short North on a warm evening. He'd been to this neighborhood four times in his life, counting tonight. Two of those times were to drive through it on the way to somewhere else.

"You look like someone who's never been to a bar," Simone said.

"I've been to bars."

"You look like you're appraising the property."

Doug looked at the windows, the tin ceiling, the long counter with its row of stools. "The ductwork's been painted but not insulated," he said. "You'd get condensation issues in winter."

Simone drank her bourbon. "That's exactly what I mean."

They ordered food. Simone got a salad with something on it Doug couldn't identify from the menu description — it said "shaved fennel" and he took this on faith. Doug got a burger. The waiter was a tall kid, early twenties, with the careful posture of someone who'd recently been told to stand up straighter, and he said "awesome" when Doug ordered, which was not a word Doug associated with a hamburger.

"How's your week been," Simone said, and it came out flat, more statement than question, the way things came out when two people had spent enough time together that the formal structures of conversation had worn smooth.

"Fine," Doug said. "Processed a hail claim. A woman in Reynoldsburg — her roof's twenty-three years old and she wants full replacement cost. The policy caps it at actual cash value for roofs over twenty."

"How does that work?"

Doug explained. He explained the depreciation schedule, the inspection requirements, the specific way you calculated the remaining useful life of a twenty-three-year-old asphalt shingle roof. Simone listened with the expression she wore when she was listening professionally, except this wasn't professional, which made Doug aware that he was talking about roof depreciation on what was either a dinner between colleagues or a dinner between two people who no longer had a reason to be colleagues.

He stopped. "Sorry."

"Don't apologize. That's the first interesting thing anyone's said to me today."

"You need to meet more people."

"I've met plenty of people. Most of them are funnier than you on purpose and less interesting."

Their food arrived. The burger was fine. Simone's salad had the fennel on it, which looked like translucent strips of something that didn't want to be eaten. She ate it with the steady, unbothered pace of a person who ate salads at bars and didn't consider this remarkable.

For a while they didn't talk. The bar was filling in around them — a Tuesday crowd, which in the Short North meant young people in clothes that appeared to have been chosen with intention, and a few older couples who had the comfortable silence of people who'd been married long enough that dinner was a proximity arrangement, not an event. Doug could hear the music now, something with a guitar and a woman's voice that was probably famous and that he would never be able to identify.

"I keep thinking about Detroit," Doug said.

"The heckler?"

"Not the heckler. The eight minutes after. When I was just talking about the drive."

Simone tilted her head. "What about it?"

"I wasn't doing anything. I wasn't performing. I was just describing what the drive was like. The coffee, the rain, the Holiday Inn. And the room got quiet."

"I know."

"But I can't do that for sixty minutes. I can do it for eight minutes when I've run out of everything else and a man's been shouting at me and I've got nothing left. That's not a plan."

"No," Simone said. "It's not."

She swirled the bourbon in her glass and looked at it like there was something she could read in the way the liquid settled. Doug watched her do this. He'd been watching Simone do things — small things, the way she held a pencil, the way she listened with her whole body tilted slightly forward, the way she pushed her hair behind her ear with her ring finger specifically — for three months, and the watching had shifted at some point from professional attention to something else. He didn't know when the change had happened. He suspected it was gradual, like depreciation — you didn't notice the value shifting until one day the numbers didn't match what you expected.

"Can I ask you something?" Doug said.

"Sure."

"Why'd you stop performing?"

Simone set her glass down. She didn't answer right away, which was unusual — Simone answered things quickly, the way someone who'd spent two decades in writers' rooms had learned to, where silence meant you agreed with whatever had just been said and your joke was going in the trash.

"I didn't know you knew I performed," she said.

"Layla mentioned it. When she was telling me about you. She said you'd done the circuit."

"Layla said that?"

"Those were her words."

Simone picked up her bourbon again but didn't drink it. She held it near her chin, and Doug could smell the whiskey from across the table, sweet and sharp, and he thought about how his own relationship with alcohol had become a negotiation since the toast — one beer, always one, always counted, always the awareness of what happened the last time he'd stopped counting.

"I did stand-up for eleven years," Simone said. "Improv for three of those. Some sketch. Chicago first, then New York."

"Were you good?"

"Technically, yeah. I could write a set. I could work a room. I understood structure — where to put the turn, how to build a callback, how to use silence. All the craft stuff."

"But."

"I was miserable." She pushed a piece of fennel to the edge of her plate with her fork. "And I don't mean nervous — nervous is fine, nervous goes away after the first minute if you know what you're doing. I mean miserable. Like the whole time I was up there, I could feel myself standing next to myself, watching. This person at the microphone doing an impression of a comedian, hitting the marks, getting the laughs, and the real me was three feet to the left thinking, this isn't you, none of this is you."

Doug said nothing. He was still on his stool, the way he went still when a claimant started telling him the real story instead of the one they'd prepared.

"The laughs were the worst part," Simone said. "Because they were real. The audience was laughing at material that worked, and the material worked because I knew how to build it, and I still felt like I was stealing something. Like the laughs belonged to whoever I was pretending to be up there, and I'd go home and it would be quiet and I'd be just me again and the funny was gone." She said this looking at her glass, not at Doug. There was a small crease between her eyebrows that he'd seen before — she got it when she was concentrating on a note she was writing — but he'd never seen it aimed at her own speech. She was concentrating on being honest, and the effort was visible. "So I started writing for other people. And it was a relief. I could hear what was funny about someone else and put it in their mouth, and when it worked it was theirs. Not mine. I didn't have to stand there and own it."

"How long have you been writing?"

"Twenty years, give or take. Three late-night shows. A pilot that never aired. A bunch of corporate stuff I'd rather not get into. Two podcasts." She paused. "Your material."

"My material doesn't work."

"It doesn't, no."

They sat with that for a moment. Outside, the light was finally softening, the sun dropping behind the buildings on the west side of High Street. The bar had gotten louder — someone had turned the music up or the people had gotten drunker or both. The bartender's silver ring tapped against a glass as she poured.

"I'm funnier on paper," Simone said. "You're funnier in person." She paused. "I don't know which is worse."

Doug thought about this. He thought about Simone in her twenties, in a comedy club in Chicago, delivering material that worked while some essential part of her stood three feet to the left, and he thought about himself on stage in Pittsburgh with his mind empty and eleven seconds of silence and the pool story that worked for ninety seconds before he lost the room, and the two images sat next to each other in his head like claims from the same weather event — different addresses, different damage, same storm.

"Probably the same amount of worse," he said.

Simone laughed. It was short, more breath than voice, and she covered her mouth with her hand and then took her hand away. "See, that's what I mean. You don't even know you did that."

"Did what?"

"Never mind."

---

The burger was gone and Simone was on her second bourbon and Doug was still on his one beer, now warm, which he drank anyway because leaving a beer unfinished was wasteful in a way he couldn't articulate but felt strongly about.

Simone was talking about a comedian she'd written for years ago — a woman whose timing was so precise that Simone could write a joke and know, within a beat, where the laugh would come. "She'd hit the word and the room would respond like she'd pressed a button. Every time. It was mechanical. It was perfect." She tore a piece of bread from the basket that had come with Doug's burger and that neither of them had touched until now. "And it was dead."

"Dead how?"

"Dead like — the laughs came in the right places but they didn't mean anything. Nobody left her shows different than they came in. They laughed and then they went home and it evaporated. Because she was asking for them. Every single joke was a request. Laugh here. Now here. Now here. And the audience complied because the craft was flawless, but compliance isn't the same thing as —" She stopped. She set the bread down. She picked up her glass and put it down without drinking.

"As what?" Doug said.

"Recognition." She was quiet for a second. "I think that's what it is. The laughs that actually matter — the ones that change the room — those aren't requested. They're involuntary. Something true gets said that nobody's supposed to say, and the laugh is the body's way of catching up to it. Like the body knows before the brain does. The social contract says don't say that, and somebody says it, and the laugh is the sound of everyone in the room recognizing it at the same time."

Doug felt something shift, the way the numbers in a claim sometimes shifted when you put the documentation in a different order and the whole sequence suddenly made sense. It wasn't new information. It was a new arrangement of information he'd already had.

"That's what the toast was," he said.

"Yeah."

"I was drunk enough that I didn't — the filter was gone. I wasn't asking anyone to laugh. I wasn't asking for anything. I was just saying things."

"Things you meant."

"Things I'd been not saying for twenty years. And everyone laughed because — because it was true, and you're not supposed to say those things at a wedding, and I said them anyway." He was talking slowly because he could feel the shape of the idea and didn't want to lose it. "And the reason the material doesn't work is that material is asking. The whole structure of a set is: here's a joke, please laugh. Even if the jokes are good. Even if they're honest. The structure itself is a request. And the toast never requested anything."

Simone was looking at him with the expression she'd worn the first day at his kitchen table when he'd told her the pool story — alert, still, the look she got when something was working. "That's it," she said.

"So I need to not ask."

"You need to not need."

Doug turned this over. It sounded right. It also sounded impossible, like telling someone to fall asleep — the trying was the obstacle. He thought about the eight minutes in Detroit, how he'd stumbled into it by running out of everything else, how the room had leaned in at the exact moment he had nothing left to offer them.

"That's the same thing my mother would say," Doug said. "Except she'd say it meaner and then ask if I was eating enough."

Simone laughed, the real kind, the kind that came from her chest and not her craft, and Doug watched it happen the way he watched weather data — not controlling it, not causing it, just observing the phenomenon and noting that it occurred.

"Your mother," Simone said, and shook her head.

"She told me the toast was the only real thing I'd said since I was twelve. She was eating a dinner roll when she said it."

"That's a good detail. The dinner roll."

"It's not a detail. She was eating a dinner roll."

"I know. That's why it's good."

They sat there. The bar was loud now, the Tuesday night crowd settled in, and Doug could hear someone at a table behind them talking about a television show with the passionate specificity that people reserved for things that didn't matter, and he could hear the bartender's ring against glass again, and he could feel the high-top's edge against his forearm and the stool's absence of a back and the warm air that came in every time someone opened the door to the sidewalk.

"The taping's in twelve days," he said.

"I know."

"I don't know what to do."

Simone didn't say anything for a while. She looked at the bread she'd torn and not eaten. Then she said, "I don't either. And I've been doing this for twenty years." She finished her bourbon. "At least you know what the problem is now. That's more than you knew in Cleveland."

"In Cleveland I was wearing a costume."

"You were. You were doing Mulaney-meets-insurance-adjuster. It was terrible."

"Thanks."

"You're welcome."

Doug signaled for the check. It came to forty-seven dollars. He put his card down. Simone reached for her wallet and he said, "I've got it," and she said, "You sure?" and he said, "You came all the way from New York to teach me to be funny and I'm not funny. Least I can do is buy you dinner."

"You're doing it again," she said.

"Doing what?"

"Being funny without knowing it."

"I'm telling you the truth."

"Exactly."

They stepped outside. The air was warm — the kind of warm where you realized your body had been bracing against cold for months and had finally stopped. High Street was lit up, storefronts and streetlights and the headlights of cars moving slowly through the foot traffic, people going to bars and restaurants and wherever people in the Short North went on a Tuesday in late May. Two women passed them going the other direction, one of them laughing hard at something the other had said, and the sound of it — unforced, involuntary — hung in the air for a moment like a demonstration of the thing they'd just been talking about.

They walked south. Simone had parked two blocks down, on a side street off High. Doug had parked on a meter around the corner from that. The walk was short and they didn't talk for most of it, and the silence between them had weight — not uncomfortable, not empty, but present. A thing they were both inside and aware of.

Doug could smell something blooming. He didn't know what it was. He wasn't a plant person. But it was there, sweet and out of nowhere, the way things showed up in late spring in Ohio when the temperature finally committed.

At Simone's car — a gray Subaru with New York plates and a coffee cup visible on the passenger seat — they stopped. Simone got her keys out. She held them in her hand without unlocking the door.

"Thank you for dinner," she said.

"Yeah."

"And for the roof depreciation lecture. That was genuinely interesting."

"It's a twenty-three-year-old roof. There's a conversation to be had."

Simone stood there. Doug stood there. The distance between them was about the width of the car's side mirror. Doug could hear the bar noise from the block behind them, and somewhere a car door closing, and his own breathing, which he noticed because he didn't usually notice it. He was aware of Simone's hand on her keys and the way she was looking at him — not the professional look, not the diagnostic look she used in sessions, but something he didn't have a category for, something that fell between the established coverage areas the way the pool and roses claim had fallen between the pool owner's liability and the neighbor's weather provision. A gap. Not a failure of the system. Just a place the policies hadn't anticipated.

It was there. Whatever it was, it was there, in the two feet of sidewalk between the side mirror and where Doug stood, and they both knew it the way you know a storm is coming before the rain starts — the pressure in the air, the shift. The everything that comes before the thing itself.

Neither of them moved, not toward each other and not away. They stood in it for a moment, and the moment was long enough to be real and short enough to not require a decision, and then Simone said, "Goodnight, Doug," and there was nothing extra in how she said it except that she said his name, which she didn't always do, and the name carried it.

"Goodnight," Doug said.

She unlocked the car. The lights flashed once, orange in the dim of the side street. She got in. She moved the coffee cup from the passenger seat to somewhere he couldn't see. She started the engine, and Doug stepped back onto the curb, and she pulled out and turned left on High and the Subaru's taillights joined the others heading north and then were gone.

Doug walked the half block to his Civic. He sat in the driver's seat and didn't start the car. The meter had expired — he'd fed it two hours and dinner had been closer to two and a half — but when he checked, there was no ticket. The windshield was clean. Small mercies, or Columbus parking enforcement, which was inconsistent on weeknights.

He sat with his hands on the steering wheel and thought about the thing Simone had said. You need to not need. He thought about the taping in twelve days and the space where a plan should be and wasn't. He thought about the toast — the original toast, the seven minutes that started all of this — and how the conditions that produced it were as unreplicable as a weather event. You couldn't schedule a thunderstorm. You couldn't prepare for the specific collision of whiskey and silence and twenty years of things unsaid and Brendan's perfect speech that preceded his wreck of one. You could only be outside when it happened and hope you survived what came through.

Be unintentional on purpose. Try to not try. The problem was clear and the solution was its own impossibility and the taping was in twelve days and Doug Bridge, insurance adjuster, fifty-two years old, sitting in a Civic with 114,000 miles on it on a side street in the Short North, did not have a plan.

He started the car. The engine turned over with the reliable, unbeautiful sound it always made. He drove home to Clintonville, eleven minutes, the route he could do without thinking. He parked in his usual spot. He went inside. He stood in his kitchen.

The kitchen table where he and Simone had worked for three months — her notebook, his voice, the recorder she set between them like a referee — was bare. He'd wiped it down after their last session. It was just a table now. A flat surface in a quiet apartment in a building where nobody knew about the taping or the tour or the eight minutes in Detroit or the two feet of sidewalk outside a gray Subaru that neither of them had crossed.

He opened a beer and drank it standing at the counter. He thought about Simone holding her keys and the way she'd said his name and the thing they'd left where it was. Then he rinsed the glass, put it on the rack, and went to bed. The taping was in twelve days. He knew what the problem was now, which was either progress or a more precise way of being stuck.


# Chapter 16

The Lincoln Theatre was on East Long Street, in a part of Columbus that Doug had driven past for twenty-six years without ever registering as a place where people went on purpose. He'd processed a claim once for a storefront two blocks east — water intrusion, failed flashing, four inches of standing water in a basement that had been a jazz bar in the 1940s — but the theater itself had never entered his consciousness until Layla sent him a one-page fact sheet listing its seating capacity (582, though the Netflix contract said 580, a discrepancy Doug had noticed and not raised), its Art Deco heritage, and three bullet points under the heading "Why This Venue Works For Your Brand."

He had never been inside it. He was inside it now.

The lobby smelled like old carpet and fresh paint, which Doug recognized as the smell of renovation that hadn't quite finished. Someone had taped black cables to the floor in a pattern that ran from the front doors to the theater entrance, and the tape was the wide flat kind — gaffer tape, a crew member had called it when Doug nearly tripped on it — that left adhesive residue on everything it touched. Doug had noticed this the way he noticed everything, which was carefully and for no useful purpose.

The stage was bigger than he'd imagined. He'd pictured something the size of the comedy clubs — Cleveland, Pittsburgh, Detroit — where the stages were platforms, barely elevated, close enough to the front row that you could see the drinks sweating. This was different. The stage at the Lincoln Theatre was wide and deep, with a proscenium arch above it and wings on either side where things disappeared into darkness, and when Doug walked out onto it for the first time, at 10:15 a.m. on a Wednesday in early June, his footsteps echoed in a way that sounded like someone else walking behind him.

The seats were empty. Five hundred and eighty-two of them, rows curving gently toward the walls, dark red upholstery that looked rich under the production lights and institutional under the work lights. The work lights were on. A balcony ran along the upper level, more seats up there, and Doug could see Simone already sitting in the front row of the balcony, legs crossed, notebook on her knee, watching the stage the way she watched everything — like a doctor reading a chart she'd already diagnosed.

The crew was setting up. Nineteen people, Layla had told him, as if the number were a good sign. Doug counted them himself because he counted things and because counting gave him something to do while people moved around him carrying equipment he didn't understand. Four cameras on wheeled platforms. Lights on metal rigs that hung from the ceiling and more lights on stands along the sides of the stage, angled inward, aimed at the stool. A sound board at the back of the orchestra section, operated by a woman with headphones around her neck and a travel mug that said KAUAI in faded pink letters — she'd been there since seven, someone had mentioned, which meant she'd been awake in this theater longer than Doug had been awake at all. She had a rubber band around her left wrist that she kept snapping, a habit that had no obvious purpose except to mark time between tasks. Cables ran everywhere, taped to the stage floor in lines that created a geometry Doug was supposed to ignore but kept stepping around. A stool sat at center stage. Next to the stool, a small table with a glass of water and a monitor angled down so Doug could see something on it, though he didn't know what he was supposed to see on a monitor that was pointed at him while he was the one performing. Nobody had explained the monitor. Doug hadn't asked.

Charlie Falk was in the third row, center section, wearing sneakers that were brighter than any of the stage lights. He had a notebook and his phone and a disposable coffee cup from somewhere Doug couldn't identify — the logo was a palm tree, which suggested Charlie had brought it from Los Angeles, which was impossible, which meant Columbus had a coffee place with a palm tree logo that Doug had never noticed in twenty-six years of living here. Charlie was talking to a woman in her thirties who had a headset and a clipboard and the posture of someone whose job was to make things happen for other people. She was nodding at whatever Charlie said with the rhythm of agreement, not comprehension, and she had a pen behind her ear that she kept touching as if confirming it was still there.

"Okay!" Charlie called up to the stage. "Whenever you're ready, Doug. Just run it through. We're not rolling, this is just a feel thing. Getting the energy of the room."

The room's energy, as far as Doug could assess, was the energy of a room that was waiting for something to happen and was professionally prepared to wait as long as necessary. The crew moved efficiently and quietly. Someone adjusted a light. Someone else checked a cable connection at the base of a camera. The sound board woman sipped from her Kauai mug. The room was cool — the air conditioning had been running since before Doug arrived, and the vents were audible, a low hum that Doug tried not to think of as the sound the theater was making while it waited for him.

He looked at the stool. He looked at the glass of water. He looked at the monitor, which, it turned out, was a countdown timer set to sixty minutes and not yet started. He looked at the five hundred and eighty-two empty seats and the nineteen crew members and the four cameras and Charlie Falk's palm-tree coffee and Layla, who was in the back row of the orchestra section, sitting in the aisle seat, doing something with her phone that could have been texting or scrolling or praying, her thumb moving in a way that suggested none of the above and all of the above simultaneously.

He picked up the microphone from the stool. It was heavier than the ones in the clubs. Or it was the same weight and his hand was different. He couldn't tell.

"Good morning," he said. The microphone turned his voice into something that filled the room and bounced off the back wall and came back to him a half-second later, so that he heard himself greeting an empty theater twice, which felt about right for how the last six months had gone.

He started the material.

The first three minutes were fine. Fine in the way that the first three minutes of the corporate keynote in Indianapolis had been fine, and the first two minutes in Cleveland had been fine, and the first minute in Pittsburgh before he froze had been fine — fine in the way that falling is fine for the first several floors, before the ground arrives. Doug delivered the opening section about insurance and risk and the way people assess their lives without knowing they're doing it, and the words came out in the right order and at roughly the right pace and they had the right pauses and the right emphasis and they were dead. Not bad. Not wrong. Dead. The words left his mouth and entered the theater and fell to the floor somewhere around the seventh row and lay there.

He kept going. The cereal bit. The bit about Roy's sandwich order that never changes. The water damage section that Simone had called "the core of the set" and that had worked, in flashes, in Detroit, for about ninety seconds before he'd lost it. He delivered all of it and it sat in the theater like furniture in a model home — arranged correctly, signifying a life no one lived in.

Charlie was taking notes on his phone. His thumb moved rapidly and his face had the expression of someone who was processing what he saw through a system of categories and equivalents, translating the dead air into development language that would make it seem like a creative choice. The clipboard woman had stopped nodding. A camera operator near the left wing was adjusting a lens that did not need adjusting, his hands finding something to do while the stage produced nothing worth recording.

Doug got to the twelve-minute mark and stopped. Not because he'd run out of material but because the silence between his sentences had become the dominant sound in the room, louder than the air conditioning, louder than the crew's patient not-watching, louder than his own amplified voice saying things that had no weight.

"Can I start over?" he said.

"Absolutely," Charlie said. "Reset, take a breath, do your thing."

Doug took a breath. He did his thing. His thing, it turned out, was the same thing — the same words in the same order with the same cadence producing the same nothing. The material that Simone had spent weeks building from the insurance stories and the cereal monologue and the Roy observations and all the pieces of Doug's life that she'd identified as the source of his voice — none of it was wrong, exactly. None of it was unfunny in the way that bad jokes are unfunny. It was unfunny in the way that a photograph of a fire is not hot. Everything was there except the thing that made it alive.

He made it twenty-one minutes the second time. Longer than the first attempt, which was worse, because the extra nine minutes meant nine more minutes of words landing on empty air and the crew's patience becoming something Doug could feel against his skin, like humidity. The water damage section came out as a monologue about rot and dehumidifiers and Home Depot and people standing on their ruined floors, and Doug could hear, as he said it, the exact version of these words that had worked in his kitchen when he wasn't trying to say them — and this version, the performed version, the version with the mic and the lights and the five hundred and eighty-two seats, was a photocopy of a photocopy, and every generation lost something he couldn't identify or replace.

He stopped. He set the microphone on the stool.

From the balcony, Simone shook her head. Not "no." Not "stop." Just the head-shake of someone watching the thing she'd known would happen happening, the way Doug watched a homeowner try to save floors that were already gone. She didn't say anything. She didn't need to. Doug had been watching Simone watch him for weeks and he knew her silences the way he knew water damage timelines — by what stage they were at. This one was day four.

---

Layla took Doug to dinner at a restaurant three blocks from the theater. It was Italian, or the menu was in Italian and the bread came with olive oil, and the tables had white paper over the tablecloths that you could draw on with crayons from a cup, which suggested the restaurant had not decided what it was. Layla ordered a glass of red wine and then a second glass before the entrees arrived, which Doug had not seen her do before. She also ordered a bruschetta she didn't eat — it sat between them on a small plate, accumulating olive oil around its edges, and Doug tracked its gradual saturation with more attention than the conversation warranted because the conversation was the thing he did not want to be tracking.

Charlie was there too. He'd changed his sneakers since the rehearsal. Doug noticed this because the sneakers were now white instead of orange, which meant Charlie had brought two pairs of sneakers to Columbus for a two-day trip, which meant Charlie had a system for footwear that Doug could not access and did not want to.

"Okay," Layla said. "So here's what I'm thinking." She was leaning forward, elbows on the white paper, and she'd drawn a small circle with one of the crayons without seeming to realize it. Red crayon. The circle was tight and deliberate and suggested a mind that was organizing itself around an anxiety it had not yet named out loud.

"What if we restructured the format," she said. "Not the content — the content is strong, the content's really strong — but what if the delivery was more conversational? Like, what if Charlie moderated? What if it was more of a sit-down, interview-style — "

"A conversation," Charlie said. He was already nodding, already building. "Yes. Like, Doug, you sit, I sit, we just — it's like a hangout. Like a late-night vibe but without the bits. Just two guys talking and the audience is in on it."

"I'm not a talk show host," Doug said.

"No, and that's the beauty of it. That's the whole — you're not hosting, you're just being there. You're being Doug. I'm just the — I'm the conduit. I'm the thing that lets the Doug happen."

Doug picked up a piece of bread and looked at it. The bread was good. It was the best part of the evening so far, and the evening was twenty minutes old.

"Or," Layla said, pivoting without taking a breath. "Okay, other idea. What if we showed the original toast? Like, on a screen behind you, and you watch it and react."

"React to my own video."

"Yes! And you can comment on it, you can fill in the blanks, you can tell the audience what you were actually thinking at each — because that's what people want to know. They've seen the toast. They want the inside."

Doug chewed the bread. He swallowed. He set the remaining piece on his plate.

"That's not comedy. That's a DVD commentary."

The table went quiet. Not the silence of a room processing a good point — the silence of a table where the only two ideas anyone had just lost their air supply. Layla's crayon circle had been joined by a second circle, tighter than the first, drawn inside it. Charlie was looking at his phone but not at his phone — at the space above it, the way people look at screens when they're not reading, just occupying their eyes while their brain works on something else.

"What about a Q and A," Charlie said after a while. "From the audience. Totally unscripted, totally — the audience drives it, Doug responds, and we just capture what happens."

"A Q and A about what."

"About everything. About you, about the toast, about your life, about — "

"That's what the profile was. That's what the whole internet is. People asking me questions and me not having the answers they want."

"But that's the bit," Charlie said. "That IS the bit. You not having the answers — that's what people — "

"That's not a bit. That's just what happens when you ask me questions."

Layla reached for her wine glass, stopped, and drew a third circle inside the second one. The crayon made a faint sound against the paper, a dry scratching that was the loudest thing at the table. She put the crayon down. She picked it up again. She put it down.

Then Layla did something Doug had never witnessed: she didn't talk. She sat there, both hands flat on the paper tablecloth, not pivoting, not pitching, not building on the previous idea or generating the next one. Just sitting. The silence from Layla was alarming in the way that a car making no noise is alarming — Layla was an engine, and engines were supposed to run, and when they stopped you checked for catastrophic failure.

"What if we just," Charlie said, and then he also stopped. He took a long breath, the kind of breath that usually preceded a reframe — a layer of new vocabulary applied to the same problem — but this time the breath just sat there. Charlie exhaled and looked at the bruschetta and seemed, for a moment, like a man who had reached the edge of his operating system and found nothing beyond it. Not cynicism. Not failure. Just the specific confusion of a person who has been inside the machine so long that the machine's logic feels like oxygen, and when it doesn't work he doesn't know what to breathe.

Doug looked at them both. Layla with her circles and her untouched bruschetta and her two glasses of wine. Charlie with his two pairs of sneakers and his phone full of notes that would not solve anything. They had been the people with answers — or not answers, exactly, but momentum, direction, the professional confidence that something would work if they just kept generating options fast enough. And now the options were used up and the speed had stopped and they were sitting in a restaurant that couldn't decide if it was for families or dates, eating bread and not talking, which was the thing Doug had been doing for twelve years and he could have told them it didn't help.

"Can I get the check?" Doug said to the waiter. Nobody argued.

---

He went back to the theater at a quarter to eleven. The front doors were locked but a side entrance was propped open with a rubber wedge — a man in a black T-shirt carrying a coil of cable over his shoulder nodded at Doug as he passed without asking what he was doing there. People had been nodding at Doug without questioning his presence for six months. It was one of the consistent features of being briefly famous — the assumption that you belonged wherever you showed up, even when you had no idea why you were there yourself.

The theater was almost dark. The production lights were off, the work lights were off, but a single bare bulb on a tall stand glowed at center stage — a ghost light, one of the crew had called it that morning during setup, explaining to a colleague that you always leave one burning. Tradition. Also safety, the orchestra pit being three feet down and uncovered in places. Also, the same crew member had added without smiling, ghosts. Doug hadn't been able to tell if he meant it. He'd had a tattoo of a compass on his forearm and the habit of testing cable connections by tugging them twice, always twice, with the precision of someone who had learned the cost of a loose connection.

The ghost light threw a circle of yellow on the stage floor, maybe eight feet across, and beyond it the theater was darkness and shape — the curve of the balcony, the rows of seats visible only as repeating shadows, the exit signs glowing red at the ends of the aisles like the indicator lights on smoke detectors. The HVAC was still running, quieter now than it had been during the day, a vibration Doug felt through the stage floor more than he heard through the air.

He sat on the edge of the stage, legs hanging over the front, feet dangling above the orchestra pit. The pit was shallow — three feet, maybe, with a plywood cover over most of it and a gap near the left wing where cables had been run through. Doug looked at the gap and estimated a fall onto plywood from stage height at roughly the same as stepping off a kitchen counter, which was not dangerous but was not nothing. He chose not to think about what it meant that his first action in the dark empty theater was to assess the fall risk. He was an adjuster. He assessed things. It was the only response to the world he'd ever had that worked reliably.

The seats stretched out in front of him, five hundred and eighty-two of them, visible only as shapes in the red exit-sign glow. In two days they would hold people. Kelsey would be in the fifth row. Tyler would be next to her, saying something agreeable about the venue, because Tyler said agreeable things about everything and meant them all equally and none of them specifically. Megan and Brendan would be in the back section, which Doug knew because Layla had mentioned the seat assignments and Doug had absorbed them the way he absorbed claim file details — completely, involuntarily, and with the understanding that knowing a fact and knowing what to do with it were entirely different competencies.

Gloria would be there. Fourth row center. She'd called two days ago to confirm she was coming and to tell him that Ruth had wanted to come too and Gloria had said no because "Ruth claps at the wrong times and I won't have it." Doug had not argued. Gloria's management of Ruth was not his jurisdiction.

Roy would not be there. This was not a wound. It was Roy.

Doug sat in the quiet and thought about his mother at the Maple Glen dining room, eating what the retirement community called lasagna, telling him that the thing about the table was the only real thing he'd said since he was twelve. She'd said it the way she said everything — as a fact about the world that happened to involve her son — and Doug had taken it in and moved on because Gloria's assessments did not come with instructions for what to do after you'd heard them. But the sentence had stayed. It had attached itself to him the way the water ring had attached itself to his kitchen table — present, visible, part of the surface now.

He thought about Kelsey at the Thai place. Not the conversation itself but the moment before it, when she was looking at the menu she didn't need to read because she'd been ordering the same thing for fourteen years, and her face had the particular expression of someone who was about to say something that would change the dimensions of the room. She hadn't said it yet. She was still deciding. And Doug had watched her decide, the way he watched homeowners stand on their ruined floors before the assessment — knowing the number was coming, knowing what the number would mean, knowing that his job was to not say it yet because they were standing on the thing that was gone and they needed another minute with it.

She'd said it. The spreadsheet. The Excel file. The ledger of small grievances Doug had built during the divorce, tracking Megan's late pickups and forgotten permission slips with the meticulous specificity of a man who did not know how to grieve except through documentation. And Doug had sat across from his daughter with his curry cooling and thought about all the rows and columns and timestamps, all the evidence he'd accumulated against his ex-wife while his sixteen-year-old daughter found the file and understood, for the first time, that her father saw her life as a dataset. He'd told Kelsey he kept score because he didn't know how to miss her any other way, which was either the truest thing he'd ever said to his daughter or the most pathetic, and Doug's experience was that the truest and most pathetic things were usually the same thing, and neither one made the spreadsheet okay.

He sat on the stage. The ghost light hummed above him. The theater breathed.

Tomorrow was a day off. Layla had scheduled it as a "mental health day," which Doug understood to mean a day where nobody had to watch him not work and everybody could pretend the problem might fix itself by Thursday night. The taping was Friday. He had no material that worked, no format that made sense, and no idea what he was going to do in front of five hundred and eighty people who had paid for tickets or been given them — he didn't know which, and the not-knowing felt like one more small thing in a pile of small things he should probably understand but didn't.

He pulled out his phone. The screen lit up his face and the first four rows of seats in a blue-white wash. It was 11:22 p.m.

He called Roy.

Roy answered on the second ring, which meant Roy was still awake, which was unusual because Roy went to bed at 9:45. Doug knew this because Roy had mentioned it once, years ago, not as a confession or a philosophy but as a fact about the time at which he went to bed, and the fact had settled into Doug's understanding of Roy the way all facts about Roy settled — simply, completely, and without requiring further discussion.

"It's late," Roy said. Not a complaint. A report.

"I know. Sorry."

"I was reading." A pause. "The library had a book about bridges. Civil engineering. It's not as interesting as it sounds."

"I'm at the theater," Doug said.

"The one for the show."

"Yeah."

"At eleven o'clock at night."

"I'm sitting on the stage."

Roy didn't say anything for a moment. Doug could hear, through the phone, what he was fairly certain was the sound of Roy turning a page. Roy was still reading while talking on the phone, because Roy did not subordinate one activity to another when both could be conducted at the same time. This was not rude. It was efficient, which was the same thing as polite in Roy's system.

"I don't think I can do this," Doug said.

There was a silence. Not a long one — Roy's silences were measured in seconds, not in the theatrical pauses that other people used to signal they were composing something important. Roy's silences meant he was constructing a sentence, and the sentence, when it arrived, would contain exactly the information he intended and nothing more.

"Then don't," Roy said.

Doug sat with this. The ghost light flickered once — a power fluctuation, or a loose connection, or ghosts.

"I signed a contract," Doug said.

"You work in insurance," Roy said. "You know what happens when you breach a contract."

"What?"

"Paperwork."

Doug laughed. It came out of him the way the toast had come out of him at Kelsey's wedding — without permission, without preparation, without the part of his brain that had been managing the situation for twelve hours getting a vote. It was not a big laugh. It was not a performance of laughter or the kind of laugh that means the joke was great. It was the sound a person makes when something true arrives without warning and the body recognizes it before the mind can intervene.

He laughed and the theater caught the sound and sent it back to him from the balcony and the back wall and the five hundred and eighty-two empty seats, and for a moment the Lincoln Theatre was full of Doug Bridge laughing at a joke about paperwork, which was the first real thing that had happened on this stage all day.

"Yeah," Doug said. "Paperwork."

"Go home," Roy said. "It's Wednesday."

"I know what day it is."

"Go home. Sleep. Eat something. Not cereal."

"I'll probably eat cereal."

"Then eat cereal." A pause. The sound of a page. "Friday's going to come whether you're ready or not. That's how days work."

"That's very helpful, Roy."

"It wasn't meant to be helpful. It was meant to be accurate."

Doug sat on the stage for another ten minutes after he hung up. The theater was quiet except for the HVAC and the ghost light's low electrical buzz and, faintly, from the alley, the sound of someone closing a truck door. He looked at the empty seats. He looked at the ghost light. He looked at the monitor by the stool that nobody had turned off, its countdown still at sixty minutes, still waiting for someone to start the clock.

He didn't know what he was going to do on Friday. He didn't have material that worked. He didn't have a format. He didn't have the specific unrepeatable conditions that had made the toast what it was — the eight whiskeys, Brendan's speech, twenty years of things he'd never said. He had himself, sitting on a stage in the dark in a theater he'd entered for the first time that morning, and the first genuine laugh he'd had in days was about a breach-of-contract penalty, and the person who'd given it to him was three miles away in Clintonville reading a library book about bridges and going back to sleep because it was Wednesday and sleep was what Wednesday was for.

Doug stood up. His knees cracked — a sound the theater faithfully amplified and returned to him, because that was what this building did: it took whatever you gave it and made it bigger, whether you wanted it to or not.

He walked off the stage into the wing, past the ghost light, through the side door and into the alley. The air outside was warm — early June, the season when Columbus stopped apologizing for its weather and committed, briefly, to being a place where you could stand outside at midnight without a coat. Doug's car was parked on East Long Street, under a streetlight that buzzed at a frequency similar to the ghost light's, as if the city and the theater were running on the same current.

He got in the car and sat for a moment with his hands on the wheel and the engine off. He didn't know what Friday would look like. He knew what the theater sounded like empty. He knew what the material sounded like dead. He knew what Roy sounded like saying the truest, simplest, most adequate thing anyone had said to him in months.

He started the Civic and drove home.


# Chapter 17

The green room at the Lincoln Theatre was not green. It was the color of a claim form — the off-white of documents designed to be functional rather than noticed. A folding table, two chairs, a mirror ringed with bulbs that gave off a dry heat Doug could feel from across the room. Someone had placed a bottle of water on the table next to two pens and a note card that said GOOD LUCK DOUG in handwriting he didn't recognize. The script was there too — seventeen stapled pages, the corner curling where he'd folded it back during rehearsal, the first line visible through the top sheet. He'd read that line so many times it had stopped being language and become a shape, the way a policy number stops being a number when you've typed it enough and becomes just a motion your fingers make.

A production assistant came in — early twenties, headset, clipboard. She had a small silver ring on her thumb that she kept rotating with her index finger, a gesture so constant it seemed involuntary, something she did the way other people blinked.

"Fifteen minutes, Doug."

"Okay."

She hesitated. "Can I get you anything? Water, tea —"

"I'm good. Thank you."

She left. The door clicked shut and the room settled around him — the hum of the bulbs, the faint vibration of the building's old heating system running even in June because buildings built in 1928 did what they wanted.

Through the wall, Doug could hear the audience. Not words — a collective hum, the sound of 580 people settling into seats and producing the noise that humans make when they're together and waiting. He'd heard this sound four times before: Indianapolis, Cleveland, Pittsburgh, Detroit. Each time it had meant the same thing — that the event was real, that people had driven here and parked and found their seats and were now sitting in them expecting something from a man who adjusted insurance claims in Columbus, Ohio. They were expecting the toast guy. Doug was not sure which man would walk out there.

He stood and looked in the mirror. He was wearing a blue button-down — not the suit, the suit was retired, the suit had done enough — and khakis that were clean and pressed in a way that felt like preparation for a job interview rather than a performance. His hair was the same. His face was the same face he'd seen in the bathroom mirror six months ago, the morning after the wedding, except the bags under his eyes were less dramatic and the hangover had been replaced by something more vertical, a standing dread that had no peak and no floor. He looked like what he was: a fifty-two-year-old insurance adjuster from Clintonville who had been asked to be somewhere he didn't belong and had run out of ways to say no.

He picked up the script. The paper was warm from the bulbs. He put it back down.

Through a gap in the stage door he could see part of the house — the first rows, the aisle, the ornate ceiling rising above the balcony. The Lincoln Theatre had been built for vaudeville, and the plasterwork and gilded columns still had the confident excess of a building that assumed it would matter. Doug had never been inside it before Tuesday's rehearsal, and the scale of it — the carved proscenium, the painted ceiling, the fact that the ceiling was painted at all — still felt like someone else's venue. His venues were the Mutual Fidelity conference room and the break room and, recently, comedy clubs with low ceilings and tables so close to the stage you could smell the drinks. This was a theater. People performed here on purpose.

He looked through the gap and found faces.

Fourth row center: Gloria. She was sitting with her purse on her lap and her coat folded over the armrest, looking straight ahead at the empty stage with the expression she brought to everything — attentive, unsoftened, prepared to have an opinion. She'd driven from Dayton. An hour on I-70. Doug had gotten a call that afternoon from Ruth at Maple Glen, who'd called to wish him luck and to inform him that Gloria had described the event as going to watch her son do whatever it was he was doing. The seat beside Gloria was empty. She hadn't brought anyone. Gloria went places alone.

Fifth row, slightly right of center: Kelsey. She was leaning toward Tyler, saying something, and Tyler was nodding the way Tyler nodded at everything — automatically, warmly, without it necessarily indicating that the content had been processed. Kelsey was wearing a sweater Doug hadn't seen before, dark green, and her hair was down, and she looked the way she looked in every context where something was about to happen to her father — alert and slightly braced, the posture of someone who has learned to prepare for the unpredictable from a man who was constitutionally incapable of predicting himself.

Back section, house left: Megan and Brendan. Doug couldn't see them clearly from this angle, but Layla had handled the tickets, which meant she'd handled the seating, which meant Megan was far enough from the cameras that whatever Doug said about their marriage wouldn't be broadcast alongside her reaction. Layla thought about these things. Doug thought about the fact that his ex-wife was in the same building and that whatever he said in the next hour would be said in front of her, and in front of the man she'd left him for, and that the man she'd left him for had once told Doug that the original toast was brave, and that Doug had wanted to evaporate when he heard the word.

He looked for Roy out of habit, the same way he glanced at the passenger seat of his Civic on mornings when he'd driven to work alone for twelve years. Roy was at home. Roy had not been invited because Roy did not want to be invited, and Doug had not insisted because the conversation would have been brief and final. Roy's absence was not a commentary. Roy's absence was Roy.

Simone was in the balcony. She'd told him she'd be there, same spot as rehearsal. "I'll be up there," she'd said that afternoon, on the phone, her voice flat and professional. "I won't be doing anything. Just watching." He'd asked her what she'd be watching for and she'd said, "I'll know when I see it," which was either a kindness or a diagnosis, and with Simone the difference was narrow.

Layla was somewhere below, managing proximity to the event with the practiced instinct of someone who understood that being near things when they happened was half the job. Charlie Falk was in the third row — Doug had seen him at the tech check, wearing sneakers that cost more than the folding table in this room, carrying the same unshakable enthusiasm he'd brought to every meeting since LA.

The production assistant returned. "Five minutes."

Doug picked up the script again. Seventeen pages. Simone's structure, his words, callbacks and transitions and a section about insurance terminology that built to a line about coverage gaps, and a section about the toast that was supposed to be self-deprecating without being self-pitying, and a section about Roy that was supposed to be the warm part, and a section about Kelsey that was supposed to be the ending. It had all been organized. It had all been rehearsed. It had been dead for days and everybody knew it and nobody had said the thing that would have made saying it unnecessary.

He walked to the stage entrance and waited.

---

The applause when Doug walked out was warm and immediate, and it was exactly what he'd been afraid of.

Not because warmth was bad. Because warmth was expectation. 580 people who had watched a seven-minute video of a drunk man saying true things at a wedding and who had driven to a theater on a Tuesday evening in June and paid for parking and bought tickets that were, based on what Layla had told him, not cheap — these people wanted the man from the video. They wanted the voice that had broken when it talked about the kitchen table. They wanted the flat insurance-adjuster delivery that the internet had decided was a comic style. They wanted the toast guy, and the toast guy had been produced by eight whiskeys and twenty years of silence, and Doug did not have either of those things tonight. He had a script and stage lights and a microphone and the accumulated evidence of four previous performances that the thing they wanted was not a thing he could provide.

The lights were bright enough that the first four or five rows were visible and everything beyond them dissolved into shapes — the general fact of bodies, the occasional glint of a phone screen. He could see Gloria. She was sitting with her hands on her purse, watching him with the composure of someone who had spent seventy-eight years watching things and had not yet been surprised. He could see Kelsey, leaning forward in the fifth row, Tyler beside her with his pleasant posture.

Doug picked up the microphone from the stand. It had the weight of the ones at the clubs but different — a theater mic, heavier in the base, the kind that expected to be held by someone who knew what to do with it.

"Good evening," he said. The words came out in his work voice — the one he used on the phone with claimants, steady and a bit too formal for a room full of people who'd come to laugh. A few people laughed anyway. He didn't know if they were laughing at the formality or because they were ready to laugh and any sound from him would have done it. Both were problems.

He started the script.

The opening section was about the video — a self-deprecating preamble that Simone had built to acknowledge the toast, contain it, and pivot. "So I gave a toast at my daughter's wedding," he began, and paused for the recognition, which came, warm and easy. "Eighty million views. I checked this morning. Eighty million people have watched me do the worst thing I've ever done, and about twelve of them left comments calling me brave, which — I've never associated bravery with not remembering what I said. That's usually covered under a different part of the policy." It landed. Mild, warm, the audience easing into what they understood: a comedy special with the guy from the video. Doug moved to the second section — the aftermath, the phone calls, the internet turning his face into a meme — and the laughs continued, spaced and polite, the sound of a room that was with him without being surprised by him. It was fine. Fine was what it had been in rehearsal.
